CAMEOS OF SCIENCE.
There is a great tendency in these days towards the cultivation of the Arts and Sciences. Excellent works on
every subject are constantly being produced to this end and there is good hope that, if peace reigns supreme
as it has done for some time, men and women who think will be able to cultivate their minds by the read-
ing of good literature—and the study of Art. Relined taste is not, as many suppose, au accident of birth but
is the consequence of education and habit. When your education is complete, you will consider it infra diqnum
to smoke anything but “ '

THE RED PAGE.
THHLS is printed by an American magazine,
John-a-Dreams, as from ‘ the Spanish of
Oampoamor, ’ Who was Campoamor ?
Now that my end is near,
And I must render my account to God,
Bend o’er me, dear.
Dearest, while yet I live,
All xvho have hated me, or done me tU,
I do forgive,
Save thee alone. Ah, no /

Alex, Montgomery writes —
ITou correctly suppose that the sentences in the
4 mangrove - flat ’ and * head - house * passages shaped
themselves without any consciousness on my part that I
was falling into iambic measure. The psychological ex-
planation of a temporary lapse into this rhythm is
(doubtless with others, as with me) as follows. The
iambic pentameter of English blank-verse—notably that
of Milton—has soaked so thoroughly into roy sub-con-
sciousness that, when instinctively I feel the need of a
graver and more deliberate note, my difficulty is not to
get the iambic swing, but to avoid it. In the passage
which you quote it seems to me that I had a vague feeling
asof prelusive solemnity—opening chords—curtain-raising
—what you will in that way ; and though I agree with you
in deprecating the ‘affectation of prose-verse,’ neverthe-
less I think that a sparing employment of the iambic
pulsation is here and there admissible—if used simply as
a change of key, and not as an altogether legitimate
element of prose composition. To show' that one may be
much more iambic in effect than in intention, let me scan
as jt sounded to me a sentence whioh you quote—
“Acrdss the 16ng perspective of the mangrove-fl&ts the
sffi# glared fierce and crimson.”
You have here in one case a collocation of three short or
weak syllables, and, in another, of three long or strong.
So also with other passages whioh would not be markedly
iambic, as read by me.’
» » * *
Thus Montgomery’s prose iambics, if one cor-
rgQtly understands him, are due to the influence
of Milton and the blank-verse poets, which makes
itself felt when there is need of a solemn, pre-
lusive strain in his prose. But there is still some-
thing to explain. Montgomery’s phrases, for
example—“ opening chords—curtain-raising
seem pointed directly at a famous passage in De
Quincey’s 4 Opium-Eater’—the passage beginning:
The dream oommenced with a music which now I often
heard in dreams—a music of preparation and of awaken-
ing suspense ; a music like the opening of the Coronation
Ahthem, and which, like that, gave the feelinguf a vast
march—of infinite cavalcades filing off—and the tread of
innumerable armies. The morning was come of a mighty
day—a day of crisis and of final hope for human nature,
then suffering some mysterious eclipse, and laboring in
spme dread extremity. Somewhere, I knew not where—
somehow, I knew not how—by some being, I knew not
whom—a battle, a strife, an agony, was conducting—was
evolving like a great drama or piece of music.
Why is there here no marked iambic rhythm to
express the 4 prelusive solemnity ’ whioh De
Quincey certainly felt and desired to make his
readers feel? De Quincey also knew his blank-
verse poets ; yet their iambics could have brought
no effect of ‘opening chords’ and ‘curtain-rais-
ing ’ to him, or he would surely have em-
ployed them in the most signal 4 opening chords ’
passage in his writings—in all English litera-
ture. So that the ‘prelusive solemnity’ re-
sides, not in the iambic rhythm, but in Mont-
gomery’s mental associations with it; and for
people who have not his mental associations—
who have not borne away from the blank-verse
poets his ‘curtain-raising’ impressions—his effect
is possibly missed. Taking his own theory, that
is, that the force of the iambics does reside in the
mental associations. But partly, at least, it must
reside in the essential rhythm. Montgomery says,
in effect, that there were kings before Agamemnon,
and that Agamemnon holds his sceptre in the way
they taught him. But who taught them ? What
impulse swayed the original iambic metrist ?

Writes 4 J.M.’—
Your Sis had once an elder brother, Hesh. He was the
outcome of the incestuous
(Why ‘incestuous’? Who should dally if not
He and She ?)
dalliance of He and She in the cerebral mazes of one
D , an Oxford graduate, whom too much learning
had brought 4 under restraint.’ D was proud of
his bantling, and lauded its lovely Hebraic form and
sibilant forcefulness. But, called in consultation, edi-
torially I slew it. Only a Volapuker or one * under
restraint ’ could endure such verbal teratogeny .
(‘Teratogeny ’ is a 4 good word’—even Vola-
piikers and people 4 under restraint ’ have few
better. To save unfamiliar readers the trouble of
reference or cursing, it means ‘monster-making.’)
as results in Hesh and Sim and Sis. For the sake of
Language and Literature and Art and Everything, put
Sim and Sis under your 4 Unabridged,’ and sit on it hard
and heavily.)
* * * *
‘ J.M.’ seems a fair specimen of the pedantic
little insects who block the pathway to reform.
The new pronoun—Nom., se; Gen., sis ; Dat.,
sim; Acc., se —formed by eliding the h of the
masculine pronoun and substituting s from the
feminine ditto, was invented to imply both sexes
by a scribe tired of going round about the stupid
collocations ‘he or she,’ ‘his or her,’ ‘ him or
her.’ First used in jest, it is approving itself
in earnest as of real utility, and the 4 foreign
feeling ’is disappearing in the habit of use. As
yet, however, the new pronoun is only used pro-
visionally, till someone can supply a better.
» * * *
There are obvious objections, which grow
email when examined. 4 Se’ is arbitrary—so is
every other word. 4 Sis ’ is unpleasantly sibilant
—so is sister. (‘ Ser ’ was originally suggested in
place of ‘sis.’) ‘Se ’ may be mistaken for ‘she’
—in practice the user has not found it so mis-
taken. And advantages shine conspicuously.
Here is a passage from a recent newspaper—
Whoever the writer of the article may be, ha or she
Is evidently well acquainted with the subject
Take it in the new way—
Whoever the writer of the article may be, se is evi-
dently well acquainted with the subject.
Is it not worth while overcoming a little un-
familiarity—a task achieved every week by
writers and readers of the new words continually
becoming part of the language—so to improve
such an awkward sentence ? There is a gain in
sound, time, space, and perspicuity.
a * * •
‘Hesh’ is (of course) not so neat as 4 se,’and
seems to have wanted ‘ declining,’ but the word
gives proof of a laudable desire on ‘ D.’s ’ part to
bridge one of the troublesome gullies in English.
‘ J.M.’ may continue to dally down the gully and
up again if he chooses. No crusade is preached:
no proselytes are sought.
And, on the same subject, ‘6xB’
Lawson makes out that nobody cares for our Australian
bush as it is, but thinks “we might love it” if it could
boast of mountains, deserts, and mighty forests, etc.
Who are ‘we’? There are two distinct types of bushmen
—the real and the mongrel. The former are men who
have taken up bush life when young, from choice ; and
the bred and born bushmen —the sons of squatters, over-
seers, selectors, drovers, fencers, tanksinkers, stockmen,
and boundary riders. They love the bush, desolate and
monotonous though it be, as dearly as the Swiss loves his
Alps, and the Arab his desert. Why they love their
weird, drought-stricken, sun-smitten back country they
can no more explain than Arabs or Esquimaux in their
case.
The real bushmen are not outcasts out back. On the
contrary, they are at home and happy there alone, and
outcasts everywhere else. I knew an out-back station
manager’s son who was sent to town to study for a pro-
fession. To use his own words: ‘ The yearning for the
bush gnawed so strongly at my heart, that one fine morn-
ing I chucked it and took train to Bourke.’ Stepping out
of the coaoh at his station he exclaimed: 4 Thank God i I
am back in the mulga and smell eucalyptus once more 1’
And the ring in his voioe convinced me the thanks came
from the bottom of his heart.
The real bushroan is unassuming and quiet as his sur-
roundings. He thinks much and speaks little. In mixed
camps he is the listener. The mongrel bushman always
has most to say, and gives no one else a chance to speak.
The real bushman is full of poetioal feelings, and loves
poetry of the Gordon, “Banjo,” "Glenrowan” and
“ Breaker ” type, but seldom if ever writes verses. His
whole heart and soul are in the bush, and in his horses,
dogs, cattle and sheep; and to win the Mulga Hack-Race
is the height of his ambition.
Real bushmen are always in good demand as laborers,
especially on cattle-ruris out back, and can command
good wages. They are rarely long out of work and more
rarely still hard up ; and they hardly ever ‘ carry their
swag.' As for cadging a pint o’ flour, they abhor the idea
of it. The average bush native would sooner steal a
horse out of a police-paddock, or break into a Govern-
ment store, than 4 hump bluey ’ and beg. Of the many,
thousands of swagmen, or ' outcasts,’ as Lawson terms
them, who are roaming the backblocks to-day, not 3 per
cent, are bush natives.
The mongrel bushmen are city-bred, and come out-back
to get away from themselves or hide from others; or they
come to make money and get back to town as fast as
possible. These latter (in N.S. Wares) are mostly Maori-
landers, Tasmanians and Victorians. They stay only as
long as they can get shearing, and they would go to the
North Pole if they could make money there. Scenery is
no object to them. But their stay in the bush is too pass'
ing to be of any account.
The mongrel bushmen who stay and die out-back (much
against their will) are men of all trades and professions,
and from all parts of the universe. They are Bill and
Jim—the swagmen who carry a bundle of valueless rags
about the bush and 4 tramp for ever through boiling
mulga scrub and baking lignum ’ for no particular pur-
pose except cadging and to do a few weeks’ rouseabouting
once a year at shearing time. (The real bushman shears
or musters in the wool season.) I imagine that some of
the lowest specimens of the human race are found amongst
them. The • come-down ’ and 4 seen-better-days' men
are also much in evidence in this olass. These men are
outcasts in the full sense of the word—but not because
they are in the bush, for they would be outcasts any-
where. They are as much out of place in the bush as the
real bushman is in their homes—the slums of Sydney,
London, New York, Paris, Berlin, and St. Petersburg.
Always longing to get back to their city-haunts and
threepenny beer-shops, they bitterly hate and curse the
bush, and prefer ‘ not to take it at all,’ and eternally vow
to quit it at the earliest opportunity. When they do get a
cheque they carry it with astonishing regularity to the
nearest snakejuice foundry, and when broke they carry
their bundle of rags across some more * blazing plains ’
and crawl along some more ‘endless tracks of red dust
and grey*—seeking undeserved sympathy, cadging flour,
cursing the bush and vowing to quit some more, till
shearing comes round again. Being unable to ride and
useless among stock they can get but little work to do
between shearings. And these—or some of them—are the
men who, when their brains are softened by their un-
natural mode of life, fish diet, lack of women, and occa-
sional superfluity of snakejuice, write doggerel about
‘shining rivers’and ‘ rippling streams.’ The real bush-
man looks with just contempt upon the mongrel bush-
man.
To get an insight into Australian bush life one must
read ‘The Banjo ’ and similar Bulletin writers, as well as
Lawson. ‘The Banjo’ and others give us the real
bushman and his sentiments as truly as Lawson shows the
pseudo or mongrel bushman. The two characters make
up the back-country population, and both are well worth
writing up and reading about.
Writes ‘ Ignotus ’ —
It was in ’6B I read Lyall’s ‘Theology in Extremis.’
The verses deal with a decent Englishman, a freethinker,
who is offered his life by Sepoy rebels if he will abjure
Christianity. He looks at his pious comrade praying, and
muses—
‘Not for a moment faltereth he,
Sure of the promise and pardon of sin ;
Thus did the martyrs die, I see,
Little to lose and muckle to win ;
Death means Heaven : he longs to receive it,
But what shall I do if I don’t believe it? ’
And later on —
• Ay, but the word, if I could but have said it*
I by no terrors of hell perplext;
Hard to be silent and have no credit
From man in this world, or reward in the next;
None to bear witness and reckon the cost
Of the name that is saved by the life that is lost.’
And he elects to die.

Some years later—in some magazine—I saw 4 Medita-
tions of a Hindu.’ I quote a few lines—
‘All the world over, I wonder, in lands that I never have
trod,
Are the people eternally seeking for the signs and steps
of a god ? . . .
‘And the myriad idols around me, and the legion of
muttering priests,
The revels and rites unholy, the dark unspeakable feasts !
What have they wrung from the Silence? Hath even a
whisper come
Of the secret, Whence and Whither? Alas! for the gods
are dumb.
‘ Shall I list to the word of the English, who come from
the uttermost sea ?
“The Secret, hath it been told you, and what is your
message to me ? ”
It is nought but the wide-world story how the earth and
heavens began,
How the gods are glad and angry, and a Deity once was
man.’

And now there has come to me a little book whioh holds
these lines and others—“ Verses Written in India by Sir
Alfred Lyall(Lond., Kegan Paul, 4th ed., ’96). Many of
the pieces are so simple and direct that one seems to
read one’s own thoughts whispered back by a lover, by j
twin brother, by the breath of the scented Indian even-
ing, by . . . ? I will quote a few verses. A soldier
sees his friend buried in the jungle and hears the musio-*
‘ Full and clear from the pacing band,
Passionate strain of a love-lorn story—
How can they breathe it in strangers’ land
Air of our northern “Annie Laurie"? . , ,
•Chaining his spirit’s upward flight,
Staying his soul, though at Heaven’s own portal,
With the soft refrain of a lost delight,
With the shadowy charm of a fairy mortal.
‘ Lured by the sensuous melody’s spell,
Little he recks of the angels’ glory,
Piercing sad is the Earth’s farewell
Sighed in the music of “ Annie Laurie.’"
* » » •
Another soldier stands waiting with his colonel and the
colonel’s wife. Rebels surround them. The colonel
eleots to stay.
*. . . And she bent from her saddle low,
And she touched my cheek and whispered “Go!”
With her eyes all full of despair and woe
“ Good bye, sweetheart, for ever i ” ’
The last verses are exactly in Gordon’s manner, if yon
read them all through. And, indeed, they are very like
Gordon in many things—differing, however, in this: that
Gordon continually sets one’s teeth on edge by dragging
in French and Latin and Greek, of which languages he
knew nothing, with ludicrous effect. I remember once
screaming with laughter at earo being made neuter. It
reminded me of the very oomio lines I had to learn by
heart as a child— ! ‘
‘ Caro carnis mavult se
Femininis addere.’
Comic, taken one way. Well, Lyall is evidently a good
classic, though he doesn’t obtrude it. Take his para-
phrase of Horace, Lib. 11., Ode vii. The last verse runs—-
• Let propriety go to the devil,
Be Anonyma queen of the feast—
I can’t see the harm of a revel
With a friend who is home from the East.’
He must have understood Horace well to have caught his
manner so exactly. Who Lyall is, I never could exaotly
find out. I believe he was in some very high position in
the Civil Service in the N.W. provinces of India. Ten
years ago I wrote to him to ask where I could get his
verses, mentioning my clear memory of the piece pub-
lished in ’6B. He replied to the effect that he didn’t
know—didn’t apparently think his verses much good.
He ought to know, one would think, but I can’t say I
agree with him.
* • • *
[According to the reference-books, Sir A. Lyall
(aged 61) is still above ground as Lieut.-Gov. of
the Indian N.W. Provinces. His brother, Sir
James, was Lieut.-Gov. of the Punjab. Alfred
has other literary merits in the shape of social
and religious studies of Asiatic life ; he wrote
‘ Warren Hastings ’ for the 4 Eng. Men of Action ’
series; and his ' Rise of the Brit. Dominion in
India ’ reached a 3rd ed. in ’93. The father, an
Eng. clergyman, travelled, wrote, and philoso-
phised to some extent, and for several years edited
the Annual Register.]
Writes 'J. Jingle’—
Lawson's tirade against the backblocks is wild, vague,
and in places distinctly untrue. For instance, to say that
‘ out West ’ there are not, nor ever were, ‘ shining rivers,
grassy plains, western hills, dawn, morn, gloaming,
gigantic trees,’ &c., is, to put it mildly, “ a verity upside-
down.’ In the backiest backblocks each and all of these
occur in their place and season, in conjunction and
separately. That the bush-bard (of late much maligned)
should prefer to write of the dawn when it is flushing a
grassy plain and shining river, rather than to picture the
plain scorched and withered and the river dry, only shows
the bush-bard’s appreciation of the beautiful, which is
the first quality required to entitle him to the name of
bard at all.
A while-back Bulletin correspondent made out the
bushman to be a stolid individual with no note of music
in him, no poetic soul. Now Lawson comes along to say
that Bill and Jim are teeming with fictional genius and
imagination, and reel off poetry whenever the billy is
boiliDg. He says, moreover, that they shouldn’t do it,
because they are thereby misleading the poor devils living
on the Australian rim whose thoughts might wander
westward. Certes! here flies a spark from the soul of
the man who wrote ‘The Men who Come Behind.’
' Stand back, Bill and Jim! while I point out to the Aus-
tralian rimmers the drear results of trekking West.’
Now, there is nothing upside-down about the verity that
humping bluey in the backblocks—either in flood-time,
drought-time, or good season—is a hard life ; but where
does the rear-guard find life easy?
That the bush is often ugly enough to breed pessimism
is the worst of reasons for deriding unpessimistic bush-
poetry, and so robbing the real, genuine bush-dwellers
(not the rim-dwellers like Lawson, who make one hard trip
and vow it will last them a life-time) of the pleasure
of welcoming in the words of others an interpretation
of the (possibly rare) beauties which they see and feel yet
cannot themselves interpret. Also, why should we love
our West if it were a desert with an oasis here and
there ? or composed of ranges of inaccessible mountains
unfit for the pasture of anything but chamois? and how
does Lawson know that we don’t love it as it is, anyway ?
Men and women go and live there permanently, yvhile in
English Africa they have had their choice of deserts,
jungles, mountains, and pastoral paradises to love and
live in, and in none of them have they settled as Aus-
tralians settle.
I herewith beg to propose a motion that in a certain
modified form we do love our West, also that we
love and respect the second detachment of track-blazing
men and women who dwell therein. (The men who came
first didn’t blaze all the tracks, by Jupiter !) Hands up.
I thought so. Thank you. The meeting will adjourn
with two lines from that stirring little poem of ‘ Glen-
rowan’s ’—
‘ Our pride is the path of our fathers,
Our hope’s in the sons of our home.’
That’s the sentiment!—don’t forget the fathers, but give
the sons a little credit too !
A BALLADE OF BY GONES.
Into what dim, unlettered night
Do our romantic idols stray?
Whither has Trilby taken flight
And where does Ben Hur’s chariot sway?
The Little Minister is gray ;
No more does Robert Elsmere pose ;
Where do the favorites delay ?
Nay, where is yestermorning’s rose ?
Forgotten is The Manxman's might;
And what of Tess do bookmen say?
The Prisoner of Zenda’s plight
Is one with Fauntleroy’s at play.
Mulvaney, Ortheris! Where are they?
On Sherlock Holmes the shadows close;
Why do their memories decay?
Ah, where is yestermorning’s rose?
They walked Romance’s flowery height,
Nor Howells’ self could them dismay ;
Made all of sweetness and of light, !
For which Philistines loved to pay.
Now each his unlamented way
To libraried oblivion goes,
And on their tombs we toss a spray
Of yestermorning’s faded rose.
ENVOY.
Prints! 'twas not yours the fate to stay
With all the art the era knows,
For fame in this decadent day
Is but as yestermorning’s rose.
EDWARD A. CHURCH, in Am, BOOKMAS.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE MEN WE MIGHT HAVE BEEN.
When God’s wrath-cloud is o’er me,
Affrighting heart and mind ;
When days seem dark before me,
And days seem black behind ;
Those friends who think they know me—
Who deem their insight keen—
They ne’er forget to show me
The man I might have been.
He’s rich and independent,
Or rising fast to fame ;
His bright star is ascendant,
The country knows his name ;
His houses and his gardens
Are splendid to be seen ;
His fault the wise world pardons—
The man I might have been.
His fame and fortune haunt me ;
His virtues wave me back ;
His name and prestige daunt me
When I would take the track ;
But you, my friend true-hearted—
God keep our friendship green !
You know ho.w I was parted
From all I might have been.
But what avails the ache of
Remorse or weak regret?
We’ll battle for the sake of
The men we might be yet !
We’ll strive to keep in sight of
The brave, the true and clean,
And triumph yet in spite of
The men we might have been.
HENRY LAWSON.
ME AND M‘GILL.
Me and M‘Gill was mates, yer see,
A matter of twenty year ;
If ever yer run agen him or me
The other ye’d know was near.
For if luck was in or if luck was out,
Sober or drunk or ill,
We battled it out through flood and drought
Me and my mate M'Gill.
We pooled our cheques at the shed we rung
With a glorious tune in hopes ;
Our picks at each other’s side we swung
In the Barrier’s stewing stopes;
And out where the glittering sun-rays bake
At Wilcannia, fit to kill,
We chipped out chunks of opal flake,
Me and my mate M'Gill.
When cheques were blued and we’d lost our nags
As many had gone before,
We’d grab our billies and hump our swags
And start on the road once more.
But never ye’d hear us squeal or fuss;
River or plain or hill,
Dust or mud was the .same to us,
Me and my mate M‘Gill.
We got through onst to the town all right
And like toffs we played it high,
Till a cop got on to M‘Gill one night,
And Mac got on to his eye.
Another came up and I fetched him one —
We was dealin’ it out with will—
Then three months’ hard in the gaol we done:
Me and my mate M‘Gill.
But a girl appeared, and our senses flew,
And me and M‘Gill fell out;
For we’d acted the fool, as a man will do
When a cunnin’ woman’s about.
She kidded us each that she liked him best,
While we’d money to fill the till:
Then one went East and the other went West—
Me and my mate M'Gill.
But, by-and-by, when the both of us land
At the homestead up above,
To see if the cove has reserved a stand
In the shed of eternal love —
Well ! if there ain’t room for both him and me,
We’ll step it out straight down hill,
And be mates in hell, as we useter be —
Me and my mate M‘Gill.
Vic.
MONTAGUE GROVER.
THE CRY OF THE COOKIE.
(After Thomas Moore.)
I never had old sheep to sell
Whose wool was parting down their back;
But what it turned as dry as—well,
Until they faded off in stacks.
I never prayed it might keep dry
But what there fell a foot o’ rain
To send the price o’ sheep so high
That I could ne’er stock-up again.
I never raised a crop o’ corn
To glad me with its golden ears
But it was reaped and held in pawn
For overdrafts of droughty years.
I never threshed a stack o’ wheat,
But when I took it to the mill
The price was sich, ’twould scarce receipt
That wily miller’s grocery bill.
I never knew the price so high
That growin’ wheat ’ud really pay,
But what the season turned so dry
I had to cut it down for hay.
I never had a chance but once
To get my neck from out the yoke—
I sold out well, and lodged “ the bunce,”
And then the blessed bank went broke.
N.S.W.
CRIPPS CLARK.

SATURDAY, APRIL 3, 1897.
“Will You Walk into My Parlor?”
Almost as soon as the Federal Convention
opened the Queensland Government tried,
by a quaint and characteristic device, to put
its subtle spoke in the wheel and bring the
whole movement to wreck. The device was
not, it is true, a very brilliant one ; still it was
a piece of well-contrived cunning of the
kind with which the Byrnes-Tozer Ministry
is so familiar. According to the voice which
came unexpectedly out of the North,
Queensland now desires, for some quite in-
comprehensible reason, and under various
impossible circumstances, to join the Con-
vention at the last moment or a little later.
When the last Convention met, Queensland
professed to be, in a certain rather uncertain
fashion of its own, a friend to the policy of
union, and not only promised to make one in
the movement but agreed informally to
choose its ten delegates by the same process
of popular election as its neighbors. Then
the Nelson Government fell away by in-
stalments. First it proposed to deprive the
people of the right of election and to select
ten sound, reliable boodle-representatives
itself by means of its Parliamentary majority.
Next it created a beautifully realistic-looking
quarrel between the Houses of Parliament as
to the election of the ten delegates ; having
stolen the right of election from the people
the two branches of the Legislature pro-
ceeded to quarrel, or pretend to quarrel,
over the plunder. Finally it threw the
matter up in real oV affected disgust, and
announced that Queensland would stand out.
Having thus left the Federal movement
itself, the Nelson Government proceeded to
do all it knew to break up the concert of the
other provinces. It became seized with a
sudden reverence for the miserable, impo-
tent Federal Council which has been such an
utter failure for the last 10 years at Hobart,
and tried to set up this dead thing in active
rivalry with the live movement at Adelaide.
It had schemes for making the Council elec-
tive, for increasing its authority, for inducing
the dissenting provinces to come in, and
generally it did its best to drag the old insti-
tution across the scent and thus distract
attention. When this failed, the principal
Queensland politicians became the voice of a
Jeremiah in the distance, deriding the new
Federal movement and prophesying all kinds
of disaster.
* * * •
Then, when the Convention actually met,
and it began to appear that Federation was
really a possibility, the Queensland Govern-
ment came forward with a new and un-
expected proposition. It now states that it
is,- after all, really anxious to join the
Convention, and' if that body will adjourn
indefinitely the Queensland Parliament will
mqet by-and-bye, and, some time after it has
met, the Government will introduce a Bill
nominating ten delegates, and if the Bill
passes in course of time, the ten Queenslanders
will be ready after a while to take a hand
in the work. And. if it doesn’t pass the
Nelson Government will possibly have
something else to suggest. And if it isn’t
intended to pass, it will be merely another
red herring drawn across the track, and a
long delay to no purpose, and, during that
delay, the Federal movement will probably
have grown scT cold that it will be hard to
find even a single spark among the ashes.
* * ♦ *
The gorgeous insolence of the proposal
calls for a certain admiration. Queensland
dropped out of the movement many weeks
ago, and ever since then its Government has
been openly hostile. During all these weeks
it made no attempt whatever to resurrect
the Federal Enabling Bill ; it had unlimited
opportunity to come in, and it refused
to avail itself thereof for the sufficient
reason that it wanted to stay out. Hav-
ing fooled away all this time it now
asks the other five provinces to wait
for an indefinite period while it changes
its mind. It doesn’t propose, apparently, to
call Parliament together at once, and pass
the Enabling Bill, but suggests that the
other provinces shall suspend operations till
Parliament comes together in the ordinary
slow course of events. Having broken its
implied promise to appoint its delegates by
popular election, and having also broken its
promise to push the Enabling Bill through
Parliament—having broken, in short, every
promise it made on the subject—it now
invites the other Australias to rely entirely
on its new promise, and to hang up
all business pending fulfilment. Also,
having declared that it couldn’t get the
Enabling Bill through owing to the hostile
attitude of the Legislative Council, it now
invites the other five provinces to wait on the
understanding that it can getthe Bill through.
And it asks its neighbors to believe that it is in
dead earnest about the matter, and is full of
a desire to go straight to work, in proof of
which the two principal members of the
Government are on their way to England,
and won’t be on hand to do any work at all
for several months. Queensland’s desire to
come in just now is a woefully thin pretence,
and, regarded as a plausible lie, it is far
below the Boodle Government’s usual stan-
dard of plausibility.
* * * *
In viewing the position of the Nelson-
Byrnes-Tozer Ministry it must always be
borne in mind that that Ministry is against any
Australian Federation which includes Queens-
land. Such a Federation must, sooner or later,
abolish black-labor; no matter what stipula-
tions are made at the outset in favor of the
Kanaka and the Chow the influence of the
five white provinces and the white element
in Queensland must some day bear them
down. Even if the Kanaka is especially
provided for in the Constitution it only
means that the Constitution will presently
be amended for the express purpose of
throwing out the piebald races of the North.
Also the Federal Parliament, being an Aus-
tralian and not a mere Brisbane institution,
would probably listen, sooner or later, to the
Northern demand for separation; if it hasn’t
the power at first to interfere in such a
matter it will probably acquire it later on.
For these two reasons, and possibly for
several others, the Nelson Ministry loathes
the idea of a union of the six provinces.
Also, it is almost equally hostile to Federa-
tion without Queensland. Queensland might
remain outside at first, but. the necessity of
getting inside the Federal Customs tariff in
ordertofinda marketfor its produce, of having
its credit supported by the Federal Govern-
ment, and of enjoying the same railway facili-
ties as the other Australias, the need to have
a share in the Federal defence system, and
many other considerations, will inevitably
make Queensland come in later on. And
when it comes in late its pet nigger is just as
much doomed as if it came in early. Whether
Australia federates with Queensland or with-
out Queensland it is equally the beginning
of the end for the cheap nigger labor system
and probably for the subjection of North
Queensland to the South. Therefore, the
present Queensland Ministry is against
Federation all the time. It first tried to
wreck the movement by standing out; having-
failed in that it now attempts to wreck it by
professing a desire to come in, but it is
equally against Federation either way.
* * * «
If the Queensland proposal had been
accepted—or if it is still accepted—the
Boodle Government gets quite a number of
chances in the illimitable chapter of acci-
dents. The Convention would adjourn with
practically nothing done. Hie Premiers
would go to England and would lose touch
of the question, and their doings there
would divert attention from Federal matters,
and their absence would seriously prolong
the delay." When they came back it
would be necessary to call Parliament
together in all the five mainland pro-
vinces, and present the overdue financial
statement and the Estimates, and to get
Supply, and do other urgent business, and
again Federation would be hung up. Imme-
diately afterwards (about October), the Vic-
torian general elections are due, and, in the
absence of the Victorian delegates, who
would be busy hustling for their own seats
and keeping the MTntyre-Gillies crowd
out of office, there would presumably be a
further postponement. If the Turner
Ministry went down its defeated members
would presumably resign their seats
Convention, and more time would be lost in
the election of their successors. Between
the things that might happen and the things
that alihost certainly would happen the post-
ponement asked for by the Queensland
Government would probably end all pos-
sibilities of pushing through any Federal
business this year. And by next year so
many matters would have intervened to
divert public attention, and there would have
been so many re-shufflings of the political
cards, that the subject would perhaps be
about as far away from the public mind as is
the late lamented Moses, whose tomb is sup-
posed to lie somewhere in a south-eastern
direction from the Dead Sea.
* * * •
Then the Queensland Government would
have two courses to choose from. It might
bring in its Enabling Bill after a lengthy
delay, and wrangle over it for many days,
and then drop it as was done on the first
occasion, and inform the other provinces
that it had again resolved to stand out. This
is the course it would probably pursue if
there was reason to believe that the delay
had so far killed the Federal movement, for
the time being, that this last fiasco would
destroy any public interest which remained.
Or it might pass the Bill as gradually as
possible, and send down ten solid Tory
nominees to wreck Federation all they knew
by making impossible demands ; or, if it
couldn’t be wholly wrecked, to make the
Federal Constitution as Tory and as unpopu-
lar and as useless as possible. In any case,
it must be remembered that Queensland does
not propose to send popular representatives ;
it promises—-whatever its promise may be
worth—only to send ten nominees of the
true Boodle Government pattern, and the
sort of Constitution which Nelson and
Byrnes and Tozer’s nominees will advocate
is certain to be as unlike the kind that New
South Wales and Victoria and South Aus-
tralia and the Queensland democracy desire
as anything can possibly be. The Queens-
land Ministry really asks the other provinces
to wait indefinitely, at great inconvenience,
so that it may, or may not, send down ten
men to do as much injury to the Federal
cause as possible. And the fact that
Premier Reid supports this indefinite
delay, and that he started suggesting a
postponement even before the Convention
reached Adelaide at all, supplies a stronger
reason than the many which went before it
for doubting that fat and shufflesome person’s
sincerity. Much lingering is the surest
way to wreck Federation. Reid indirectly
suggested a possible postponement when he
returned from his famous Queensland mis-
sion. He suggested it again in Melbourne
two days before the Convention met. He
suggested it some more at Adelaide before
the Convention had even formally set to
work. Altogether, Premier Reid’s yearning
for postponement is growing too obvious.
The Great Patent Medicine Swindle.
The Bulletin uses the above term advisedly.
It believes that nine patent medicines out of
ten are more or less swindles, and that seven
or eight out of ten are wholly swindles with no
redeeming feature about them whatever. In
this respect they are no worse than many other
things, but they occupy a position of privilege
unlike that of any other swindle in this country.
The individual who gets money from a healthy
person by presenting a valueless cheque, on the
false pretence that it is good, is a criminal, and
liable to a long term in gaol. The individual
who gets money from a sick person by falsely
pretending that certain slush in a bottle is
something quite different from what it is, is not
a criminal ; at least the law in this country
has never treated him as one. The person who
gets value for what is valueless, by putting the
name of a non-existent person to a bank-order,
is run in. The individual who gets value for
what is equally valueless, by putting the name
of a non-existent person to a testimonial alleg-
ing that the individual, who never lived ami
whose address is wholly imaginary, was
snatched from the brink of the grave by the
use of something at 7s. 6d., is, for some occult
reason or other, not run in. The law is be-
ginning to worry a good deal in some places
about the authenticity of prospectuses and
balance-sheets, but the authenticity of the
patent medicine testimonial is a thing of
which it takes no cognisance. It is ° also
commencing to trouble itself considerably
about food and drink being what they pre-
tend to be; the idea of trying to ensure
that medicine shall be what it pretends to be
troubles nobody. The code agitates itself
greatly to protect the public against its own
weakness in trusting to confidence men, three-
card men, bogus-cheque men, welshers, “ long-
firms, counterfeit-coiners, dealers in bogus
notes, sham bankers, and the like; but the
patent-medicine confidence-man is free of all
supervision. This immunity is wonderful and
unaccountable.
* * * „
The patent-medicine quack is about the
meanest of all the frauds above ground. He
lives on the sick, the suffering, the miserable,
the incurable. He probably makes his biggest
income (in proportion to their numbers) out of
the incurable ; the desire to remain a while
longer in this most disappointing of all worlds
is so strong that the average individual, when
given up as hopeless by the medical faculty,
will believe anything, and pay 7s. 6d. a bottle
for anything, that promises him relief. In that
respect the medicine fraud is on a par with the
mediaeval friar who sold pardons and thus kept
people out of hell for a consideration. He also
undertakes to keep people out of hell for a con-
sideration ; it is the latent fear of hell, or some-
thing unknown and unpleasant, whether it is
called by that name or any other, which is the
fear of death ; and the man with the mystery
in the bottle trades upon that fear and lives by
it. Also he does it with a ghastly and fraudu-
lent insolence which, in almost any other walk
of life, would result in penal servitude.
• * * *
The thing above all others which sells the
lying mixture in the bottle is the published
letter from the patient who had suffered for
years from more diseases than any one human
being could hold, and who tried the mixture and
was brought back to life. Most of these letters
are lies, and most of those which are not abso-
lute lies can’t bear inspection. They are a
curious study. One patent medicine dealer
lived for years by publishing a solitary testi-
monial from an alleged patient who was cured
in the ’6o’s. (He is still publishing it, by the
way, and seems to trade successfully on that
more or less real document of many years ago.)
Most of the tribe, however, publish a con-
tinuous stream of grateful letters from indi-
viduals who were cured in Great Britain or
America—mostly people named Smith who
can’t be verified, and who are alleged to hang
out in small villages, some of which appear in no
gazetteer, and presumably lie at the other end
of nowhere. As they are too far off to be in-
quired into, they serve their purpose well
enough without involving any risk. The local
testimonials carry more weight, but also
involve more trouble. The present writer
recently investigated a number issued in
this country by two or three advertisers.
The majority, so far as could be found out,
were bought and paid for; in a country where
an offer of a billet at 20s. a week will bring
along 400 applicants, there are thousands of
people who, for a very small sum, will sign
and swear to a declaration that they had any
incurable disease that was ever heard of, or all
the incurable diseases on record, and recovered
through using one bottle of anything under
the sun. At all events the writer knows of
people who have been always in apparently the
best of health, but whose names appear under-
neath published letters setting forth that they
were laid up for incredible periods with all man-
ner of horrors until their attention was drawn
to Blank’s great what-is-it at 3s. 6d. or 7s. lid
per bottle, and they took 19 bottles instantly, or
something to that effect, and were cured. (The
19 bottles are a wise device which tends to make
the incurable purchase large consignments.)
And the writer presumes that these people were
paid for saying such things, as hardly anybody
would make that kind of an exhibition of him-
self gratis.
* * * *
Some of the others, apparently, are not paid
for. Quite a number bear the names of people
who can’t be found in the directory at the
alleged address. Sometimes the address is
there, but some other person is in possession ;
sometimes the address can’t be found, either.
One recent case dated from number one
hundred and something in a little street
where the numbers, according to the direc-
tory account, don’t run as high as thirty,
An instance that the writer knows of was a
matter of mere forgery ; the medicine dealer
had a nodding acquaintance with his victim
and presented him with a bottle of his particu-
lar horror, and then published a letter pur-
porting to be from him setting forth that the
gratis customer—who was in perfect health—
had just recovered from a shuddersome variety
of complaints, and had been practically a walk-
ing charnel-house. In another instance the
medicine-dealer obtained the signature of a
friend in blank, and then went back on his
friendship by filling in over it a statement of
weird symptoms which made all who knew the
alleged owner of them view him with sus-
picion for long afterwards. One person whom
the writer can produce received a letter from a
perfectly unknown medicine-fiend, to the effect
that his testimonial had come to hand, and the
fiend was deeply grateful, and would publish it
at once, and congratulated him on his recovery.
In this case publication was stopped by prompt
threats of legal proceedings and assault and
battery, and the letter—which bore no resem-
blance at all to the alleged writer’s real cali-
graphy—was impounded and destroyed. Some
of these testimonials are unspeakably weird
affairs; one of the weirdest—it went the rounds
of the Australian press some years ago —set
forth that the writer, now perfectly recovered,
had been in a dreadfully bad state and used to
swell up so suddenly in the street that the
buttons flew off her clothes with the unex-
pected enlargement. The only really genuine
testimonial that the writer knows of was one
signed some years ago by a well-known Aus-
tralian politician. He had been cured of an
incurable complaint till he died, and, feeling a
good deal better almost in his last moments, he
signed a statement attesting his complete re-
covery. Then he pegged out, and a most valu-
able testimonial had to be promptly withdrawn
from circulation.
* * * *
Ihe average patent nostrum consists of a
weak and non-committal concoction of some
ordinary drug with which every physician is
acquainted, and this compound is aimed pro-
miscuously at every complaint under the sun.
Its sole merit, as a rule, lies in the advertising
and the (mostly bogus) testimonials. Every
year one or two hundred thousand pounds of
good Australian legal tender go out of the
country in payment for this mullock ; in five
provinces it is chargeable with a verv moderate
duty, but in N.S. Wales it comes in absolutely
free, and yields the State no revenue whatso-
ever. Under these circumstances The Bulle-
tin suggests four much-needed reforms. In
the first place, all patent-medicines should be


heavily taxed. Secondly, they should bear
on the label a definite statement in
plain English of the ingredients of which
they are composed, said statement being
supplied by the State analyst and cer-
tified by him. Thirdly, the patent medicine
dealer should be allowed to publish in Australia
the testimonials of his Australian cured patients
only, so that there will be a reasonable chance
of verifying his statements. Also, the publi-
cation of bogus testimonials, whether they are
the testimonials of people who don’t exist, or
those of alleged people with no address attached
for purposes of verification, or those of people
with bogus addresses, or those of people who
exist and can he found, but who never had the
diseases alleged against them or were never
cured of them as stated, should be recognised
by law as what it is—about the worst kind of
an attempt to get money by false pretences —
and should be visited on the first conviction
with a minimum fine of wfilOO, with the alterna-
tive of twelve months. Extra convictions
should he charged for on an increasing scale.
This is merely bringing the law as to false
pretences in medicine into line witli the law as
to false pretences in everything else. A code
which is so particular about the man who
swindles the healthy population with a bogus
cheque for <£l, and takes no notice of the one
who swindles the sick out of vast sums
witli bogus accounts of imaginary cures, is an
anomaly that needs strong treatment.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Party Government in the Commonwealth.
The first voice raised in the Convention against
importing the clumsy foolishness of "Govern-
ment by Contradiction into the Federal Legis-
lature was that of Richard Baker, of S.A. It
is a noteworthy circumstance, by the way, that
most of the Conservative members of the Con-
vention who have developed themselves up to
date have proved reasonably liberal and pro-
gressive. Probably their conservatism is largely
a matter of habit and association, and when
they get away from the old atmosphere and the
old environment a lot of the Tory instinct is
left behind. Baker, K.C.M.G.—the man whom
Kingston went out looking for with a gun—
was certainly not the man whom anybody
expected to raise the first demand for the
abolition of the ancient bosh of Party
Government, and his pronouncement is all
the more gratifying. His theory—one which
The Bulletin has persistently advocated for
many years-—is compressible into four pro-
positions : (1) A system which requires one
party to uphold practically everything the Go-
vernment does, however bad it may be, and
another party to denounce practically every-
thing the Government does, however good it
may be, is demoralising ; (2) a system by which
the country pays (say) 45 men to do everything
that is wanted, and (say) 35 men to prevent
them doing anything that is wanted, is idiotic ;
(3) a system by which Parliament must accept
or reject the whole Ministry, and must accept
or reject the whole Ministerial policy—by
which it can’t keep Turner and throw out
Duffy, or keep Reid and bounce the fish-like
Gould, and according to which it can’t accept
those features of the Govt, policy that it wants
without also accepting those it doesn’t want, and
under which it can’t have Freetrade without also
approving of Reid and his mendacious budget,
or have Protection without cordially endorsing
Lyne and his heavy-going Toryism—is utterly
pig-headed. Also (4) a system by which a
great part of the time of the House is taken up
by considering whether the Government shall
be thrown out or not is wasteful and wearisome
and utterly profitless. The remedy advocated
by this paper, and supported so unexpectedly
by Baker, K.C.M.G., is the Swiss method of
government whereby the House elects Ministers
for a fixed term ; retains the right to endorse or
alter their policy instead of merely retaining
the right to follow them blindly or eject them
blindly ; has no want-of-confidence motions,
and isn’t compelled to support a bad ad-
ministrator because he has become identified
with a good policy, or support a bad
policy in order to retain a good administrator.
The utter accursedness of the Party system, if
it is applied to the Federal Parliament, will be
that it will be necessary to organise an Opposi-
tion, then it will be necessary to find something
for the Opposition to quarrel about, and, when
the quarrel is thus carefully constructed, it
may open a breach which will keep open for
centuries. Yet there must be an Opposition
if the Party system is to last, and there must be
a quarrel and a chronic row and a bitter ani-
mosity to keep the Opposition alive. Party
Government is bad enough in the old Legisla-
latures where the quarrel is already estab-
lished and in full blast; to import it into a new
Legislature which might otherwise get along
without any such established shindy is sheer
insanity.
The Wrong 1 End of the Stick !
The Sydney Alfred Hospital authorities have
been pointing out to Government the “ necessity
for taking some action to cope with the marked
increase of phthisis in N.S. Wales.” Consump-
tives, they assert, should be assorted, according
to stage of disease, into three classes, each of
which should receive special treatment, in a
special hospital, in a special situation. Which
suggestion—humane, and rational, and thera-
peutically up-to-date though it doubtless is—
impels The Bulletin once more to “move the
previous question,” and to assert that the Royal
Commission asked for would be rather more
usefully employed at the bottom than at the
top of the question. Causation—where cure
is practically hopeless is much the more
important consideration of the two; and
that much of the tuberculous disease in
New South Wales lies directly and unques-
tionably at the door of Government itself,
let a con file of choice little items help to
show. At Balmain police-court, the other day,
a slaughterman at Glebe Island abattoirs was
fined for having cut away from a pig’s carcase
the sub-maxillary glands, which, according to
the prosecuting official, were “ one suppurating
mass of tubercle.” At Glebe court, same day,
the owners of a bacon-factory were fined for
having a tuberculous pig’s carcase in the salt-
ing-room amongst the sound meat. Now, the
circumstance that in both these cases the
inspector “ happened-in ” at the right moment
for the public is no proof that he doesn’t much
oftener fail to happen-in at the wrong moment
for the public - poisoner. The former of
these offenders, in fact, had been warned
before for tampering with diseased carcaSes,
and it is pretty certain that, if—even under
the reputedly rigid Glebe Island system of
supervision—carcase-faking is not uncommon,
the tubercle-propagating possibilities of the
comparatively uninspected suburban slaughter-
houses are a ghastly reproach to a community
which calls itself civilised and professes to be
vastly concerned about the consumption-crop
due in large measure to its own murderous in-
difference to the sowing of the seed. The un-
licensed and non-supervised private slaughter-
house is more than a nineteenth century
anomaly ; it is a national crime ! And even
such Government inspection as exists is ren-
dered mostly perfunctory and incomplete by
the ridiculous inadequacy of a staff, which
should be doubled—trebled—quadrupled, if
necessary. If any case should be one of
“ D n the expense ! ” it is that of the conser-
vation of the public health. But there doesn’t
seem to be a very massive chance of driving
this into the skulls of a people which, while it
shrieks itself into hysterics over a poor half-
dozen alleged killings by one Butler, goes
placidly on poisoning itself by thousands and
its posterity by incalculable multitudes of
disease-inheritors. Phthisis, besides being
hereditary to the extent of transmitting an in-
creased liability to infection with its specific
germ, is more inevitably, if more tardily, fatal
than small-pox, cholera, or the plague; and
yet, while we guard ourselves with feverish
assiduity against these enemies from without,
we calmly take to our bosoms—or our lungs—a
disease which has in Europe destroyed, first
and last, more lives than all the wars of history
and all the non - indigenous epidemics put
together. Truly, as regards this matter, the
end of our national nose appears to be unduly
remote.
The Tasmanian Fly-Speck.
When the Federal Senate comes to be planned
out Tasmania promises to be an obstacle. It is
practically agreed that the (nominally) Lower
House of the Commonwealth shall represent the
States according to population, therefore in it
N.S.W. and Victoria will, at the outset, own
more than half the votes. If the Senate is fixed
on the same basis then the less populous States
(S.A., Westralia and Tasmania) will be practi-
cally annexed ; their share of votes will not be
large enough in either House to command any
serious attention. Therefore, it is proposed—
rightly enough—to compromise by giving
all the States, large and small, an equal
number of votes in the Senate. So far as
the mainland provinces are concerned this idea
is good enough. Westralia, S.A., and Queens-
land will some day hold as large a population
as either 1 N.S.W. or Victoria, and then the
present inequality will right itself. Some other
day, having more room than either Vic. or
N.S.W., they will probably have a much
larger population, and then the equal State
representation idea may do a good turn
to the two provinces now on top, and pre-
vent them altogether losing their politi-
cal consideration. But Tasmania makes the
thing ridiculous. Tasmania is so small that
the inequality, in its case, will never
right itself. It can never hold enough popula-
tion to make it equal with any of the mainland
provinces. With the others the equal State
representation will justify itself by-and-bye ;
in the case of Tasmania it can’t. Westralia
will probably separate later on into three or
four provinces, or perhaps a dozen. The Terri-
tory will certainly set up for itself some day
apart from S.A. Queensland will probably
split into at least three, and even N.S.W. may
separate into two pieces in course of time. But
even one-twelfth of Westralia would be three
times the size of Tasmania, and one-third of
Queensland would be eight times its size.
Tasmania is too small to be taken seriously; to
have a full-grown Government and Opposition
and two HousesofParliamentanda Governorand
a separate debt and political policy and deficit
for 160,000 people and 26,000 square miles of
territory is sheer waste. If the Isle of Sleep
could devise some way of amalgamating with
Victoria, it would save a great deal of needless
expense, and the two together would still be
the very smallest province of Australia—just
about one-third the size of the next smallest,
N.S.W. The idea may seem impracticable at
present, but if Tasmania would think it over
next time it is awake and decide to annex Vic-
toria—it wouldn’t sound so bad if it were put
that way—the results should be beneficial for
both parties. If something of the sort isn’t
done, Tasmania and Victoria will by-and-bye
be the two least important provinces in the
Federation, or out of it, as the case may be.
Through mere want of elbow-room they are
bound to be left hopelessly behind in popula-
tion and importance. Combination would at
least keep them to the front a good while
longer.

That brief and hurried globe-trotter, editor
Cooper, of the dreary Tory Scotsman, says,
among other good advice, that if he could bring
out 10,000 Scotch artisans, they could all find
employment in the N.S.W. backblocks inside a
few weeks. Mr. Cooper having been a week
or two in Sydney, and having enjoyed the (vide
S.M. Herald) “well-deserved compliment of
being elected an honorary member of the —haw
—Union Club,” naturally knows all about the
unemployed, the rabbit plague, the drought
horror, the ticks, the low price of wool, the
railway freight difficulty, the bush-fires, and
all the other obstacles out back, and can see his
way to so far overcome them as to find work
for 10,000 men right off. Meanwhile, over
a great part of the north of Scotland
the population has been decreasing for
many years, and though that place is much
nearer and more convenient to him the
bounding Cooper never thinks, apparently, of
finding work for his 10,000 artisans there.
Also, The Bulletin draws attention to the
fact that out of 54 great cities enumerated by
Mulhall the only ones that have grown less in
the last half-century than Cooper’s own city
of Edinburgh are Amsterdam, Cairo, Calcutta,
Constantinople,Dublin, Lisbon, Madrid, Naples,
Palermo, Smyrna, Tunis, and Venice. If the
Edinburgh globe-trotter can’t keep his own
city moving in the great world procession, but
it must needs lag at the tail among the lost-dog
towns of Turkey and Egypt, he is a nice
individual to advise Australia as to how to
create a population boom. Mr. Cooper,
by the way, runs the dreariest city paper
in Great Britain. The Scotsman is a how-
ling wilderness of Presbyterianism and
dust, and, barring the old Edinburgh Daily
Review, which died years ago after a vain
effort to be as depressed as its contemporary
in the adjacent thoroughfare—the Scotsman
came out in a crooked side street off the old
High-street and the Review used to be pub-
lished in a bye-way up 127 steps —it has long
held the record for tomb-like solemnity. If
Cooper can’t run a paper decently, considering
that he should know something about papers,
his attempts at running a country which he
knows nothing about are likely to be interesting.
The party in the Convention which wants to
federate the railways seems to be growing
stronger. Most of the members who have
alluded to the subject are in favor of railway
federation, while its opponents have, so far,
lain very low. It is hardly possible to make
out a decent case for the retention of the pre-
sent system, with its various gauges; its ruinous
differential rates ; its provincial system of
building lines to connect every place with the
seaport of the province, however remote it may
be, and refusing to connect with the nearest
seaport, however near it may be, if it lies in
another province; its many different manage-
ments, and different varieties of rolling-stock
necessitated by various gauges ; the consequent
needless loading and unloading at the borders,
and other clumsy expedients. It is utterly
impossible to make out anything like a de-
cent case for provincial railway management,
but possibly the provincialists will rise up like
John Brown’s soul and give a silent massive
vote when the time comes.

Three of the Premiers— Turner, Braddon,
and Kingston —definitely announce that their
visit to England will not be allowed to interfere
with the progress of the Federal movement.
Their respective Parliaments will sit in their
absence with an Acting-Premier in charge, and
consider the draft constitution, and when the
three bosses get back from their record-reign
grovel the Convention can meet again and go
on with business. Under these circumstances
the proposal mooted in N.S.W., that the Con-
vention Bill shall be hung up till Premier Reid
gets back, is absolutely inexcusable, and looks
like treachery. If this idea is adopted, N.S.W.
will be three or four or eight weeks behind
the other provinces, and they will have to wait
until it catches up ; the Enabling Bill will have
to be amended to allow of the extra delay;
then the Vic. general election will come along
before the Convention is through with its
second sitting, and all will be confusion.
“ Fluffy ” to The Bulletin :
David Syme’s victory at the Vic. Federal elections
frightened friends and foes alike. There are many Radicals
who now gird at the Age because its power is too like
despotism to be pleasant. And yet there is a big proba-
bility that the inclusion of Fat-man Fraser and Fat-man
Zeal was a masterly stroke of tactics that snatched
victory by a seeming surrender. If Fraser and Zeal
had not been included in the Age ticket, Wrixon and'
Sargood would have been elected. There is a good deal of
evidence for this statement. In the first place, there
were only seven representative Radical candidates,
namely, Turner, Deakin, Peacock, Isaacs, Tr.enwith,
Berry and Higgins. The other Radicals were Styles,
Cook,Vale and Lormer. Styles is smart enough, but he has
not yet “ arrived ” —his speeches are too statistical to be
popular, and he is practically unknown outside Trades
Hall circles. Cook is miles away from being a genius.
Vale is impracticable—and Lormer the same, only more
so. The difficulty about these four men was that they
were not class enough for a Federal Convention. But are
Fraser and Zeal class enough ? Of course not. Fraser
and Zeal got into the Convention with Age plus Argus
support. Either journal alone could not have got them
there. David Syme skilfully picked on these two as being
the least harmful of the Tory Ten, arid thereby dished the
Tories. The Argus recognises that, if it had been tac-
tician enough to drop Zeal and Fraser as soon as the
Age championed them, the Tories would not have suffered
so smashing a defeat on the Fourth of March, but it does
not dare to say so. In a luminous analysis of the elections,
Professor Nanson, of Melbourne University chair of
mathematics, practically proves the voting of the three
principal tickets to be :
Age followers 25,000
Argus followers 22,000
Trades Hall followers ... 19,000.
Higgins was on both Age and Trades’ Hall tickets, and
secured 25,000 + 19,000 votes. Fraser and Zeal were on
the Argus and Age tickets, and scored 25,000 + 22,000
votes each. Styles and Cook, with Trades’ Hall support
only, scored 19,000 votes each. Higgins, Fraser, Zeal,
Cook and Styles got practically the votes here stated
and no more. Take away the Age support from Fraser
and Zeal and give it to Styles and Cook (the highest of
non-newspaper candidates), and they would have got
34,000 votes each. This would have been quite insufficient
to place them as winners—because Wrixon and Sargood
each scored more than 42,000 votes. Wrixon and
Sargood would have been numbered with the chosen,
and Sargood would not have abandoned his trip to
Japan for nothing. It is all very well to gird at
King Dawvid, and tell stories about Zeal’s wee bit
doggie and Fraser’s yellow pup. Let the prophets,
who are wise when the numbers are up, please state
specifically how the elections could have been better
generalled. The sum of it is that the Tories had to be
represented, and instead of getting Wrixon and Sargood,
as they would have wished, they got Frazer and Zeal.
Let the galled jade wince, our withers are unwrung.
♦
Dobson, of Tasmania, brings into the Federal
Convention an atmosphere of old, crusted
Toryism that is almost refreshing. Tasmania
is the most Tory province of Australia, and
most of Tasmania seems to be the Dobson
family in one aspect or another. Social recog-
nition in Tas. consists in being invited to Mrs.
Henry Dobson’s parties ; not being recognised
by that charming lady means being in outer
darkness. The Dobsons are the great boss
fossils of the fossil isle, and naturally their
representative isn’t enthusiastic about Federa-
tion. The Dobson crowd can more or less run
Tasmania, but in a Federal Parliament they
would have to jostle with keen, eager, hawk-
like men who had risen from the ranks, and who,
with their new ideas and their haste, would
run right over the Dobsons. Therefore the
Tasmanian potentate beseeches the Convention
to go slow. When one compares the old, stolid,
earthy conservatism of Dobson with the vigorous,
up-to-date article of Richard Chaffey Baker
and Simon Fraser, it is evident that Tas-
mania’s Toryism is so far behind that it can
hardly keep up with the dust that the last
fossil in the procession of Australian fossildoni
leaves behind him as he scoots along. >
S.A. Treasurer Holder advocates that Federa-
tion shall be practically confined to defence,-
quarantine, lighthouses, and a few other trifles.
He doesn’t “see his way ” to include the debts,
because they vary so much in amount per head
of population, &c. ; or the railways, because
they don’t return anything like the same rate
of interest; or the customs, because one province
might contribute more per head than the others.
In fact, Holder’s idea is that until the Federa-
tion can start on a basis of absolute justice it
must not start at all—on which basis nothing
in this world would ever start. The S.A.
Treasurer is also against representation in the
Senate according to population because it would
swallow up the smaller provinces, and he is
against equal State representation because it
would be manifestly unjust. Holder is one of
the great crowd of learned pessimists who see
48 ways of not being able to do something, and
not one effective way of doing anything. The
idea of simply doing the thing as accurately as
possible, and saying nothing about any small,
unavoidable discrepancy, never occurs to pain-
fully accurate people like Holder.
The beautiful T. J. Byrnes, Q. Attorney-
General, now announces that Premier Nelson
and he instigated Gov. Lamington to cable
Chamberlain, when the Premier’s record-reign
invitation arrived, that Byrnes would like to
go too. Byrnes wasn’t asked ; apparently he
wasn’t wanted; and probably Chamberlain
was not even aware there was a Byrnes. But
he perforce responded with an invitation ; the
rules of hospitality didn’t leave him the option
of the North Brisbane electors. He omitted,
however, to cable what he thought of the pro-
ceeding. What does any host think of the
proceedings of a person who fishes for a welcome
at the tail of an invited guest? The secretary
of every banquet committee has to face many
such applications from the horde of self-
respectless bummers. But, in spite of Mr.
Byrnes’s rather remarkable political ante-
cedents, no one exactly expected to find him in
such an attitude.
Barton, Symon, and Glynn were the first
three Federal delegates to pronounce definitely
in favor of a Federal Court of Final Appeal.
Turner, R. E. O’Connor, and Carruthers
were the first three to rise on the other side
and whoop for the good old system, whereby
an Australian Judge is sent to England, 16,000,
miles away, to help in hearing Australian
appeals, while the plaintiff and the defendant
and the whole case toil there after him, instead
of the same man hearing the same case on the
spot. Turner, with his new British gaud on
his soul, thinks the Privy Council appeal
system should be preserved, because it is a
“ link ” binding us to the “mother country.”
Which is a fine example of loyalty at someone
else’s expense. Turner, being personally loyal,
wants a “ link,” and the litigant who is ruined,
in law expenses by his case being carried to the
Antipodes, has to pay for Turner’s link.

Further American advices report the con-
tinued success of saltbush-cultivation on the
Californian alkali plains and in other places
whither Von Mueller’s seeds have travelled.
So great was tire demand for the plant that
seeds, privately raised, were realising up to
sdols. per lb. at the end of ’95. Meantime, the
bank and mortgage co.-ridden squattages of
N.S.W., perpetually overstocked, are being
denuded of the plant, and to a still greater ex-
tent of the even more valuable cotton-bush.
These fodder-plants, covering tens of millions
of acres of State lands, constituted a national
asset of first importance. Yet the province has
allowed all that made these lands of value to
be steadily wiped out; and nowhere has any
encouragement to cultivate the plants been
given. On hundreds of square miles of Wes-
tern grazing country, rolling a few years
back in saltbush and cotton-bush, the latter is
now as extinct as the diprotodon, and the
former as scarce and dwarfed and diseased and
drought-weakened as the blacks are that camp
behind the half-filled shed during shearing and
live Heaven knows where and how the rest of
the year.
Quibble came a cropper, for a wonder, in the
Vic. Supreme Court lately, in his first serious
tilt against the Factories Act. That bite noir
of the sweater and the huckster curmudgeon
and the early morning Chow provides that
every shopkeeper shall permit his assistants to
take a half-holiday each week. “ But,” said
Quibble, “permission implies asking for per-
mission.” “And asking,” replied Common-
Sense, “spells ‘the shoot.’” But, as Law’s
blind eye is towards common-sense, it never
sees consequences, and the counter - jumpers
quaked and didn’t want half-holidays above a
whisper. “Ah! happy thought!” said the
Full Court, “ set a quibble to catch a quibble.
The Court will take judicial notice of the fact
that the assistants desire a half-holiday when-
ever they can catch one, the Act imposes on the
masters an active duty to respond to this de-
sire, and there you have your half-holiday
secure, and the tail of the peacock uninjured.”
There’s a Q.E.D. here somewhere, we are told,
but, anyway, Common-Sense gets home all
right this time.
Up to the time of writing, The Bulletin:
is pleased to note that the Federal Convention
has hardly mentioned even once that the Common-
wealth will be the brightest gem in the diadem of
Hingland, or the grandest jewel in the Ber-ritish
crown ; and Australia’s glorious destiny, and the
Greater Britain in the Pacific, and the beloved
mother-country have scarcely been alluded to.
Also, the Southern Cross is having a well-earned
rest, which that tired constellation must great]y
appreciate, and the National Anathema hasn’t
been sung once. Most of the giddy frill and beer-
froth of tiie ’9l show seem to be absent th.s time,
which is a blessed promise of better things. Aus-
tralia can’t attend to its own business and tie
itself up in convulsions of loyalty at the same
time.

The Corbett - Fitzsimmons battle will
shortly be reproduced with faithful details at
the Edison Electric Parlor, 102 Pitt-st., Sydney.
It was cabled that the great fight was recorded
all through by the Kinetoscope, and the colonial
branch of tho Edison Laboratory before the fight
took place made its arrangements for securing the
record. The Edison Vitascope, Kinetoscope, and
Kinetophone, and the wonderful X-rays are still
on show in the Parlor, and are attracting great
public interest.*
