SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “Matsa.”
Criterion “ Sweet Lavender.”
Tivoli Rickards’ Variety Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “Prisoner of Zenda.”
Royal “ A Woman’s Revenge.”
Bijou Brough Co. Farewells.
Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
Two Sydney theatres, the Royal and the
Lyceum, gave up the struggle rather suddenly at
the end of last week. Westmacott, ex-manager of
the Royal, gets an influential benefit there next
Monday afternoon (May 5) ; Bracy, the Lyceum
ex-manager, doesn’t get anything in particular.
The experience of the last two years shows clearly
that Sydney can’t keep four theatres and two
variety shows going at once ; out of the six, it
generally happens that two are utterly hopeless,
and one or two more are doing very moderate
business, while one, and only one, is booming.
Two theatres never boom violently at the same
time now ; the material isn’t available. Some-
times there isn’t any boom at all, or the semblance
of one. Whether the theatrical frost that has
been steadily settling down on Sydney for the last
five years is still getting harder, or whether it is
as hard as it is likely to be, is an unsolvable ques-
tion. Meanwhile a lot of public sympathy, and
very little public coin, goes with Bracy, whose
pieces were well-staged, well-played, and in every
way deserving of support, though somehow the
support consistently stayed away. The Royal is
expected to open again shortly under new manage-
ment, but no news of any new lessee for the
Lyceum is to hand.
* * * *
“ Matsa ” closes at the end of next week,
its last days being soothed by the presentation of
a new edition of itself. The only things which
are notably new about the new edition are some of
the songs and certain breakings-out in a fresh
place by Lauri and Little Gulliver. No one, for-
tunately, has laid a sacrilegious hand on ancient
Memphis with a view to making a new edition of
it, or has interfered with the flood in the Nile, and
Howard Vernon’s mummy is the same soul-filling
old edition as heretofore.
* * * *
“ Sweet Lavender ” is announced for next
Saturday at the Criterion. It is several years
since this pretty little play was staged in Sydney,
and since Dick Phenyl last donned his dusty
barristerial gown, and gathered up his brief, and
tried to dim the radiance of his drinky nose as
counsel for the defendant; and it is about due for
revival. “ Lavender ” is a far more artistic play
than the “ Bookmaker ” —in fact, nothing prettier
than it has been produced in recent years —and
Dick Phenyl, with his mustiness, and his fallen
fortunes, and his aroma of pessimism and beer,
is a live character alongside whom Joseph Trent
is mainly a shriek. Trent is a noble and moral
shriek certainly, but by comparison his shriekful-
ness is painfully apparent. Sydney always had a
profound reverence for “ Sweet Lavender,” who
is a loveable little girl in a neat print frock, and
she should be good for a fortnight at least.
* jfc * *
Business is respectable at the Tivoli and
very flat at the gorgeous Palace, which shuts up
on Saturday for an indefinite period pending the
arrival of new talent. The old talent, which was
quite plentiful enough to keep one house booming,
was insufficient when spread over two, and for the
last week or so the Palace co. has been rather thin
and commonplace. Godfrey has a benefit on
Saturday and departs for England, but as there
will still remain Coleman, Alice Leamar, little
Alma Grey and the Allisons, there should be
enough to keep the Tivoli alive until Rickards*
next batch of attractions begin to arrive.
* * * *
Small shows are uumerous in Sydney just
now. James M‘Mahon has another display of new
picturesathis Cineinatographe, and thenew “Cin.”
at the Crown Studios is doing well. The Royal Stan-
dard, which was practically dead for several years,
is now in possession of a dramatic co., which
slumps on the horror variety of drama—a new
horror every week, or twice a week if wanted—
and runs on the “admission by silver coin ” prin-
ciple. A small and obscure variety co. has
arisen and asserted itself at the Gaiety, another
old-time theatre long abandoned to the ghosts and
the beetles. Still another obscure variety co.
wanders round the suburbs, and raises its soul-
wail in Botany and Camperdown and other such
places. Altogether there is much variety among
the Sydney shows just now, and at least one of
them can only be adequately alluded to with road-
metal.
* # * *
The Vitascope people at the Edison
Electric Parlor (Pitt-street), announces the receipt
of a phonograph in which can be heard the voice
of Butler, the alleged Glenbrook horrorist.
Butler spoke into quite a lot of phonographs in
San Francisco ; on one occasion he spoke poetry at
an extra high price, but the poem proving not
original the showman sued him for the recovery
of the money. In the present instance Butler
speaks plain prose in his ordinary voice—the one
he is supposed to have negotiated with Lee Weller
in ; and his verbal signature at the end of the re-
marks serves as a guarantee of good faith —at least
The Bulletin presumes so.
* # * *
This being the last week in Melbourne of
Mr. and Mrs,. Brough and Go., the three farewell
performances of those admirable artists should
need no special recommendation from the press.
The Broughs have deserved well of the city where
B. and B. started business ten years ago, and
where, at the most prosperous moment of its life,
the young firm was burnt out. Also, Melbourne
in general freely acknowledges the debt, therefore
the few opportunities that remain for pouring con-
science money, so to speak, into the Bijou treasury
ought to be rushed by a tearful public. Any-
thing short of a crowded house for the last
(Thursday) night of “Mrs. Tanqueray” will
leave room for an allegation that the aforesaid
public is an ungrateful beast, and if “ Dandy
Dick ” doesn’t bring the season to a glorious finish
The Bulletin will make a point of saying “ dam”
on Robert Brough’s behalf. Meanwhile there is
every reason to hope that “ dam ” will not be
called for.
* * # *
“Two Little Vagabonds,” which might
have achieved a great success, but didn’t, is re-
tiring from Melbourne Princess’s on Friday night.
A short second innings of “ The Prisoner of
Zenda ” will open on Saturday, and play the pre-
sent company off the premises, where they will re-
appear later with “The Sign of the Cross.”
3? * * *
The Distressed Actors’ Fund takes a
matinee benefit at the Princess’s on Friday (April
2nd). The D. A.F.’s cash in hand is so nearly ex-
hausted that a series of matinee performances —in
lieu of another Dramatic Fete —will be required
to keep this excellent charity going for the next
12 months. This performance, the first of the
series, appeals to the public appetite for “astound-
ing bargains,” rather than to the uncommercial
sentiment of brotherly love. Every man who
buys a ticket receives exceptional value for his
money, and if he can derive further satisfaction
from the knowledge that almost the whole of his
little outlay goes straight into the hand of an in-
digent pro. instead of getting swallowed up by
working expenses, then the ticket-purchaser
doubles the magnitude of his entertainment
accordingly. The bill includes acts from
“A Village Priest” and “Prisoner of Zenda,”
also a new comedietta, a selection of concert items,
and turns by Chirgwin and Will Whitburn.
Finally, the Trial Scene from “Pickwick,” with
J. C. Williamson as the presiding Judge; Purves,
f).C., as Buzfuz ; Miss Florence Terriss as Sam
Weller, and a strong display of well-known
actors, and other professional people presenting
features of interest. Among these will be the
■candidate who was returned at the bottom of the
poll for the recent Federal election.
* * * *
“ The Derby Winner ” is about to quit the
Royal after a well-sustained run of 42 nights, and
the weird, peculiar laugh of the baffled adven-
turess, when the plump Gussie escapes from her
clutches, must soon fade away into silence. “ A
Woman’s Revenge ” takes the stage next Satur-
day (3rd) with the modest intention of staying for
11 nights only.
* * * *
At the Opera House matters remain about
as lively as usual, or even more so. Chirgwin’s
piebald identity is gratefully received, likewise
Bellman and Moore and My Fancy, and the pro-
gramme in general. A few astounding announce-
ments for Easter will, no doubt, be fired off as
soon as the present condition of absolute furore
and overwhelming ovation begins to cool down.
* * * *
Seeing that competition is the immortal
soul of show business, The Bulletin observes
with pleasure that Bach’s “Passion” will be per-
formed at St. Paul’s Cathedral this (Thursday)
evening, by organist Wood and a good musical
company. A curtain-raiser, in the form of a short
parsonic monologue, is to precede the oratorio,
and the audience will get at least an opportunity
to join in a chorus during the evening. Tickets
(2s. fid.) are now obtainable at the box-office, on
the distinct understanding that infants in arms
will be refused admission, for, although the King-
dom of Heaven has been likened to a little child,
the sanctuary is no place for an agonised baby
when other music is going on.
Wr * * *
A member of the St. Paul’s (Melb.)
Cathedral chapter puts in a reasonable reply to the
contention that no gate-money should be charged
when the choir gives a special performance. “ Is
anyone prepared to say that those who are willing
to subscribe to the production of this music are
solely bent on amusement, while those who wish
to hear it for nothing are only desirous of an oppor-
tunity of worship ? ” This seems a fairly honest
way of meeting the hypocritical argument that
plate-money is more religious in character than
gate-money. Experience pro vet that a large pro-
portion of the audience at a cathedral musical
entertainment (or “ act of worship ’) don t
contribute a red cent to the plate. Ane
purchaser of a half-crown ticket practically
admits the justice of the plate’s claim to
public support, and is, therefore, a more sincere
churchman, as a rule, than the non-paying wor-
shipper ” whom he elbows out of the tabernacle.
Anyway, there cannot be free music in any church.
From one source or other it is invariably paid for
as an act of worship, more or less, homebody
must provide the cost of this luxurious musical
form of prayer. “No pay, no pray,” is a fairly
good rule to apply to special cases; quite as good
as the doctrine that “ he prayeth best who payeth
least. ”
#* * *
Perth begins to consider itself no small
beer of a show-town. Geo. Jones and Lawrence,
who have made a considerable pile from an “ Olde
Englishe Fayre ” in the Western capital, are the
first lessees of Molloy’s new Theatre Royal, and
partner Jones is coming along to Melbourne to
secure talent for the opening. It is possible that
Brown-Potter and Bellew will be tempted to take
the trip. Business round the goldfields is not
very booinsotne, but the Perth showmen get big
audiences and high prices. Fitzgerald Bros., for
instance, were shocked at their own moderation in
charging only 10s. and ss. per head when they
witnessed che “unseemly rush” for seats in the
tent a few days ago.
At Poverty Point.
A young and effeminate Victorian tenor appeals thus-
wise for praise from any friend who may drop into the
artists’ room during a concert: “ Did you —er —hear my
first song? I’m—er—afraid it didn’t —er —(blush and
simper) go very well. Will you be staying for my—er—
next song? Yes! O, that is nice of you. I will ask you,
then, to kindly go round to the front— with your stick.”
The banging of only one impassioned walking-stick upon
the floor can make a vocalist very happy.
Haresfoot: “ Julius Knight was the recipient, the other
night, of a laurel-wreath from lady admirers. What noble
achievement to deserve the laurel ? Apart from this, the
flower business over men is ever a nausea. Any actor in
the last generation would have regarded it as an insult.”
According to Kalgoorlie Miner, Henri of Navarre
Brownlow is about to marry Miss Rhoda Hay, the charm-
ing Tasmanian brunette who formerly slew the world
with her smile at Heathorn’s, Hobart, and afterwards at
the Sydney Royal Hotel. Even Eastern womankind con-
ceded that Miss Hay was a beauty.
A new comedy company, with Titheradge as manager,
is spoken of as probable in succession to the Broughs. It
would be popular.
“ ‘ Dot ’ Boucicault is stage-managing for George
Edwardes, at the Garrick, Justin M’Carthy’s new comedy,
‘ His Royal Highness,’ ” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent. “ In the caste are Fred. Kaye, Aubrey
Boucicault, Blanche Massey—looking as of yore, like a
capital T in petticoats when she smiles—and Percy Lyndal,
of the starchy virgin linen. ‘Dot’ was looking for a
theatre, but evidently couldn’t find what was wanted.”
“ Australia’s Ada,” writes The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent, “ this month (Feb.) is on tour with Edward
Lloyd, Watkin Mills and Mark Hambourg in the North of
England and Scotland. Every day in the 28, excluding
Sundays, there is a concert, and Miss Crossle.v’s repertoire
consists of two songs with a possible vocalising of the in-
evitable ‘ Sunshine and Rain.’ Her ‘ notices ’ at Birming-
ham were gorgeously grandiloquent, but the unspoilable
Australydy wisely reckons by box-office criticisms only.
She is now fully engaged a year ahead.”
Harry Paulton took to the stare 36 years ago, and made
his first London appearance in 1867. But it is quite pos-
sible that Young Australia will cotton to Paulton’s
humor, which is much less ancient than Johnny Toole’s
“ funniness.”
The Epsom crowd, in Holt’s “ Derby Winner,” Melb.,
consists of 30, at a shilling a night.
Melb. : “ The Salvation Army paid £3O for the Theatre
Royal, all Sunday, and had a ram, jam and cram, but was
out of pocket through the miserable dub-up. They first
went for the Princess, but Williamson sensibly said he
would not let them have it for £150.”
The Melb. Softgoods Association had bills all over town
of a programme of 13 numbers, to be sung between the
acts of the “Little Vags.,” on their night. The stage
performers ought to sit down.
Miss Malone, Australian star of the Fisk Jubilee Singers,
with a Nagel voice, died in America a few weeks ago.
She had been a slave, and her maid was daughter of her
former owner.
Albert Whelan, of Bracy’s Opera Co., is identical with
Waxman, well-known in Melbourne for his imitations of
actors. His Tapley imitation used to take the cake in
that line of entertainment.
Capt. Jack Sutton is a great favorite wherever he goes ;
his genial manner pleases everyone. An instance of his
affability : When touring Maoriland in charge of the cow-
boys with Wirth’s circus the captain was introduced at
Nelson to an Anglican parson. The Satan-puncher was a
great admirer of horseflesh, and expressed his doubts of
the ability of Sutton’s men to tame and ride a certain
well-known “ bucker ” they were announced to tackle the
next day—he was afraid some of them would get hurt.
The captain wasn’t a bit angry at the reflection on the
skill of his men. “ Don’t you flurry your liver, old man,
don’t you flurry,” he replied. “ My boys ’ll ride any
goddam thing with skin an’ hair on, you bet.
S. : “ Critics of ‘ Two Little Vagabonds ’ miss two very
creditable efforts, all the more deserving of praise because
they are small. Atholwood, as an old innkeeper, is on
the stage for about three minutes, but the picture is
accurate and full of little clever touches. Long young
Majeroni, as a liveried flunkey, repays close attention for
a few seconds in one act. To ignore conscientious effort
in small things is to justify the pro. who slurs a part
because there is 1 nothing in it.’ ”
The popularity of “ The Sign of the Cross ” is not likely
to be helped by the sale of the novel in which its story is
told. It is a brutal thing, unredeemed by good writing.
It is dabbled with pore and bulged out with atrocity
Yet parsons have helped to boom the play in London
The clergy, however, seem to have a morbid fancy for the
bloodthirsty and the horrible example, as for example
Fitchett and the Bleed that Smudged the Empire. ’
“ In Sight of St. Paul’s,” Bland Holt’s next novelty at
Melb. Royal, includes a terrific fire. The old Royal was
burnt, the play that night having been the “ Streets of
New York,” with its fire-scene.
It is worthy of note that as soon as an English actor
receives an engagement from an Australian manager he
becomes “ the famous Mr. Buskin ” in the columns of the
Australian press, though previously unheard of at this
end.
At a concert. Cycle enthusiast (as a singer finishes a

right, Jack.”
Toole chokes Irving off Australia. Johnny is a sys-
tematic defamer of this hole.
Peart’s dive has been beaten by one Finney, who, at
London Aquarium, dives from the dome into a shallow
tank beneath the floor level, having first ignited his
clothes. This human comet is promptly extinguished on
reaching the aqua pura.
Remember-me-no-more Robinson, according to London
World, is putting the finishing touches to a new opera,
“The Nut-Brown Maid,” “which is very similar to one
that was produced in Melbourne last year.” In Melbourne,
it was called “ Predatoros” —probably because of the sug-
gestions of brigandage from other people’s music made by
the score.
Pinero’s new play, to be produced at London St.
James’s, is a five-act comedy—“ The Princess and the
Butterfly ; or, The Fantastics.”
Pro. : “ I can trace the ‘ Prisoner of Zenda ’ idea in
Madame Celeste’s ‘ House on the Bridge of Notre Dame.’
Celeste acted the Count Feraudy and Zambaro, a gipsy
vagrant boy. Two ruffians tumble the Count through a
trap, into a watery grave. In a few minutes Zambaro
turns up, and is used in personation. Marcus Clarke’s
drama, ‘ Plot,’ produced at the old Melb. Princess’s, dealt
with the Man in the Iron Mask, and had a ‘ Zenda’-like
substitution, Eloise Juno doubling the parts.”
The old-time ironbark pro. who lately joined Melb.
Salvation Army, and who slangwhanged the stage so
vigorously, has “seceded” and gone back to the foot-
lights. Religion, marrow bones and graft, and no
treasury day, were tough morsels to swallow, and not half
as nourishing as the old game, despite its many ups and
downs.
One very plain reason for Johnny Coleman’s triumph
over the broken-hearted Crackles rests in the fact that
Coleman, as an artist, is publicly modest, never appear-
ing to look for applause, but unassumingly going through
his biz. with a doorknocker face. Crackles, on the other
hand, punctuated every point with a painful wait for
gallery' demonstration.
The very latest theatrical novelty comes from Chicago,
where a manager stages a sausage-machine in full blast.
The hog appears on the stage alive and departs in the
form of sausages, distributed among the audience as
“ souvenirs.”
Irving so drills his people that they become replicas of
himself. A plucky little woman, at a rehearsal, drew
herself up, exclaiming, “ If this goes on we shall all be
Irvings 1 ”
Under Cowen’s direction, Lilian Tree lately sang with
the Halid orchestra, at Huddersfield. Every number was
encored ; and Cowen, who is very chary in congratula-
tions, was enthusiastic over Miss Tree’s success. As one
result, she found herself booked for several good engage-
ments. To an impresario who suggested Australia for a
tour in the near future Miss Tree “offered a cold refusal.”
Editor Bulletin, —I regret to inform you of the demise
of James Leo Lewis, a member of my co. for the past four
years. He succumbed to paralysis in Hongkong hospital,
7th inst., and as I can find no address of his relations in
Australia among his papers, I would like to know if his
mother, who belonged to Adelaide, or his wife, who came
from Grafton or the New England district, are still living.
His friends will be comforted to know that everything
medical science could suggest was done for him, and,
having taken charge of his affairs, I have had him buried
in the English Cemetery with customary religious rites,
and have had a handsome tombstone erected over his
grave. I have in my possession several articles of jewel-
lery and other effects which I am anxious to hand
over to his next of kin. If they will communicate with me,
enclosing satisfactory means of identification from local
authorities, with their permanent address, I will be
pleased to send them fuller particulars. Our co. sails for
Shanghai 4th March and remains until April, then Yoko-
hama. —Yours, Bert Willison, proprietor Great World
Circus.
Dear B.—Are your remarks about the “cold cheek” of
Mrs. Maesmore Morris within a mile of being fair and
reasonable? The writer cannot condemn her perform-
ance as the nurse in “The Two Little Vagabonds,” and
so frigidly discounts its merits by assuming all to be due
to arctic impudence. There might be some excuse for
this if the pretty lady had merely “ to stand motionless,
at perfect ease, upon the stage.” But she has to do more ;
she has to act and to talk, and she talks with charming
naturalness and in a voice of uncommon sweetness, and
she acts in a manner that would not discredit an actress
of talent and long experience. Because this was not to
be expected, is it fair to fish round for excuses that repre-
sent her success as discreditable on the whole? As for
the assertion that she will only do well “ as long as there
is nothing particular to be done,” it is easier to win honor
in one's own country by acting than by prophesying, I
believe. —Yours, S.

The WILD CAL
COLUMN
The scheme of the proposed A.J.S, Bank re-
arrangement is now announced, though it is not definitely
known how far it is on the road to absolute acceptance.
The institution owes to its depositors about £5,100,000 at
414 per cent, and about £1,000,000 at 3£, which makes
£6,100,000 owing and £264,500 a year interest. It is pro-
posed to subdivide these into about £1,500,000 “A”
deposits at 3 per cent, certain ; about £4,200,000 “ B ”
deposits carrying 3 per cent, certain and an additional 1
per cent, if the profits allow, the said 1 per cent, to be
due after certain provisions not yet definitely explained
are made fora reserve fund and possibly a dividend to share-
holders ; and 400,000 inscribed stock (perpetual)deposits
at 3'4j per cent, and an additional 1 per cent, if earned.
On this basis the interest could be kept down as low as
£185,000, being a saving of £79,500, or, if the profits
allowed, it would go as high as £231,000, being a saving of
only £33,500. A side-proviso, however, sets forth that
the ordinary depositors shall have the option of convert-
ing £2,000,000 of their deposits into perpetual inscribed
deposits the 3i per cent, variety. Presumably it is
mainly the “ B” deposits which will be thus convertible,
partly because there are not £2,000,000 of “ A” deposits
altogether, partly because to convert the 3 per cent, of “ A”
deposits into 3!4 and possibly 4£ per cent, inscribed
deposits would be a big sacrifice. Assuming that the
depositors grab eagerly at this privilege for the sake of
the extra interest—which seems tolerably certain—things
will pan out something like this : £1,500,000 “ A ” de-
posits at 3 per cent. ; £2,200,000 “ B ” deposits at 3 per
cent., to be raised to 4 if possible ; £2,400,000 perpetual
deposits at 3£ per cent, to be raised to 4£ if possible.
Therefore theinterest may be as low as £195,000, making
a saving of 169,500, or it may be as high as £241,000,
making a saving of only £23,500.
The largest probable saving, as above shown, is
£69,500 a year, and as the last six months for which
figures are available showed the A.J.S. to be going to the
bad at the rate of £48,200 for the half-year (£96,400 per
annum) the concession isn’t nearly large enough to
make things square. Even if the depositors did not take
up the perpetual 3£ per cent, stock the saving would be
only £79,500, and would leave the bank still going to lee-
ward at the rate of £17,000 a year. Therefore the only
hope seems to be that things may shortly improve, and
what chance there is of an early improvement only those
who know the real nature of the bank’s securi-
ties can say. Hitherto affairs have gone downhill
rather steadily. The “A ” deposits are to be
repaid between jan., 1898 and 1907 ; the “ B ” deposits are
repayable apparently at any date from 1907 to 1917 ; the
inscribed stock deposits are repayable only at the bank’s
option on six months’ notice. A further complication,
however, sets forth that the “B” deposits shall be
secured on certain of the old assets, not yet fully specified,
and whenever the proceeds of their realisation reach
£50,000 the money shall be used to partly pay off the “ B ”
crowd. It is further provided that all new business from
1893 onward shall have absolute priority, and shall be
guaranteed by all the assets of the bank, so how certain
assets are to be specially ear-marked for the security of
the “B ” lot is not yet quite clear. The first impression
is that the scheme doesn’t provide enough relief; the
second that it is lamentably complicated, like all British
reconstruction devices. Another feature is that the
British depositors, who have the smallest interest in
the concern, and who have already got a larger propor-
tion of their money back than the Australians, seem to
be making all the row and doing all the arrangement.
The British depositor has been a reliable curse ever since
’93. The only bank with no British deposits—the Colonial
of Melbourne—got a reduction to 2£ per cent, with no
trouble and no complications, but the J. Bull creditor has
a yearning for elaborate schemes with much incompre-
hensible circuitousness in them. The prospects of the
A.J.S. shareholders are difficult to estimate until more
particulars come along, but they look very moderate at
present.
It is cabled that the House of Lords has altered
the London Bank of Australia’s Reconstruction Bill to
read that the amount of interest immediately payable to
depositors may be reduced, but that the difference
between the new and the old rate shall be a debt still
owing by the bank, which must be paid before any
dividends are declared. If this is really what the House
of Lords intends, then the bank gets no relief at all, and
the reconstruction might as well be dropped. The differ-
ence between paying money which isn’t earned and
owing money which isn’t earned is not large enough to
keep any institution afloat.
* * » •
The Mutual Life Association shows a very
respectable year’s business for 1896. Renewal premiums
are £126,223, which is £3090 less than last year and £7OOO
less than in 1893. New premiums (£15,278) are £3OOO
more than last year. Interest on investments (£54,580)
is £lOOO better than in ’95, and the total income (£197,722)
is £2OOO better than that of ’95 or ’94, arid only £3OOO
worse than the total for ’93. This is about as good as
could reasonably be expected, and the improvement as
compared with last year is very marked. Out of the above
income £47,993 is added to the funds—fully 19000 more
than last year’s saving—making them £1,135,760. The
association pays away a very large amount in surrenders
(nearly 130,000 in ’96 and over £31,000 in ’95),
else the saving would be much larger. The in-
terest realised on the funds keeps up very well ;
there are practically no arrears of interest—the amount
under this heading is too small to be worth mentioning,
which speaks volumes for the safety of the investments ;
and the item, “properties acquired by foreclosure”
(£64,875) is less than it was last year, which also says a
great deal for the good management of the concern. The
securities have been written down by £10,531, making
£42,806 written off in four years. The woeful slump of
recent times makes depreciation absolutely inevitable in
the best-managed business, and the Mutual Life Associa-
tion takes the wise and honest and solvent course of
meeting the inevitable, and keeping itself square as it
goes along. Altogether, barring the expense account,
there is absolutely nothing to which exception can be
taken.
The Mutual Life Association’s expenses in ’93
were a shade over 28 per cent, of premiums. They got
down below 27 per cent, in ’95, and now they have risen
again to nearly 30 per cent. Barring only the Mutual of
Victoria, the Life Association has now the biggest expense
rate among the seven large life offices of Australasia ; and,
as the Mutual of Victoria is effecting an amalgama-
tion which will result in a very large saving, the Associa-
tion is in a fair way to have the bad pre-eminence of being
the most expensively managed office of any note in this
country. The expense rate is probably inevitable. There
is comparatively little new business to be done in these
bad times. There are seven large Australasian offices,
three small ones, and three American and one English
institution scrambling for what insurance is to be got
among only four million people. The easy terms on
which surrenders are granted makes a big drain under
this heading alone, and it is necessary to find enough
new business somewhere to counterbalance it and the
ordinary losses, so as to keep up the prestige of the
offices. Therefore, a lot of money has to be spent in get-
ting the new business. If the N.S.W. Government, when
it made life insurance compulsory among newly-appointed
civil-servants, had had the courage to make all civil-
servants insure in a new State Life Department—which
would thus have started with a big clientele, a big pre-
mium income, and no expenses to pay for procuring busi-
ness —it could have run its insurance business with an
expense rate of something like 3 per cent as against the
above-mentioned 30. And the advantage to the public of
an office where the difference between these two figures
went in bonuses instead of in ex.’s would be something
enormous.
* * * *
The Citizens’ Life Assurance Co. (Sydney)
issues its balance-sheet for ’96, and the ordinary life
branch (as distinguished from the industrial 6d.-per-week
department) seems to boom wonderfully. New premiums
are £16,897 against £16,380 for ’95, £12,593 for ’94, and
£7836 for ’93. Renewal premiums are £34,014, whereas
in ’95 they were £24,435 and in ’94, £16,098. Interest on
investments is set down at £4565, whereas in ’95 it was
only £3269, and in ’94, £2387. Out of its total income
of £55,483 the co. has saved £31,059, raising its funds to
£110,510. This result is achieved largely by keeping
the expense rate down to 15 per cent, on total in-
come, and that is apparently done by charging that
much and no more to the ordinary business, and fasten-
ing the rest on the industrial department, which,
of course, is vastly the more expensive branch. The
result is that out of over £30,000 office expenses,
apart from commission, the ordinary branch pays £4529
and the industrial, with about 2£ times the income, pays
£23,131 —over five times as much. In addition, the whole
of the dividend (£2000) and the amount written off furni-
ture are charged to the industrial. What will happen when
the ordinary branch grows so large that the industrial
can’t stand the weight is quite another thing.
The industrial branch doesn’t look quite so well.
Premium income is £129,954 —£4500 better than in ’95,
£10,()00 better than in ’94, and an increase of £22,000 on
’93. Interest and rents (£7973) and sundries bring the
ordinary income up to £138,103. Out of this very large
sum the co. saves and adds to its accumulated funds just
£3112. But a casual windfall —“ profit on realisation of
securities, £8543” brings up the total addition to
£11,655. In ’95, the industrial department saved out of
ordinary revenue £10,500 ; in ’94, £lO,OOO ; in ’93, £22,000.
Comparing these figures with the very humble £3112 of
this year, it seems that the industrial business is getting
very near to the time when all its big income will be re-
quired for claims and expenses. Out of the income of
£138,103 expenses swallow up £74,630, equal to 54 per
cent, of the entire revenue, or nearly 11s. in the £. This
is certainly not so bad as in some previous years, still it
is a heavy item. Apparently, by the way, the ordinary
branch gets somewhat the best assets, as well as the
small end of the expenses; according to the actuary’s
report its assets yielded £4 16s. 2d. per cent, interest last
, v hiip those of the industrial only panned out £4 7s.
6d. per cent.
One point which the average insurer seldom
considers is that the Citizens’ is a proprietary, not a
mutual, institution. Therefore the management rests
wholly with the shareholders; the policy-holders, who
have £275,000 in the concern, have no control, and the
shareholders, who are interested to the extent of £20,000,
run the entire business. Even if the directors borrowed
the whole of the policy-holders’ funds, there is, apparently,
no way in which the latter can interfere. At the recent
meeting an attempt was made to raise the question of
directors’ borrowings from the society, but the motion
was ruled out of order. The Bulletin hears, however,
that the amount borrowed from the Citizens’ Life Co.
on mortgage by certain directors, partly, it would appear,
on their own account and partly as trustees, is fully
£71,000. It has no evidence and has heard no
allegation that the security isn’t good enough or
that the interest isn’t paid regularly. But in the ordinary
course of human nature, if the director applies to himself
for a loan, and passes his own security as good, and
grants himself the loan, he is liable to take a biased view
of the value of the property. Also he can’t be expected
to sue himself for unpaid interest, and foreclose on him-
self, and sell himself up so enthusiastically as he
would some one else, for the sufficient reason that
no one ever kicks himself so hard as he kicks the
Other fellow. Further, the office collector or valuer or
auditor can’t be expected to take such strong measures
with his employer as he would with another man. Alto-
gether, in Tiie Bulletin’s opinion, directors should not
borrow from their own companies—least of all should
they borrow the policy-holders’ money when the policy-
holders have no control over the money—even if the
security is worth three times the loan. The transaction
may be absolutely safe and clean, but nothing will ever
make a censorious world feel sure that it looks safe and
clean. Directorial borrowings were one of the evils
which the Turner Government set itself to stamp out in
Victoria, and what the Victorians don’t know about mis-
managing companies is not worth knowing.
* * * *
Sydney Civil Service Co-operative Society
shows a half-year’s profit of £3785. Shareholders receive
10 per cent, dividend, 10 per cent, bonus on their cash
purchasers and 3% per cent, on their monthly accounts,
which absorb £3765, leaving a very small balance of £2O
to the good. Capital paid-up is £6993; reserve fund,
£11,500 ; so the position looks thoroughly sound.
* * * *
Perkins and Co. Limited —the old Queensland
brewing business of the oft wrecked Jonah Perkins, now
turned into a company—publishes a balance-sheet which
is calculated to make the big Melbourne blue-ruin men
turn green with envy. The year’s profits are £12,945. A
10 per cent dividend absorbs £6125, leaving £6820 to
carry forward. The previous balance on hand was
£18,593, so this makes a total of £25,413 accumulated pro-
fits on hand. By way of bonus, it is proposed to give
every shareholder one additional share for each four
shares he now holds, thus converting £14,583 of the
accumulated profits into capital. Considering how many
wrecked ships the original Perkins got safely ashore
from, the buoyancy of his co. is perhaps quite natural.
* * * *
One of the many mysteries which had to be
sorted out when the B.N.Z. became a Govt, concern is
that of Walter Guthrie and Co. The Guthrie concerns
apparently lived more or less on the B.N.Z. for years
past. The beginning was the firm of Guthrie and
Larnach, which was a great timber affair in the boom
days. It was floated into a limited co. which went from
bad to worse for some years, and finally closed up, and it
was alleged in some quarters that the B.N.Z. lost £lOO,OOO
altogether in the wreckage. Then Walter Guthrie and
Co. emerged, and after a time this grew to be
Guthrie and Co., Limited, and became more or less
connected with the Pine Co., the Gore Timber Co.,
the Colonial Co., the Southland Co., the Dunedin
Timber Co. and the City Saw-Milling Co. These concerns
owned a lot of each others’ shares, and owed each other
money, and drew bills on each other, and ran up another
big overdraft, or series of overdrafts, with the long-
suffering B.N.Z. When the B.N.Z would supply no more
money the inevitable winding-up set in, and seems likely,
by present appearances, to last till the crack of doom.
The present writer’s knowledge of Maoriland affairs goes
back into the seventies, and he doesn’t remember a time
when a Guthrie co. wasn’t bossing the Otago and South-
land timber-trade, and reputed to be in some trouble or
other with the B.N.Z.
The record balance-sheet of the year comes to
hand from the Assets Purchase and Realisation Co. (Mel-
bourne). It sets forth that, for the six months ended
13th February, the receipts were £3 13s. 9d. and the
expenses £4463, so the loss is £4459. The accumulated
loss two years ago was £2014 ; it is now £15,796, so the
co. seems to be having a real good time. As the paid-up
capital is £31,114, it thus appears that more than half of
it is already gone. The co. owes £16,000 to the City of
Melb. Bank and ChafTey Bros. Limited, both in liquida-
tion, so it evidently had quite a remarkable gift of putting
its money into the worst kind of investments. It has £60,000
worth of alleged property, and the balance-sheet doesn’t say
anything about one penny being received as interest or
rent, so it would almost seem as if all the investments
were up a tree together. If so, it would be interesting to
know how much the Assets Co. hopes to pay in the £.
The principal asset seems to be £40,000 worth of
city property mortgaged for £28,500. The Assets Co., by
the way, was run into its present astounding situation
under the management of some of those business men of
“ the right stamp,” who, it was said, would be scared out
of the directing business if the Government started
interfering with company management. The advisable-
ness of not scaring these valuable men out of the business
shines forth in these two items : receipts, £3 13s. 9d. ;
expenses, £4463.
The recent alleged frauds (to the tune of =£4000)
on the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society by means of
forged mortgages seem to suggest looseness in both man-
agement and auditing. Of course it is easy enough to forge
a mortgage —especially where the property is held under
Torrens’ title—but a mortgage has to be supported by
the title to the property, and it is not easy to forge that
and have it passed by the co’s solicitor. Every well-
regulated co., lending on mortgage, requires its solicitor
to certify in writing that everything is in order before the
money is paid over, and, as a further check, it is the
auditors’ clear duty to see periodically that all advances
are vouched by the production not only of the mortgage
but the title-deeds and the solicitor’s certificate. Recent
banking and other disclosures seem to show that audit by
officials nominally appointed by shareholders is more or
less a farce, and (The Bulletin says this for the thou-
sandth time) legislation is urgently required making
compulsory a yearly State audit of all banking and insur-
ance institutions.

Cabled that the English Wholesale Co-operative
Society has decided to open a branch in Sydney. What
does it mean ? A mere buying agency—or is Sydney to
have another formidable (yea, irresistible) universal pro-
videry ?
* * * *
Melbourne (writes a commercial correspondent)
is on the up-grade again. The shoals of bill-shavers and
other bummers who disappeared during the dark days of
depression are creeping back from Lord knows where to
their old haunts. Daily, faces familiar in the old days,
but which suddenly evanished, meet you unexpectedly at
Collins-street corners, and in reply to ordinary greetings
these ghosts of the past remark that they have just re-
turned to the old pitch to see how things are moving
along. Some of them fade away again, but a great many
stick to the pitch full of schemes that have millions in
’em. They never appear to hook anyone, but there they
are, up to date in dress, living on the fat of the land, with
cash in their own pockets and both hands in those of
other peop I '' whenever opportunity offers.
* * * *
“ The will of W. Pattison, one of the Mount
Morganaires, has been lodged for probate. The estate is
valued at £447,000.” Surely some mistake. W.P. was
supposed to have lost all his money years ago in buying
Mt. Morgans up to £l7 and making gigantic wagers that
they would go to £2O. He made short work of a heap of
money. One election trip to Rockhampton is said to
have cost him £20,000. All the other big holders seem to
have taken warning by Pattison, who was a good butcher
but a bad mining speculator.
* * * *
D’Arcy, the biggest shareholder in Mt. Morgan,
continues to live in great affluence in London. No
wonder. Some years ago D’Arcy was considered good
and big enough to be taken into association with the
South African millionaires such as Robinson, Barnaio,
Rhodes, and Co., and he now draws more money from his
S. African speculations than from Mt. Morgan.
* * * *
Socialist Lowry, of Townsville, recently entered
the Q.N.B. premises and demanded a reconstruction
voting paper, claiming a right to vote on the ground that
that institution was indebted to him and some 500,000
fellow Queenslanders to the extent of 3£ millions. In
case voting-right was refused Lowry demanded his £7
back as compensation. A sickly smile pervaded the
financial ruin while the argument proceeded.

A. 0.: “Half the weight of the stone is gold.”
Full weight of stone, of course, unstated. Does it weigh
a ton or a dwt. ?
* * * *
Great Boulder shareholders in Adelaide have
renewed their growfis against the London board and all
its works. The chances are the L.B. will continue to
ignore them and draw its fat fees with div. bonuses,
and, when pushed into a corner, will disclose the early
history of the float in justification of its present conduct.
* * * *
Kalgoorlie : “ All the batteries are fully sup-
plied, and water is running to waste owing to the want of
purchasers.” If the pumps at work in the water-shafts
don’t soon make a wonderful difference in the supply the
accumulation of ages in a drought-stricken country—the
place will be the water phenomenon of the world. The
soft auriferous channels of the Boulder lodes are just the
places to find water, but the chances are that, as in the
case of other dry camps, pumps and time will greatly
diminish if not entirely exhaust it.
* * * *
“ Phenomenally rich stone has been struck in the
Fortune-of-War, W.A. It is the best yet taken out of the
mine.” Not too soon, for in the meanwhile 17 calls have
been taken out of the shareholders. Then comes the next
wire : “ Have holed through the rise. Only a few cwts. of
rich ore.” Thus endeth another W.A. pocket.
* * * *
A start has been made to re-open the copper and
lead mines of Northampton, Westralia. They were
worked over 30 years ago, and the first W.A. railway was
built to connect them with the coast. An early WA.
Governor declared that the production of copper and lead
would be the staple industry of the country. The mines
went down with the price of those metals, and the district
changed from the leading mining to the chief pastoral
region of the province. This scheme is apart from
Brookman’s, to work copper and erect smelting-works in
W.A. Whatever the result, there is bound to be more
discrimination than in the gold-boom, and much less rob-
bery and money wasted on “cats.” It is hard to salt a
copper-mine, and impossible to float one on rich copper
specimens. * * *
The amazing richness of the discovery made in
the Golden Ridge mine, W.A., is indicated by the share
market-sales, 9d., Bd., 7d. “ Put not your faith in
news wires from Westralia,” is engraven on public
memory. % «
From one of the big managers of one of the big
mines at Hannan’s to The Bulletin : “ Our camp is
developing wonderfully well, every drift we drive and
sink we make developes rich ore. The Boulder is a great
success on the 300 level. They have a west lode which is
about 7ft. wide. The ore next the walls is a schist, while
the centre of the reef is a blue quartz. The schist will
average 3£oz., and the blue quartz 7oz. per ton. The sul-
phides are rich, but there is a high percentage of free
gold. The fever is very bad here, and the country is
overrun with labor. Do what you can to keep men away,
for there is really nothing for fresh hands to do. The
producing mines as well as all leases with any prospect
are full-handed. By the death of ‘ cats ’ many hundreds
of men have been thrown out, and other 1 cats ’ are dying
daily with the same result.”
• * » *
The silence of a graveyard is not in it compared
to the quietude surrounding the Maritana mine at Han-
nan’s. Surely its great richness cannot have petered, and
yet the management would hardly turn out thousands of
ounces of gold in the dark. That would not be right in
these days of mining freedom and enlightenment! The
writer has much pleasure in reminding the public of the
good things said of this mine a few months ago. “Care-
ful inspection leaves no doubt that the Maritana has
struck the main lode of the Great Boulder, and has get it
richer than the Boulder itself.” Nothing is yet known of
the size of the lode in the Maritana, as only 3>4ft. has
been driven, but there is no doubt the whole formation
will average over 20ft. and the prospects average over soz.
to the ton.” . . . “It is now considered certain that
all the mines on the line must get the Boulder lode, while
the green lode of the Croesus is pronounced to be the
main reef of the district.” There are columns of this
sort. Said of Bravo Jones, who was one of the first
directors, that being asked if he had suffered on his Wes-
tralian trip he replied, “ No —only from the shock of see-
ing the Maritana mine.”
Westralian mining prospectuses, asked for by
the writer recently, are coming to hand, and they show
such an awful gulf between anticipation and realisation
that many of the W.A. experts must wish them out of
print or destroyed. Sooner or later these things must
rise in judgment against them. One at a time : Hannan’s
Napier, once called Maritana South. Bull put up £20,000
working capital for one-fifth of the co. on the strength of
reports and assays. “One of the ironstone specimens
from the Napier showing no visible gold was assayed by
Johnson Matt.hey for 128 oz. 16dwt. to the ton.” One
expert : “ I have not seen the equal to the Napier in the
district for the number of r'eefs and richness.” Another :
“ A great quantity of gold was taken from this reef esti-
mated by the prospectors to be about £l5OO worth.” A
third : “There are 10 reefs all carrying gold.” What has
become of the mine ?

» * * •
Ivanhoe, W.A., month’s yield, 952 tons, 21920 z.
Lode cut at the 300 ft. level. It is under 2ft. thick, and,
so far, only samples about an oz. to the ton. It is ex-
plained that, as in the case of the Boulder, some driving
has to be done before the lugger and higher grade ore
body is met with. Also, that at this level the ore is a
little better than it was at the same point above.
* * * *
Premier Forrest, at Bunbury : “Don’t let any-
one forget that we are crossing the stream, and that this
is a very anxious time in the history of W.A. and for those
in power. There has been a great deal of booming in our
mines, and in some cases the investors had got no return,
while in others they have received ample reward.
Development must take the place of booming. No
country can go on living on booming.” All hope of the
new boom coming early this year is abandoned ; prayers
for a new boom are mingled with curses on account of
the boom that has passed away. Zeb Lane attributes the
“slump” to the war scare. Lane is no fool and knows
better. Great is the name of Lane—because of the acci-
dental greatness of the Boulder group of mines.
* * • *
The public are tired of reading about the rich-
ness of the Bletchington mine, near Orange, N.S.W. Let
the battery do some talking on, say, 1000'tons.
* * * •
N.S.W. Public Service Board has made sub-
stantial additions to the first-fixed salaries of the out-
door officers of the Mines Dept. Chief-inspector Slee
goes up £9O to £650 ; J. Hebbard, the inspector at Broken
Hill, goes up £lO5 to £350 ; the other inspectors, Milne
and Godfrey, £3O each, making the pay £275. The pay
now may be good enough for the officers ; but it is none
too much for the offices. The opinion of a man drawing
£245, or even £350, a year could hardly be expected to
be quite convincing to the thousand and two-thousand-a-
year B.H. managers. And in the present state of the
N.S.W. mining regulations, several of the most important
of which have been judicially declared to be rubbish, the
inspectors have to depend almost entirely for their
influence with the managers upon the weight of their
individual opinions.
* * * •
From London to The Bulletin : —“ The big
monthly yield from the Gibraltar has attracted attention
here. Shares are in demand, and they will, I think, go
higher if the market is led to believe that anything like
3000 oz. a month can be kept up. It will also help along
N.S.W. mines owned in London, and cause others to be
sold.” .The general impression has been that the mine was
not nearly big enough to keep up such an output, that
the vein was so small and the ground so hard it was only
a matter of a few months when the mill would have to
wait on the mine for ore. It would seem, however, that
other veins in other mines acquired by the co. have been
developed and are likely to furnish large quantities of
stone, that the Gibraltar vein itself has been proved of
greater length than formerly worked, and altogether the
future of it is bright. Owing to the complete failure of a
boring-plant and the great difference in the cost of hand-
work where all the power is supplied by water develop-
ments are a little behind, and consequently a smaller
output for two or three months will soon have to be
faced, but a regular and continuous output and yield are
promised. Certainly the co. for its pluck deserves it all.
Northern mining men are dropping off quickly.
Died the other day at Rockhampton Harry Trenear, at
one time principal chemist and assayer to the Mt. Morgan
Gold-mining Co., and “brains” to the managing figure-
head. Trenear was subsequently for a time in charge of
the laboratory at gorgeous Taranganba, and afterwards
employed at various outside ventures. A quiet, modest,
unassuming man, all brains and no bombast.

The appointment of J. Harcourt Smith, assayer
of the Western S.M. Co., as Tas. Government Geologist
in place of A. Montgomery, the Mount Huxley under-
taker, came as a surprise. Mr. Smith is a brother-in-law
to one of the Barclays, of the Commercial Bank, and in
that cocoanut there may possibly be some milk.

SOCIETY
Chief-Secretary Chamberlain is treating
the Australian Premiers rather shabbily on their
visit to London. He is lodging them in a hotel.
Now, when old Li Hung Chang visited London
he was given the use of a mansion in Carlton
House Terrace. When the son of the Ameer of
Afghanistan was there last year he, too, was given
a mansion, with servants, &c. Surely an Aus-
tralian Premier is as important a person as a dog-
eating, pig-tailed Chow Viceroy, or a harem-keep-
ing young savage from Central Asia. Any two-
penny-halfpenny German princeling would have
rooms in one of the Palaces around_ London—St.
James’ Palace, Hampton Court—or in one of the
. numerous official residences. But an Australian
Premier is made the “guest of the nation” in a
pub.—which pub., by the way, was mostly built by
Jabez Balfour, Esq. Perhaps Chamberlain is
under the impression that the Melbourne boomsters
are still in office.

According to E. 0. Lobb 50 per cent, of

the Europeans in New Guinea die in the first year
or so. That joyous land revels in 300 wet
days out of 365 per annum, and the normal
heat stands at 90 degrees. Forty men went
with Lobb to Duan in ’BB—of whom 11 are now
alive. No less than 60 Europeans lie buried
at Sud-Est, although no more than 160 men ever
visited the place. If a vessel lie a week, along the
coast fever is bound to attack it. Lobb asserts
there is only one possible policy, viz., wait till the
tracks are opened up. In a word, let the other
fellow die first.
* * * *
A little story is going round the Northern
papers re proceedings of certain New Guinea
Inspectors who wanted carriers from a native
chief who was not disposed to be friendly. The
Papuan, after some barneying, directed his men to
plant a cocoa-nut on a stump and then stood his
best marksmen off at a distance and bade them
shoot the cocoa-nut full of arrows, which they im-
mediately did. Spearmen then transfixed the
nut with spears in featly fashion. Whereupon
the boss sharpshooter among the whites placed a
salmon-tin on the cocoa-nut and shot a hole
through it from a distance of 200 yards. A
friendly chief, however, brought the parties to
reason and peaceful arbitration.
* * * »
In a Westralian town a lately-imported
potentate, who at once dropped into the magis-
tracy, has taken possession of the most fascinating
local daughter of Aspasia. The other night there
were loud knocks at Miss Aspasia’s door. Game
the stentorian voice of Justice from within :
“ Haw ! Go from that —haw—door at once, or I
shall have you brought befaw me in the morning ”!
Not quite a drawing-room anecdote, but it puts
the Westralian bureaucratic system in a—haw-
nutshell, doncherknow.
* * * *
Justice Simpson in a last-week Sydney
divorce-case—
He was of opinion that clearly adultery had been com-
mitted by the wife. At the same time the husband had
conduced to the adultery by his cruelty and negleot;
nevertheless, that adultery prevented the wife obtaining
the relief she asked for. Both had committed faults
against the marriage contract, and the law was clear on
the matter. Neither could obtain any relief.
O, admirable law ! If one party is proved
thoroughly unfit to be united in marriage, a
divorce is granted ; if both parties are thoroughly
unfit, they must drag their chain until the end.
One crime dissolves the marriage bond, two crimes
tighten it! What logic ! what wisdom !
* * * *
Canon Potter, of St. Kilda, Vic., a Tory
parson, is shocked at the idea of Chow laundry-
men being fined for working after hours, more
especially, he says, as there is much in common
between his work and theirs, Cleanliness being
next to Godliness. Alas ! for the people who owe
their cleanliness to a Chow, and their Godliness to
Potter. Some parsons, in striving to make their
parishioners fit to die, only succeed in making
them unfit to live.

Hospital war being removed to London. Einlay-

is pretty sure to go.
A photo, of Miss
Marie Elster first ap-
peared in The Bulle-
tin a good while ago,
when that lady’s vocal
excellence and oper-
atic possibilities were
not generally recog-
nised by Australian
critics. Up to that
time Marie Elster’s
appearances in grand
opera parts had been
confined to an unlucky
Westralian season,
and barring one or two
short engagements in
Melbourne she got no
chances to turn her
notes into gold on this
side of the world.
Early in ’95 Miss
Elster went ’Ome, a
buxom matron, with
a physique of the
genuine prima-donna
type and a beautiful,
well-trained soprano
voice which Jwas still
young enough to grow
stronger. Also, the
enterprising artist had
much brains and a
limited knowledge of
stage business. . After
arriving in Great Bri-
tain she fell ill, and
lost some time in
recovering her health
before she could make
a fair start as the new
Australian prima-
donna of Neilson’s
Opera Company. Miss
Elster was an immedi-
ate provincial success
on the charm of her
singing. Presently she
began to score as an
actress likewise, in
“Eaust,” “IlTrova-
tore,” and English
opera, and two months back she made her
London debut as Santuzza in “Cavalleria
Kusticana. Ada Crossley, who was present
on this occasion, reports that her friend from the
Antipodes was voted “ a magnificent Santuzza ”
by certain magnates in the operatic world. A
Covent Garden engagement for Miss Elster has
been runted at, and her name, already made, seems
certain to expand into a “great name’’ within
the next few months.
* * * *
Some months ago, a schoolmaster opened
a private school in a N.S. W. 'country district. His
pupils’ fees soon got in arrears. Verbal and
written requests to their parents for money being
of no avail, he one day locked the scholars up and
sent a boy to their parents with the message that
Jim and Lucy were impounded, and would be
kept in pound until released by payment of amount
owing for school-fees.” The last of the nine im-
pounded urchins was released at half-past ten that
night, and two days afterwards The Bulletin
informant met the schoolmaster, with his drum
up, making for Mulga town at the rate of four
knots: an hour.

At Melb. University annual commence-
ment the other day nine women were made
Bachelors of Arts and four Masters of Arts ; like-
wise, one was capped Master of Science. Three of
the six matriculation exhibitions and all three
entrance exhibitions in musical subjects fell to
girls ; likewise two Arts exhibitions, all three
music ditto, and one in medicine. Three final
honor scholarships in Arts, two Wyselaskie
scholarships (big cash prizes running into three
figures), and the Cobden Club medal also went to
the stockinged sex.

Melb. commercial-travellers held a male
beauty-show at their picnic, the other day, and
seven lady-judges plunged for a beautiful drum-
mer named Chapman, who has supplied the illus-
trated weeklies with pictures of his lovely face
and is flourishing as a society beauty in the space
usually devoted to a classically-draped barmaid
with a limp lily and consumptive expression.
True, beauty is only skin-deep, but in a drummer
that depth is considerable depth.

N.S.W. Public Service Board recently
advertised for a shorthand-and-typewriter clerk.
There were over 50 applicants, including four well-
known newspaper reporters, also a man holding
the Victorian law courts’ reporting certificate.
The fact that it is worth the while of such well-
qualified persons to go after a £3 a week billet
casts lurid light on the oft-repeated statement of
shorthand teachers and typewriter importers that
there is an unlimited demand for competent
operators.
* * * *
A fact as strange as Bret Harte’s fiction.
A true tale from Eastern Gippsland. A store-
keeper in a small township between
Bruthenand Omeo “ packed ” some
goods out to a miner’s hut on the
Gibbo Range, and found on arriv-
ing there that the miner was away
somewhere in the surrounding deso-
lation on a prospecting tour, and
that his wife was in bed, and a
little stranger was quite imminent.
Necessity knows no law of con-
ventionality. The storekeeper
“Sairey-Gamped ” the little stran-
ger, and both are doing well, but
that grocery man has acquired a
nickname that he will never out-
grow, and finds life not worth living
on account of being knocked up at ungodly hours
by bucolic jokers, who inform him with much ap-
parent excitement that his services are in urgent
request down at Mrs. Blank’s on the flat or up
the creek at old Whatshisname’s.
* * * *
In a Sydney court, the other day, a bank-
rupt was asked how he had lived “ in luxurious
style on =£3 a week. To which he replied, with
conscious pride, that that ‘ ‘ was a problem to
which he had devoted considerable time in the
interests of social economy, and the results of his
numble efforts were now before the court! ”
* * * •
How they die in the bush. On Boatman
Station (Q.), lately, four hapless swaggies ran short
ot water on the track. A Hindoo came along on
horseback at whom the four unfortunates made a
run to beg for water. The Hindoo, fearing they
were mad, galloped off, and a few days later four
corpses were discovered by the wayside.


Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had post free for 1/3. Senior,
chemist, George-street, Sydney.*

Rudall, the man who is conducting the
search for the two lost explorers, reports, re the
pair of skeletons which he found in the desert:
“ I remarked to Trottman at the time that one
man had large feet.” That is practically the
whole epitaph and obituary of the pair. No one
knows who they were, or when they died, or what
they died of. There isn’t even a recollection of
two men having dropped out of notice anywhere,
or a theory as to why they went out into these
regions. All that can be said is that two men
sometime or other left their bones out there, and
one of them had large feet. The other one doesn’t
even get that scant allowance of personal obituary.
He is merely the man who was with the man that
had large feet. It is impossible to die much more
indefinitely than that, even in this Great Disap-
pearance Land. Name, age, birth-place, date and
cause of death all lost, and not even a personal
characteristic to hang as a tag on to his bones.
Simply the dead unknown who was found along
with the unknown who had bigger feet than usual.
$ * » »
The funniest thing in connection with
the late monster meeting of the Salvation
Army in Melbourne passed without com-
ment. When Panjandrum Booth handed the
£ 1000 Indian famine-fund cheque to Mrs. Booth
he requested the immense audience—over 3000—
to smile so that the photographers on the platform
might be able to snatch a flashlight picture of one
enormous grin of benevolence. They smiled- -the
whole 3000 of them—simultaneously. Now the
average ‘Army’ mouth —distended as it is by
much hallelujahing and ‘ volleying ’ —must be at
least 4in. wide when at full stretch. Therefore,
if these 3000 smiles were placed in a row they
would make one vast, appalling grin 300 yards
long—a chasm of affability which could have
demolished a corned - beef sandwich weighing
nearly 7cwt.—at an ounce to the inch —in one
terrific bite ! What a blood-curdling sight for a
caterer !
* * * •
Many of the Methodist parsons of Australia
are playing on a small scale the part which some
people expect Australian Parliaments to play on a
large. A large popular vote demands the union
of the Methodist churches in all the provinces, but
the parsons, fearing loss of prestige and influence,
are doing their best to nullify their congregations’
wishes.
* * * *
Gov. .Buxton mortally offended tlie pro-
prietors of the Adelaide dailies by omitting them
from the invitation-list of the garden-party given
to the Federal delegates, although he included the
visiting pressmen. Result: Only an inch of refer-
ence to the function in next day’s papers.

Ovide Musin’s company
of musicians visits Sydney
for the third time (for the
principals) during Easter
week; and instead of

Friday, the Town Hall will
echo cheerful concert strains.
Musin is a manly musician
whose hair is cropped com-
paratively short, and whose
only airs are those bracketed
with his name on the pro-
grammes, He says he is a
Belgian, but the rollicking
way wid him justifies his
friends’ suspicion that he
really hails from Ballyhooley.
On this occasion, besides
Mine. Musin and her smiles
and her voice, and Mons.
Violon, and his collection, of
stamps, Musin brings with
him that trusty pianist,
Eduard Scharf, and Alfred
Hill, the young Maoriland
violinist, composer, and con-
ductor. Baritone Burns
Walker will specially assist
on Good Friday. But it is
on Miss Constance Hat-
herly that perhaps the keenest
hopes centre. Miss Con-
stance, who is a kind of
Crichton in petticoats, being
a chess-player of weight and
a swimmer of amazing
mettle, has already, though
young, won distinction as a
harper.- She is a medallist
of the Lond. Academy, and
was pupil to tb s great
Oberthiir—than vhom no
harper has been more cele-
brated since David himself.
Miss Hatherly is said to
have added something of his
magic to her own, and it
is confidently hoped that
Sydney audiences will greet
her not only with pleasure but with enthusiasm.

Though the Victorian Government is a not
over-merciful crowd, young Phillips will probably
not be hanged, in view, among other things, of
the fact that the jury said, in answer to the Judge,
that they believed t\p bought the pistol to send to
his brother, and that he did not take it to Cooper s
office in order-to shoot Brady. To hang a youth
of 19 years in .face of such a finding would be
utterly at variance with the strong and ever-grow-
ing public sentiment that capital punishment,
where inflicted at ail, should only be awarded for
murder with malice aforethought in the lay sense
of the term.
To hang the condemned youth Phillips
Vic. Govt, would have to get a fresh apd competent
executioner, the old ghoul, Pauling, having dis-
appeared months ago. It’s hard to get hold of a
hangman who really knows the ropes, and Pauling
was a perfect windfall when he assumed office.
The previous strangler, Jones, cut his throat in an
attack of d.t’s on the day before Mrs. Knorr, the

whole of the officials were in a quandary a wire
came from Pauling (who had previously acted as
“assistant ” at two or three functions of the kind)
applying for the hideous billet.

“London, March 20.—A report of the
Indian Department Committee, which has been
published, discloses an appalling condition of the
troops of the Indian army. A proportion of 536
men per 1000 is shown to be suffering from con-
tagious disease.” Only 464 men per 1000 free from
the most destructive and insidious of all diseases.
What an army to lead against a healthy enemy !
This is portion of the bill England has to pay for
the retention of India; also, it is no doubt in
great part the sweet result of the repeal of the
Cantonments Acts at the instance of holy noodle”
dom.

The other day, at Singleton (N.S.W.), two
sheep-stealers were sentenced to three months’
gaol. How does this compare with Judge Gibson’s
monstrous sentence of three years for stealing a tin
of baking-powder, value about ninepence ? Is there
any sense of proportion among N.S.W. Judges, or
does each Judge go entirely by his liver ’
S.M. Herald takes Justice Williams (Vic.)
to task for giving two years (with periods of “ soli-
tary ”) to an oft-convicted petty larcenist who had
again come up for sentence. “ There is something
shockingly inhuman in a sentence of two years
hard labor, and the solitary confinement amounts
to barbarity,” says the Herald— which, however
is strangely silent re an apparently far more cruel
case at its own front door —the above - quoted
ninepenny in which N.S.W. Judge Gibson, the
other day, gave three years (involving nine months
“ solitary ” !).
* * * *
A Leichhardt larrikin was fined £3 or two
months’ gaol by a Sydney magistrate a few days
ago. A little before that another magistrate m a
Sydney court fined an illicit-still worker £3OO or
six months’ gaol. If the larrikin’s cash basis was
all right it should have been 200 months, or over
16 years for the still-worker. Or, the other way
about : if the latter’s fine bore a fair proportion to
his term of imprisonment it should either have
been £3 or If days, or else £lOO or six months for
the larrikin. As magistrates persist in these dis-
proportions, why does not Parliament take the
scandal in hand ?

From S.M. Herald:
INUNDATION OF THE NILE.
Forty Miles Wide at Memphis.—Great Damage to
Property.
LONDON, March 22.
The River Nile has rapidly overflowed its banks, and
the country is inundated. At Memphis the river is 40
miles wide. Great damage has been done to property.
Fifty persons have been drowned.
The big Webb-Leaver (Sydney) slander
case shows how entirely the public is at the mercy
of the daily press. The Herald and Telegraph
versions of the trial give totally different impres-
sions of the evidence, the parties, and the merits.
Some evidence: —
Mr. Heydon : Were these the only two occasions on
which you searched your husband’s pockets ?
Witness : I think those were the only occasions. •
Mr. Heydon : Are you sure ?
Witness: Well, when I was at all suspicious I always
searched Mr. Webb’s pookets.
Mr. Heydon : Was it not hundreds of times ?
Witness : No, it was not.
Mr. Heydon : Were you not suspicious-many; times ?
Witness : I searched many times, and I did not find
Mr. Heydon : How many times did you find letters ?
Witness :On three occasions. I searched every time I
was suspicious. . , „
Mr. Heydon : What did you do with the third letter?
Witness: It was taken from me before I had time to
securt it. Mr. Webb was writing in the office and I
watched him, and read it when he was out of the office,
but I had no time to take a copy before he returned.
Mr. Heydon : Then that is the way you used to compass
him round with sweet observances?
Witness : Yes.
THE STORY OF THE FLOOD.
Grandmamma went to the play,
(Grandmamma is ancient very)
There she saw in bright array
Memphis (now a cemetery).
Poor old Granny ! Poor old dame !
Thought that “ Matsa ” was no fiction,
So when the cable message came
She told her lore in choicest diction.
Memphis on the Nile (a dream)
Was to her still bright and chippy,
And she knew not, it would seem,
Memphis on the Mississippi.
Who can blame her, for it may
Once have happened as she stated ;
Granny’s merely (that’s her way)
Some six thousand years belated.
ZEF.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.
Lenkowe: As Judge Gibson appears to dis-
tinctly admit that the sentence (three years for stealing a
tin of baking-powder !) was awarded partly because of the
theft—which was the only thing he had any right to deal
with —and partly because of the £l9 9s. which the country
is out of pocket, by reason of the fact that the accused
refused to let the Police-court deal with him, and insisted
on being remanded to quarter sessions; also, partly
because of the worry of trying the prisoner at all; his
Honor should be requested by the authorities to say
exactly how much extra gaol was put on by reason of the
two latter considerations, and that part of the sentence
should be remitted. Also, he should be advised of the
unwisdom of opening his mouth to give reasons and
putting both feet in it; and recommended to keep his
temper. Just think of it—three years in gaol (including
nine months’ solitary confinement) for stealing nine-
penn’orth or so of soda-and-acid ! Lloyd J. : Now, do

The B. : Will print “Har.” E.O. : “ Svengali Syme
and Trilby Victoria ” sounds well, and is pretty nearly,
but not quite, true Densmore : The Bulletin has
opposed, and always will oppose, flogging because, as it
has said five hundred times, the lash is a torture intended
only for the man in workman’s garb. We challenge you,
•or anyone else, to give us one solitary instance during the
last 50 years in which a young blackguard of “society”
parents has been tied up and flogged. We challenge you
to name one instance in which a sexual-offender of the
broadcloth-and-belltopper order has been lashed—though
there have been hundreds of such offenders bowled out,
and we have seen several of them behind the spikes with
our own eyes. There is no magistrate who dare lash a
young man with a society mother and nice, scented
Government-House-list sisters; whereas any brute of a
beak can safely lash a poor fisherman—never, it would ap-
pear, previously before court in all his life for any offence
—who had been guilty of not altogether voluntary expo-
sure while drunk on board a harbor steamer. Yes, that
fisherman was the very first person flogged under the new
N.S.W. lashing regulations.
