A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 30,1897.
Mr Dear Myee—
The Centennial Hall was nearly, but not quite,
filled on Saturday afternoon by people who paid
for admission to Miss Constance Evelyn’s farewell
sing-song, and as all the artists filled the bill
gratuitously, Miss Evelyn will have a substantial
residuum of profit to take her to the under-world.
The front-seaters were all in elaborate attire.
Women dress more for a Town Hall concert than
for the theatre in Sydney, because in the large
spaces of the vestibules and corridors their glories
are more gazed upon ; in Melb. it is the other way
round. The Princess foyer sets off a graeefully-
godetted skirt, but Town Hall aisles are crushing.
The draw of the concert was the new baritone,
Mr. Burns-Walker, a Byronie youth whose photos,
have been beautifying George-street lately. He
knows how to sing, and was rapturously received.
The women love him —his baritone raised in pas-
sionate Italian ditties makes ’em cry, and he threa-
tens to take the place of the lamented Ryley. Miss
Evelyn, in yellow silk with lace and fur adornings,
sang “ Che faro,’ and the inevitable “ Sunshine
and Rain,” and bowed over many bouquets.
Everybody was encored—the performance conse-
quently lingered on until nearly 11.
While dailies continue to keep gushing over us
as a distinctly musical community, I observe that
the distinctly musical units who help to make up
the community continue to keep on departing
from us. Every week one or two aspiring song-
birds or instrumentalists sail away to get a musical
education, which education, when achieved, they
carefully keep away with. Past week Yvonne
Leverrier went away in the German liner with
her family to visit her ancestral land of Brittany;
and Nellie Young, leaving by same boat with her
bridegroom, Wiedermann, was adorned, ere de-
parting, with a golden lyre on behalf of Sydney
Liedertafel.
While the vessel was tied up at the Quay the
Barbarossa band used to play selections every
afternoon on deck, to the delight of passengers by
the North Shore ferry-boats, always ending up
with “ God-save ” as an attention to Grandma ; if
the “Watch on the Rhine” was played, it wan-
dered into the programme incidentally. The ter-
rifying notice to beware of the screw, painted on
the enormous stern, wasn’t translated as it was on
the Friedrich. To be chopped up by a twin screw
would be bad enough, but it would add to the
horror of the situation to be minced by a “doppel-
schraube.”
Some new plums were inserted into the “Matsa”
hotch-potch on Saturday. Florence Young
dashea through Royle and Caron’s “Federation ”
song with spirit and the help of chorus, waving
the Australian flag ; but it suited J ules Simonsen’s
manly tenor better when sung by him a fortnight
ago without orchestral aid. Little Gulliver and
Lauri burlesqued the topical song of panto, by
merely naming the subjects and saying nothing
about them, a profound method of treatment
which caught on. A coon song was sung by Carrie
Moore and Ivy Scott in verandah hats and striped
cotton clothes. The d.c. was crammed with
juveniles for the matinee. Two or three curled
darlings were got up in Fauntleroy style, and
several fairies of about five or six looked sus-
piciously aureolined as to the hair. But, as a
rule, the Australian mother can’t be accused of
overdressing her cherubs. She sensibly ties a
large, floppy hat on her daughter’s curls and en-
cases her in a print or muslin “ Mother Hubbard,”
and rigs her boys out comfortably in “Jack Tars.”
Queen Victoria’s benevolence to the extent of
■£lo has been awarded an aged couple named
O’Neill at Port Hacking. For years the old lady,
whose first husband was cook in the Royal kitchen
at Windsor in Uncle William IV.’s time, has
retailed scraps of antique reminiscences to tourists,
and a kindly visitor seeing the destitution of the
pair—the old man is an ex-soldier, short-serviee-
without-pension man—interested himself, and was
the means of getting the royal bounty forwarded.
A good opportunity this for the “ loyal ” press to
have the recipients of H.M.’s gift photographed
for publication. The cheque might also be repro-
duced pictorially and gazed at re vertxitially by
adoring subscribers.
Church millinery is the cause of many ructions
and much ill-feeling between the lately-departed
Bishop Smith and some of his clergy, who consider
the Primate’s view on the width of a chasuble’s
hem to be dangerously heretical. The “high”
contingent, who like a fancy ceremony, pay their
pew-rent to see the parson proceshing around the
church every Sunday, hands pressed together and
thumbs up and a piece of needlework pinned to
his coat behind, and feel swindled if the rites are
not conducted on those lines; whereas “ Mary
Ann Smith,” as the late Canon Kemmis used to
call him, is grovellingly “ low,” and the contempt
felt for his ideas of running a service by the brass-
candlestick-and-cope-and-all party is more bitter
than the hatred of the Greek Christian for Islam.
At a certain city theatre, one night last week,
pros, played to a d.c. numbering six ; at another
show, suburban, only four had handed in the neces-
sary silver coin at opening time. Actors now
swap stories about record poor houses. Up to date,
nobody has gone one better than an experience of
the Dan. Barry co. Showing one summer evening
in a remote town they waited until considerably
after opening time for some audience, but in vain.
At last in the dusk of twilight figures were seen
coming along the dusty road. The co. rushed
behind and prepared to ring up, but the “ audi-
ence ” scampered hurriedly past the door. It was
a mob of horses.
E. Fitts will exercise his fine voice in “ I Love
but Thee ” till the end of “ Matsa ” season, when
he will close his own mouth professionally and
make other people open theirs. He returns to
tooth-doctoring, and will probably give Collins-
street a treat by letting that moustache grow
again.
Rose Musgrove, second daughter of her father,
has at last succeeded in removing the parental em-
bargo against her playing in public; she will
appear with the fashionable amateurs who are to
occupy the Princess’s soon in “Le’lamine.” About
two years ago Rose made a brilliant success as
The Gutta Percha Girl in the “ Arabian Nights,”
played in Prahran Town Hall for local charities.
A.fter two performances the laudatory notices of
his daughter’s song-and-dance accomplishments
seemed to annoy G.M., for he forbade her to play
any more.
Summer tourists to Tasmania generally return
with the two sassiety conversational plaints of the
islanders —the wild jealousy that exists between
the two cities about Govt. House location, causing
Launceston to agitate for the building of a vice-
regal palace for itself, and the manlessness of all
toney gatherings. The much-rushed visiting
youths are given such an overwhelming dose of
attention by Tasmanian belles that they feel like
Alexander Selkirk, and murmur * * They are so
unaccustomed to man their tameness is shocking
to me.”
A recent M.L. paper publishes an advt. from
the very newest woman, calling upon her husband
to remove his goods and chattels from her house,
threatening, otherwise, to auction them for storage
expenses. Name and address appended. A re-
freshing change from the old-time agony advt.,
imploring the absent one to return home to his
heart-broken Susie.
A servant-gal-worried Sydney matron has solved
the difficulty of hired help by employing “ real
lydies ” in reduced circumstances as domestics.
She declares that being “missis” to one’s equals
is a real pleasure, and finds comfort with a large
house-staff for the first time.
In Westralia, what with Afghans running amok,
Hindoo menials at a big Perth hotel trying to do
for the proprietor, and Chow club-servants revolt-
ing, the white inhabitants have no time to re-
member to be thankful for alien hired help. The
Weld Club, which is Perth sassiety, has been
lately much troubled by a very literal sort of strike,
in which the chief-steward was bumped hard on
the head among the Chinkey under-hirelings, the
chief cause of complaint being “too muchee
work.”
Despite all the writing-down it has had, the
theatre-hat continues to be the same pompous and
aggressive head-piece as ever. It is most likely
puffed up because about the first time it was
noticed was when Heine sat behind the rose-red
gauze hat in the French theatre, and all the horror
of the tragedy appeared to him bathed in the
most cheerful rose-light. Millinery that has got
itself into literature won’t condescend to take any
notice of mere newspaper abuse.
It is the Newcastle ? not the Sydney School of
Arts, which is proposing to turn its lady readers
out of their room and introduce billiards instead ;
but now members have voted to have the click of
the cues heard in the lecture-room. The secretary
of Sydney School of Arts writes me reproachfully
that the 700 women-readers on his members’ list
have their special room brightened weekly by the
Eink cover of The Bulletin. The authorities
aving provided a properly-equipped reading-
room for subscribers threw it open to both sexes.
The real reading or studying woman doesn’t go to
a library to chatter ; but man, the superior crea-
ture, imagines that all she wants to do is to gossip,
and so he runs her into a little room of her own,
and tells her to be a good little girl and amuse
herself with all the pretty picture-papers.
Sydney Lancers will have a procession through
the city on the day of departure, so that every-
body may have a chance to gaze admiringly on
the noble 25, who are all bong-tong. For some
time there has been a determination to keep the
whole corps strictly blue-blooded. Once upon a
time (long, long ago) their ranks included a sordid
person who actually sold collars and braces. They
wouldn’t have minded had he vended them in
large quantities, but he dealt in them one at a
time. He was a nice-looking fellow and a good
horseman and his moustache was not dyed, but
the other Lancers didn’t take to him because he
sold socks. None of them sell socks now.
Discussion is raging in Melb. just now about the
scheme of the women doctors to raise funds and
run a women’s hospital on their own account. The
Nicest, following the lead of Mrs. “Big” Clarke,
are cold-shouldering the new movement and sup-
porting an idea to build an infectious-diseases
hospital, so the lady-medicoes appeal to the women
of Victoria to push a shilling-subscription called
the “Queen’s Shilling,’ in order to replace the
present temporary building where Drs. H. Sexton
and her mates minister to their suffering sisters,
and make up prescriptions in a cupboard
only large enough to hold one small
woman and the drug bottles. About a dozen
lady doctors take turns and are kept hard at it.
These declare that the opposition of men doctors
to the new scheme is caused by pure jealousy, and
bring forward the “ modesty ” plea as their trump
card for their own sex’s support. But men doc-
tors are down on the idea of a hospital officered
altogether by women, allegingthat no Australian-
trained woman doctor has yet had enough experi-
ence to be trusted with life-and-death operations,
and instancing the great percentage of deaths oc-
curring during the female regime at the Carlton
Women’s hospital.
There is an old bed in a dusty room in the
Colonial Secretary’s department in a certain pro-
vince, but the bed is never used. However, the
Premier and his wife were very late in town one
night recently, and ’phoned for the room to be got
ready that they might camp therein. Prepara-
tions were hastily made, and the head of the
Govt, and his wife retired for the night only to
fight hordes of bloodthirsty mosquitoes. A fat
Premier in pyjamas chasing mosquitoes in the
dead of night is not a dignified sight. The
struggle proceeded until dawn, when the attend-
ant could be got at for mosquito-nets, which the
tired Premier had to fix up himself as he balanced
on the wire-mattress edge. But he consoles him-
self for the night of woe by thinking that, at any
rate, there are no skeeters at the Hotel Cecil.
They held a harvest-festival at an up-country
church. When completed the arrangements made
the edifice look like a vegetable John’s cart.
Marrows, pumpkins, smellful onions, even ple-
beian but well-washed “murphys,” were shown
conspicuously. Waving grasses tickled the
preacher’s ear at the reading-desk, and a good
fruity trophy was fixed up in a large box loudly
labelled “Keen’s Mustard.” Another case, on
which stood a mighty melon, was marked “ Some-
body’s Whisky.” “ Praise God from whom all
blessings flow.”
A jumbo-drapery had an interested crowd around
its windows lately, all pressing and pushing to see
an absorbing object in a neat glass box. The
thrilling sight was a pair of silk stockings once
worn by Queen Victoria. Are Australians more
grovelling than Britishers? Such an exhibition
as a pair of royal hose wouldn’t attract any atten-
tion in a London shop—-not even a sneer for the
simplicity of the tradesman who could imagine it
an attraction.
A suburban Bethel, in a dilapidated state of
disrepair, is flanked by a bright, neatly-kept par-
sonage. The parson takes care to be comfortably
settled himself, while the ‘ ‘ House of the Lord ”
is merely an easily-pulled-down temporary struc-
ture. On the same principle are the tumble-down
shanties seen in old-established suburbs with
expensive stabling : the original settler erected a
run-’em-up for himself, while he arranged the
stables to fit in with the grand mansion he
intended to build w'hen he made his pile. Then
he died or went bung or sold the property, and
the mansion never got itself built.
Down-trodden governesses are going to strike
for higher wages, and, as an initiative, form them-
selves into a union. It is a dark horse of a move-
ment as yet, but any step in the direction of
wringing better terms from stingy employers, who
rate “ board and lodging” as an equivalent for
imparting the polish to a family, will have a good
effect. If better terms are granted on one side,
the other ought to concede a higher standard. In
a cheap engagement it isn’t always the poor gover-
ness who falls in.
The “missis” has her side of the story to give.
Very often, in a back-blocks household, the in-
structress from “ town ” who has made an outlay
in showy blouses, and warbles at the piano fluent
ballads all the evening “without a husk,” im-
presses with awe the soul of her non-musical,
shabby employer, but grinds not much grammar
into Tommy and Ethelinda. The incompetent
who hasn’t “got up to vulgar fractions,” and the
triple-armour-plated, University-trained, musi-
cally-degree’d damsel, both call themselves gover-
nesses.
“ The pietists of a staid suburb still bemoan the
deceitfulness of human nature, as revealed by the
conduct of a once-revered brother. The ancient
in question, like St. Paul of old, posed priggishly
as a celibate, contenting himself with the minis-
trations of a like-minded niece. This devout
damsel employed her talents in collecting for
righteous objects, and invariably prefaced her
requests with the convincing words : ‘ Uncle says
Christians ought to distribute ! ’ One morning
fellow-saints read, to their intense surprise, in the
local paper, that someone described as the wife of
the good man aforesaid had died in another pro-
vince. Next day the widower and his alleged
relative were seen coming out of a town church all
blushes and orange-blossoms.
“ A party of Melb. folk, wishing to visit London
during ‘ Long Reign ’ festivities, have been can-
vassing round to find persons belonging to their
own top-lofty set who are willing to join a ‘ second
saloon party ’ on one of the mail-boats. The
accommodation offered at second-class rates more
than satisfies these snobs, but they dread the chance
of contamination by the indiscriminate ‘ second-
classer,’despite the fact that the passenger-list of
a recent outgoing mail proved that many folk of
unquestioned position travelled second class, while
the saloon was dedicated to bookies, pub.-keepers,
and persons of even weirder callings.
“ Judging from pictures by some Melb. photo-
graphers, sassiety girls here have as little hesita-
tion in posing to the camera-man in an advanced
state of undress as the most hardened ballet-
misses. To slip the bodice entirely off the
shoulders and bust, and to envelope the nude parts
of the figure with faint gauze, appears to be the
most popular arrangement for a photographic
toilette. To possess the smallest taint of modesty
is to be confessedly demodi, but one of Melb.’s
most up-to-date maidens felt her cheeks flame
when on showing some proofs of her photographs
to an old family friend the ancient remarked : ‘ Fie,
my dear young lady ; in my young days those
would have been considered smoking-room pic-
tures.’
“ Either as a proof that Lord Brassey intends
to get rid of all his superfluous belongings pre-
paratory to departing from these shores, or as a
merely speculative venture, His Ex. has given out
to the yachting fraternity that he is willing to sell
his beloved Helen, the yacht—of course, should a
suitable price be offered. A recent article in an
English periodical stated that as she now floats
the Helen is the smartest craft of the kind in Aus-
tralian waters.”
“ Word comes from Germany that Percy
Grainger, the musical Victorian lad, facially ful-
fils as exactly as ever one’s mental conception of
that nursery-rhyme saint—* the curly-headed boy
who never told a lie.’ But years, ’tis said, are not
passing over the cherubic urchin’s head without
developing more or less of the usual fickleness of
boyhood. Music is no longer his only love.
Painting claims the lion’s share of his youthfui
affections, and, if he had his own way, much of
the time at present devoted to compulsory piano-
drill would be spent in sketching from Nature.
“ Alberto Zelman (senior), accompanist-in-chief
at the Coy concert, had apparently brushed his
curls for once and threw a characteristic touch of
soul into the music, much of which the sam *
vocalists had sung under his guidance as conductor
c agono. Apropos of those tangled tresses
of his, a pet pupil, a gushful young matron, once
begged him, after an apologetic preamble, to get
his hair cut and so put an end to the irreverent
jeers of non-musical philistines. The old man
chuckled and said if that would make her happy
he d do it m a * jeef. ’ So he seized a scissors and
there and then mangled off his ringlets in one fell
swoop, leaving bald spots here and coarse, hideous
tufts somewhere else. Then he looked at himself
m a scrap of broken glass and laughed and said,
* now he was even no more wenerable ! ’ Since
then he has been let alone and his skull thatch has
gone its own wild way without outside interference.
“ At a recent Toorak wedding a female relative
of the bridegroom wore a magnificent gown of
spun glass. Broken bottles are now converted
into the most beautiful fabric imaginable, which
hangs in wonderful folds and has a lus-
trous sheen on it, eclipsing the glossiest
satin. In building a spun-glass costume the
modiste has to use infinite pains in binding over
the seams and covering rough edges of every sort
as there is a faint danger of minute particles of
glass working their way into the flesh of the
wearer. The material looks richest in the darker
tones—deep red, navy and black particularly, and
is as yet very expensive indeed.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Queen Victoria’s message to the National
Convention was a rather tardy. Salisbury doesn’t
believe in the movement at all—sniffs Republic.
An insensate and disgusting thing has been
the exhibition of boy murderer Phillips’ portrait,
as the surpliced chorister, over the petition for
signature at Cole’s, Melb.
Of the men new to interprovincial politics
R. E. O’Connor (N.S.W.) has made about the
best impression in Adelaide. His speech on Bar-
ton’s resolutions was far the shrewdest effort on
the Conservative side.
Gov. Gormanston, of Tasmania, failed to
turn up in person at the Mt. Lyell banquet. The
steamer conveying H.E. got half way there,
but, owing to the rough sea, turned back to
Hobart. When the weather moderated another
start was made, but this time the gallant fisher-
man “ wasn’t taking any,” and was represented at
the banquet by Private-Sec. Rawlinson.
Up to the end of the third day of the Con-
vention it seemed evident that there was to be but
a poor show of a fight on the question of representa-
tion in the Senate. In fact, at the end of the
third day Simon Fraser (about the worst speaker
of the 30 for Vic., N.S.W., and S.A.) “assumed
that the principle of equal representation was
generally conceded in that body and need not be
argued.” And “hear-hears” fell thick upon him.
Already the wisdom of Premier Turner’s
suggestion, that the Federal Convention should
take all the preliminary oratory for granted and
go straight to work at drafting a Constitution, has
become apparent. Too much talk is the trouble
up to date. The Convention began with an
abstract motion that it was advisable to do certain
things with a view to promoting a closer union,
&c., &c. Then every soul in the Convention
started to expound all his views on the great sub-
ject at full length. They have been at it now for
a week, and, at time of writing, were still ex-
pounding, and more than half of them have yet to
be heard from. And all this talk leads to nothing.
When the draft Constitution is ready, it
will have to be debated, clause by clause,
and all these things will have to be said
over again as a reason why the clauses
should be altered or should not be altered. The
preliminary discussion is sheer waste of time. The
Westralian delegates now announce that, if the
Convention doesn’t get along faster, they will have
to leave in order to look after their provincial
elections before the real business is anything like
finished, and consequently they will have no voice
in the Constitution-making after all. This may
result in W. A. standing out of the Federation in
disgust. The member who saw that too much
irresponsible fluency was the rock ahead of the
Convention was the level-headed man of the
gathering. When it is remembered that all the
stupendous financial problem is still to come, it
looks as if the debate might go right ahead until
June.
Howard Denver, the wealthy Englishman
who bought Butler’s clothes in ’Frisco, is known
as “Jack the Ripper, jun.” He earned his title
by buying from every source the suits worn by
murderers while committing assassinations. As
the murder-anniversaries come round Denver
dresses himself in the suit of the day and goes out
to “ repeat the murder ” by walking over the
haunted path. Already he owns 90 murderers’
n!u^ S ’) them hideously blood-splashed.
The ’Frisco papers absolutely teem with Butler
matter: 240 columns of Butjeriana appeared in
the local daily press alone within a week. The
most engaging article (which, as the prisoner is to
be tried in Australia it would ba unfair to repro-
duce) is by clever, picturesque Alice Rix, in the
Examiner. Among other things she says that
Butler says he is 37, but looks 50, and a worn 50
at that.

Of the ten S.A. Federation Conventionists,
Kingston, Holder, Baker, Downer, and Solomon
are native-born ; Cockburn, Gordon, Symon, and
Howe are Scotch ; and Glynn is Irish. Not an
Englishman among them.
Chief Justice Way, selected for the Privy
Council presumably because of his superior legal
knowledge, is paid a smaller salary than 14 other
Australian Judges. Way receives £2OOO per annum,
and the Victorian puisne Judges are each salaried
at <£3ooo.
The banquet given to N.S.W. Minister
Sydney Smith at Glen Innes show a couple of
weeks ago, surpassed in misery the famous North
Shore blow-out to the great Dibbs. Not a vege-
table appeared on the table, though Smith is
Minister for Agriculture.
Sydney junior bar gets a big leg-up in the
absence from jury causes of Pilcher, Salomons, and
Donovan, Q.C.’s, in England, and Barton and
O’Connor, Q.C.’s, in Adelaide. Want and Rogers
in common law and Gregory Walker in equity are
the only “ silk ” men left in practice.
Pognon, cyclist Lesna’s ‘ ‘ master of tech-
nique,” is an adept in the arts of love, and ex-
plains that there are 32 different ways of kissing.
“ As for the Australian—pooh! ” exclaims Pognon.
“ The Australian! he has only one way. The
Australian is very badly brought up. ”
Octogenarian It.C. Bishop Salvado has
lived 50 years amongst the natives of Westralia
at his New Norcia Mission, and is one of the few
men who understand them properly. He has
two native bands —one of string and the other of
brass—and if he would go showman with them
■could raise lots of money. All the natives on the
mission support themselves, and are taught useful
trades. The hoary old Bishop is composer of a
weird piece of music—“ The Corroboree ” —and
when his organ answers not he fills in the soulful
wails with his husky “ Italiano ” voice.

Melbourne’s chief lady palmist is neat at
repartee. The other day at a chapel-fair, after
reading the hand of a sceptical local preacher, who,
at the conclusion of the stance, impressively en-
quired “What makes a clever woman like you
carry on with this humbug?” the lady replied
“Oh I don’t know, but, after all, there isn t
much to pick between a palmist and a psalmist
nowadays.”

from England, met Judge Windeyer in London
After some conversation His Honor remarked, I
suppose you know what lam doing here . Ihe
lawyer knew very well, but he did not care to
bluntly say so. The Judge, however, made no
bones about the matter. “ You know they intend
to appoint an Australian Judge to the Bnvy
Council, and I am going to stay here and see it
through.” He has seen it through.

The Earl of Kilmorey
should have livelyrecollections
of the time when, as Lord
Newry, he visited Victoria on
the Duke of Edinburgh’s staff,
30 years ago. He got into
scrapes innumerable, includ-
ing a fierce battle with the
stalwart relative of somebody
at, Geelong. He was also pre-
sent at the West Melbourne
cock-fight when the choristers
of the Wesleyan Church ad-
joining the house in the back-
yard of which the roosters did
battle, broke down in the
middle of a hymn because
they, from their gallery, could
see and enjoy the cocks battling and the feathers

Duke were at that cock-fight too.

tapped gently as to whether they would accept
“ silk ” in honor of Her Majesty’s Record, as
gently whispered nay. The barrister who becomes
a Q.d. handicaps himself in several ways, and, to
still more heavily weight the honor, has to hand
out some 60 or 70 guineas in fees to one and
another etiquette-made claimant.

GETTING INTO CONDITION.
Premier Turner did not take his K.O.M.G
gew-gaws with him to Adelaide, and he was the
only knight at the banquet without half-a-pound
of gold and enamel on his shirt-front and a star on
his coat. Little C.M.G. Todd was resplendent,
Braddon was gorgeous, Fysh looked as if he had
slipped out of the waxworks; but Turner was a
plain figure in black and white.
Everyone admits that Premier Reid made
by far the best speech at the National Federation
banquet. Gov. Buxton was twaddlesome, Turner
commonplace, Braddon dry-as-dust, and Kingston
lost force by sticking too closely to his notes.
Reid said exactly the proper things in a most
attractive way, and he has won a big reputation
in Adelaide on the strength of it.
The deadly monotony of the first day’s
Federal Convention proceedings was relieved by
an extraordinary incident. There was, of a sud-
den, a report like distant thunder. “ A military
display,” said some of the delegates. “ Then there
rapidly followed a noise as of a thousand people
shaking rattles, laughing the while. “ Somebody
drowning a laughing-jackass,” said another dele-
gate, and a messenger was despatched to stop the
row. Then there came a creepy, nerve-shaking
cry akin to the last despairing wail of a lost soul
in Sheol, and every delegate’s hair stood on end.
The disturbance was, however, by this time located.
It was Vic. Chief Sec. Peacock’s laugh.
From Woronora (N.S.W.) Chronicle
NOTICE.
mHE proprietor of this journal begs to intimate to All
T whom it may at any time Concern, that, in future,
persons desiring their names omitted from the “ Court
Chronicles” column (indictable offences excepted) may
obtain the concession on contributing the sum of 2s. to
the Hospital Fund.
This step is taken to protect the proprietor from charges
of favoritism in the suppression of delinquents’ names.
A receipt will be given for all such sums, which will be
handed over to the treasurer of the Bulli Cottage
Hospital.
G. H. Reid’s enthusiastic championship of
Speaker Abbott’s claims to the Presidency of the
national Convention may be explained by the fact
that most of the Premiers go to England presently,
and if one of them could go as President of the
Australasian Federal Convention that one would
stand a step or two higher than the rest. So
Kingston had to be kept out, if possible. But
Kingston having been appointed, the tacticians
decided that their case would be stronger if they
could show that the mere accident of locality had
everything to do with the filling of all the offices.
So Baker was elected to the chair in committees
instead of the senior Speaker, and E. G. Black-
more was made clerk.
The latest intelligence about the “dying
Victorian poet ” Wm. Gay is that he is about to
get married —
Now really, Mr. Gay,
This is good news indeed
To think that you from fear
Of Death will now be freed ;
For when a man doth wed
And dare the nuptial strife,
Death will not be so dread
A fear to him as life.
When a boy, Premier Reid was a bit of a
practical joker, and he still delights in relating
one little incident of his younger days. A grocer,
on whom he, in common with others, had a “ set,”
used to clean his windows with a mop and bucket
of water. George, in passing, dropped a small
iron nut in the pail, and repaired to a safe dis-
tance to watch the effect. When the small-goods-
man had almost finished, he dashed the remaining
water on the window, completely smashing it!
George * 4 gat. ”
Thomas Elder, the just-deceased S.A.
millionaire squatter, spent the last two years of
his life playing with colored counters. His large
bequest to Adelaide hospital, by the way, would
probably have been struck out, owing to the politi-
cal squabbles in that institution, had testator been
mentally capable in his latter days. Dr. Perks,
formerly Resident Officer, and the first to leave
the Hospital, was his medical attendant up to his
death.
An old identity in the person of Mrs. C.
F. Gerler, “the mother of the German station,”
a settlement near Nundah (Q.), died there the
other day. Pastor Gossner, founder of the Lutheran
mission, brought Gerler to Queensland in ’43.
Gerler prospered greatly, and built a capacious
dwelling on the site of the slab hut which first
sheltered him. In ttiis dwelling the couple cele-
brated a few years ago the 50th anniversary of
their marriage.
Premier Kingston publicly offered to bury
all past differences with Symon and Baker, two of
his Convention colleagues, but Baker excluded
Kingston—although the latter had that day been
elected president from the invitations to a
reception to certain of the delegates, and
Symon and Baker both absented themselves
from the banquet given by the Ministry next
day. These facts illustrate the spirit which ani-
mates Kingston’s political enemies, but they also
explain why the masses stick to him. Even S.A.
Register, which has made desperate efforts to
be fair of late, had to break out into a sneer at the
S.A. Premier’s election to the presidentship.
In answer to The Bulletin’s assertion
that the Tories cannot raise a poet, the Argus has
adopted a young man who was discovered by the
local Freetraders, and who has the rhyme bias
strongly developed. This genius is allowed to air
his patriotic “ pomes ” in the Argus, in considera-
tion of the fact that he occasionally refers to Pim-
perialism, Freetrade, and conservatism in an
appreciative way. His last outbreak is “An Aus-
tralian Anthem ” of such virility that it suggests
the wild enthusiasm of a sick hen moralising under
a tub. Sample appended :
Let no bold foemen come, insulting,
Raiding our coast and treading our shore;
Or, swift to meet them, may we uprising
Drive them afar, that they oome no more.
This is decent Fitzgibbonese, and, if the Argus
keeps its new poet going till the general election,
there probably will not be a single Conservative in
next Vic. Parliament.
PERGY CAVILL.
Photo., Brand.
Charles Bonney, who died in Sydney on
March 16, aged 84, was South Australia’s last
pensioner. He had drawn =6250 a year from the
province since 1856, when the new Constitution
Act was passed and he lost his permanent appoint-
ment as Commissioner of Crown Lands. He came
to Sydney in ’34, and in ’36 went to C. H. Ebden’s
station, where Albury now stands. Thence he
led an exploring party with a flock of sheep to
Port Philip in Jan., ’37- He returned to Albury
by way of Launceston and Sydney, and, taking
10,000 sheep with him, formed a station at Kil-
more. He travelled twice to Adelaide with cattle
before 1839, and continued his journeys overland
with stock until 1841, when Sir George Grey, then
Governor of S.A., made him Commissioner of
Crown Lands, a post which he retained until Re-
sponsible Government came in ’67. He was also
a member of the first (Finniss) Ministry formed
in S.A. Mr. Bonney returned to Sydney nearly
30 years ago, and during the closing period of his
life he was quite blind. His son is still a S.A.
civil servant.

Parkes, Robertson and Dibbs were rejected
by their constituents on the first general election
subsequent to their acceptance of titles. A memo,
for G. H. Reid, Seddon and Kingston.
W. J. Lyne, a Tasmanian by birth, was
the first to state openly in the Convention that he
was determinedly opposed to allowing Tasmania
equal representation in the Federal Senate.
All the S.A. delegates but Baker and
Downer were anxious that Kingston should pre-
side at the Convention, but the two knights
wanted Barton. The election caused much heart-
burning.
THE NEW WOMAN.—AT HANNAN'S, W.A.
A District Court action which arose out of
a claim for remuneration for composing speeches
for candidates at the recent Convention elections
was settled out of Court the other day, in Sydney,
for half the amount demanded.
“We give a vote in Tasmania to the man
who deserves it,” said Premier Braddon at the
Convention. All The Bulletin can say is that
there must be a dreadful lot of impersonation
carried on at Tasmanian elections.
The set-back received by the Australian
Labor-parties at the Federal elections is likely to
prove beneficial. The workers may now see the
necessity of swapping the man with the mouth
for the man with the mouth and brains.
Premier Nelson’s variations on his rela-
tion to Australian Federation, and his coming into
or staying out of the Convention, resemble closely
Swanhilda Butler’s vacillating explanations as to
how he came by Lee Weller’s property.
So far as it has gone the Federal Conven-
tion of ’97 appears to be head and shoulders above
the one of six years ago in brains, knowledge and
earnestness of purpose. A vast number of
mediocrities have dropped out —Dibbs, Suttor,
Jennings, Rutledge, Macdonald-Paterson, Bray,
Thynne, Gillies, Munro, Wrixon, Shiels, Collard-
Smith, and Nicholas Fitzgerald are a few good
samples. The Victorian contingent has improved
most, and it had also the most room for improve-
ment ; the Vic. delegation of ’9l didn’t contain a
man, bar Alfred Deakin, who knew when to come
in successfully out of the wet. The N.S.W. and
S.A. delegates are at least as bright a lot
as those which represented these two provinces in
'9l, and on that occasion they were about the
best of the whole collection.
Carruthers, at the Convention :
Everything pointed to Sydney as the proper place for
the Federal capital. The Primacy of Australasia was
there, and so was the head of the Roman Catholic Church.
Peacock : Yes, but Melbourne has the head of the Sal-
vation Army.
At Bathurst. Two old Irish women meet
after Cardinal Moran’s Convention-election defeat.
“So the Cardinal did not get in, after all! ”
“ No ! And glad I am—he might lose the other
billet.” Prudent old girl! If Tom Slattery only
had as much horse-sense !
Stout’s dreadful earnestness in advising
Seddon to go to England is one of the reasons
why Digger Dick has been so very undecided about
going. Stout also wants Parliament to go on
sitting in Seddon’s absence, and the natural infer-
ence is that the Cold-Tea Knight thinks he sees
his way to making Jock Mackenzie do something
supremely foolish in his chief’s absence.
Premier Braddon (Tas.) thinks that care
should be taken to prevent the Federal Govern-
ment having too much revenue, because “ a
deficit is useful in various ways.” A deficit
involves either temporary or permanent borrow-
ing, or else default. Which of them is “ useful in
various ways ? ” Also, in which ways ? There are
likewise various ways in which it is useful to have
your pants too short at the feet, if Braddon only
knew it.
At the wind-up of the Wesleyan Confer-
ence in Melbourne a resolution was carried to the
effect that the Conference wishes “ to secure the
embodiment in the new Australian Federal Con-
stitution of a formal acknowledgment of God as
Ruler of Nations.” And these are reverent men !
Reverence as generally existing amongst parsons
is a something that depends upon the total absence
of a sense of humor and the existence of a bloated
conceit, a form of moral elephantiasis.
Premier Reid has, it is alleged, received
the personal assurances of a majority of Assembly
members that they will allow N.S.W. Parliament
to adjourn till he returns from England. The
plan of campaign will, therefore, disclose itself
thus : Reid will boldly declare his intention to go,
and will wind up with a motion for the necessary
adjournment. A member will promptly apply the
closure, and the House will divide. A man sure
beforehand of his majority can afford to be daring.
A curious subterranean movement, of
which Premier Reid is supposed to be the origina-
tor, has been going on in the Federal Conven-
tion, the idea being to force a sudden adjournment
for an indefinite period on the ground that the
present time is “inopportune.” Almost all the
rumors of delay seem to somehow centre round
Premier Reid. He is accused, on apparently good
evidence, of having suggested a postponement
even before the election. He mentioned a possible
adjournment the other day in Melbourne, and
again in Adelaide. He was connected somehow
with the latest scheme for waiting till Queensland
might come in about next July or August. He is
also the only Premier who proposes to hang up
the Federal movement till his return from Eng-
land. Altogether there seems to be a mist of
suspicion round the rotund man from N.S.W.
England has a carriage-tax, a
footman-tax, and an armorial-bear-
ings tax. Who says no more direct
taxes are needed in Australia ?
The Melb. metropolitan commit-
tee of the Australian Natives’ Asso-
ciation is probably the nearest Aus-
tralian approach to Tarn man}' Hall }
both in the nationality and religion
of its inner ring and in—other things.
Melbourne Age, suddenly dis-
covering that N.S. Wales has borrowed
.£1,230,000 for defence purposes, while
Victoria has borrowed only .£98,000,
urges the Government to borrow some
money forthwith and buy rifles and
cannon and things with it. In this
great loan land the pained feeling that
comes over a province when it finds
that it has omitted to borrow as
much as its neighbor is truly pathetic.
Rho : “ ‘Kish the buke’ is abso-
lutely unknown in France, Belgium,
Austria, Spain and Italy. Even in
mossgrown Britain any man may
demand the Scotch form of affirma-
tion, without stating his reasons for
objecting to ‘ kish the buke,’ under
the sth section of Bradlaugh’s Act.
V ictorian law only allows the refusal on
the ground of alleged conscientious
reasons, and the section so reads as to
imply that only adverse theology can
be a conscientious reason. Surely a
man may have conscientious reasons
against accepting a dose of syphilis
from, say, a previously-sworn prosti-
tute, or a dose of cancer or leprosy from
any other predecessor. A case recently-
occurred at South Melbourne where a
doctor J.P. ordered a fresh Bible to be
got, having noticed that a witness
just sworn had a cancer of the lip.
London Times has just started an agi-
tation for the utter abolition of the
worthless and dangerous book oath and
substitution of a plain affirmation
covered by a perjury punishment.”
Rho : “ Tribune Trenwith is a ‘ cert.’ for
the extra Vie. portfolio which will be rendered
necessary by Turner and Duffy’s exodus. Isaacs
will be the next Premier, and Peacock will remain
Chief Sec. and Minister of Education.”
The United States did not settle the slave-
question before federation, and the consequence
was a civil war. Let us hope that Queensland
will definitely stay away from the Convention, so
that Australian Federation will start with the
slave-State outside.
“ An inquiry into
the management of
N.S.W. Marine Board
will shortly be under-
taken by the Public Ser-
vice Board.” Ha, ha 1
Already can you hear
the dry bones rattle in
ship-chandlery circles.
A SHORT STAY IN TOWN.
MRS. CHOLMONDELEY-TAYLOR : “ Oh, I am so disappointed, Mrs. Be Jones. Only an afternoon
call, when I expected you to stay with us a fortnight !”
MRS. DE JONES : “ I'm so sorry. I would if I could —but I have an exertion ticket, and it
perspires to-morrer.”
The Australian Premiers are to have
honorary membership of the Carlton Club during
their stay in London. The Carlton is blue Tory
from the door-mat to the last purple-jowled,
gouty Earl. Will those Premiers who accept the
hospitality ever succeed in getting away from the
Carlton Club ?
S.M. Herald remarks that when Gov.
Buxton proposed the toast of “ Australia,” at the
time when the Federal delegates took a snack with
the S.A. Ministry at Adelaide, the sentiment was
greeted with a ‘ ‘ deafening British cheer. ” Ap-
parently, in this poor, miserable country, even the
cheers are borrowed from England.
USEFUL, IF .
BOSS COCKIE : “Now, I
wonder if I cud git
that there feller to
spend his holidays up
at my place? ”
When the Federal Enabling Bill wasgoiim
through S.A. Assembly in ’95, John
then Opposition leader, said, in tones of deepest
melancholy: “It is not much use iny taking an
interest in shaping this measure. If the country
votes in one constituency it won’t elect me
because I have not played to the gallery suffi-
ciently. I won’t even have the temerity to stand. ”
“Oh, yes, you will,” retorted Kingston; “cheer
up ; they’ll put you in all right.” And they did,
although, when the time came, Kingston would
rather that Downer’s prophecy had been fulfilled.
A point of great importance struck the
Federal Convention last week in the question
whether the whole business should be hung up to
meet Queensland’s latest whim. Sydney Tele-
graph responded next day with a particularly apt
and able leading article--S.M. Herald, as usual,
waited to see how the cat would jump.
Seddon is about the only Australasian
Premier who can go to do the Record Reign grovel
with a light heart and reasonable safety. He isn’t
in the Federal movement, and his financial year
closes 31st March, so he can get his financial state-
ment through in April, announce his surplus, then
shut up the premises and depart in a blaze of
glory.
The O’Sullivan, M.L.A. : “In 1830 the
population of Australia was under 300,000 ; nowit
was 4,500,000. At this rate of progress, within 50
years we would see 30,000,000 people inhabiting
these lands.” Quite so. In 1830, or thereabouts,
G. R. Dibbs was one foot high. In 1870 he was
six feet high. At this rate of progress in 1910 he
will be 36 feet high.
Westralia Forrest’s jubilation over achiev-
ing a record in an uninterrupted Premiership
directs attention to the extreme variableness of a
first Premier’s tenure of office in Australia. Forrest
has exceeded six years by a couple of months, just
beating Queensland Herbert, who ruled from 10th
Dec., ’59, to Ist Feb., ’66. But Donaldson, the
first N.S.W. Premier, was on deck only a couple
of months, and Charley Cowper, who succeeded
him, scarcely five weeks. The first Premier
(Haines) in Victoria held on for 14 months, but
O Shanassy, who followed, fell in six weeks.
Premier Finniss (S.A.) lasted ten months, and
Baker, who succeeded, held the fort for nine days.
Tasmania’s first Premier, the stately Champ, held
good for three months, and Gregson, his successor,
for two. So that a record in first Premierships
would seem to be allotted to the individual as
red hair or a hooked nose is.


A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. #
The deterrent effect of capital punishment
was well exemplified in Melb. recently, when a
Swanston-street restaurateur fired four shots at his
late lady love, the day after young Quinn had been
sentenced to death for the Albert Park Beach
murder.

ment law-suit a la Mercadool will relate to a cer-

What have the Melb. University students
done, for good or evil, that they should get their
“ Anthem ” verses quoted by the local press ?
When Halford left the meds.,
They felt keen grief at partin’:
Their “ cheek ” was drenched and their heart wag
wrenched,
And now they say “ It’s Martin."
The poet, in this case, had a drenched cheek like-
wise.

It is reported that Melbourne Age pro-
poses to run a candidate fbr every Vic. constituency
at next general election, and thus decide the ques-
tion whether David Syme really owns Victoria or
not. if David the Dictator would run a Liberal
candidate for every seat in the Council, and use
his immense power to shift the fossils bodily, he
would do a lot more good. Almost every Vic.
reformer devotes himself to Liberalising the As-
sembly, which is as easy as falling downstairs,
seeing that the Assembly is reasonably democratic
already ; and denounces the Council afar off, while
leaving the difficult job of reforming it to some-
one else. In other words, they all go out and slay
the Toryism that is already dead, while leaving
the live article severely alone.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The final night of N.S.W. Wheelmen’s Carnival
saw far and away the best racing of the meeting, but in
cash the League panned out badly on the night’s biz,
profit being 6s. 4d. A good crowd would have come but
for the rawness of the night; there is not much fun
watching Megson and Payne out-manoeuvre Green and
Edwards when one is wishing for a warm bed and a hot
brick. The triplet race was a magnificent one between
most jealously rival crews ; Marshall’s triplet beat Green-
wood’s by half-a-wheel. Australia v. England, one mile
unpaced, was a truly stirring contest. Never before did
Payne and Megson ride with such pace and judgment.
They out-generalled the Englishmen in tactics and left
them hopelessly in the sprint home. In the paced five
miles Payne -asily beat Edwards, and Megson beat Green
by perhaps uie best effort yet seen upon a Sydney cycling
track. To leave his own exhausted pacers in the last lap,
to catch Green and his fast triplet, to hang on to Green’s
wheel, and then to come round on the outside and beat
the Englishman home is a feat of which the most
enthusiastic of Megson’s admirers never thought him
capable. Megson’s form just now is perfect.
• $ $
Vic. League’s March meeting opened with fine
facing but mediocre gate : £250 was taken in the after-
noon, and less in the evening. Fact is the general public,
having but a superficial knowledge, don’t care a rap for
“scientific ” cycling; they want to see the game anyhow.
They can see it on the unbanked Melb. cricket-ground
grass track, and they can’t see it on the St. Kilda and
Exhibition paths, where, owing to the greed of the man-
agers in bringing the fence right up to the track’s edge,
only the front row can view the whole race.
The “ points ” of the meeting were the splendid
sprints of the dauntless Megson, the failures of Bilmartin,
the fine handicap riding of the rapidly-improving youth,
O. O. Tebbutt, and the sickening smash that took place
in one of the heats of the Federal Handicaps.
Megson takes long to strike form. During the
early part of his English trip his riding was ridiculed, yet
in his last half dozen races his finishes were hailed as
“ revelations in sprinting.” In the Mile Championship of
Australasia he did not meet Martin, who was beaten in
the preliminary heat by both Middleton and Relph, but
he won his heat with great ease, and when half way in
the last lap of the final, he swung round and flew past
everybody and flashed in with a three-length lead.
In his heat of the Mile, Martin did what he
has never done before. Pushing out at the half lap,
Relph collared him as they turned into the straight.
This is just the spot where Bill makes his final kick and
wins all his races, but on Saturday, though he kicked his
hardest, the Englishman’s lower gear got away steadily,
and, as Middleton came past both with a great rush, the
Plugger gave in.
Martin was evidently so disgusted with his
form that he failed to come out for the Federal Handicap,
where he had to give Megson 30yds; but after tea he
showed up among tne 26 starters in the Five-mile Vic.
championship, in which, strange to say, there was but
one fall and no hurts. Martin, fearing Megson’s final
sprint, tried to run the latter off his legs, but the Sydneyite
kept them all company, and when “Bill” made his final
rush, he swept past the Yankee on the curve with Walne
right on his back wheel. Martin faded away as in the
Mile, allowing Parsons to get third place.
Megson has never ridden better ; his finishes
are of the sort with which he astounded Melb in the ’93
Austral. For the last 18 months the country has been
raked to find someone able to down the man with the
iron jaw. It looks for the moment as if the man had been
found in Megson. Just now, however, Martin is obviously
not at his best.
Tebbutt (90yds.) won both his heat and the final
of the Federal Handicap in fine style. During the past
week this cyclist, practically unknown ere he won the
£5OO of the A.N.A. race, carried off two races at Benalla
as well —£6 Jin all. He should be a scratch man by the
time he is 21.
Of the outer division of first-class men, O.
Prowse, Hickox, and Berry look the most likely to de-
velop into champions.
The “ ordinary ” race, won by Kellow, would
have been a throw-in for any of those individuals who
still ride the antiquated high machines about the streets ;
the riders who used to shine in by-gone days seemed
utterly lost on returning to an altitude of sft.
Rolfe is probably the world’s oldest racing
cyclist. Though 46 he is still in condition, and on March
17 won the Bendigo Hibernian Handicap of £3o—his son
won the Half-Mile dash at the same sports.
The Jewell family of Maryborough (Vic.) goes
even one better by clearing the whole board at the local
March sports. Of the five events three were won by A.
Jewell and two by his brother Don. The last-named
cyclist lately cleared “ the card ” at Ballarat—heats and
finals—a total of 10 races.
One of the most sensational-looking smashes
ever seen on a cycling-track took place in the fourth heat
of the Federal Handicap 30yds. from the winning-post.
When all the leaders were straining every nerve, Carter,
of S.A., skidded and fell, bringing down Middleton, Gar-
gurevich, and several others. The two named were
stunned and badly bruised, but no one was seriously hurt.
The cycling accidents that appear most fearful only end
in bruises or cuts. It is when a man steps slowly and
gracefully from his machine as it is going dead slow and
then drops to the ground that he receives fatal internal
injuries.
* * * *
The L.V.W. knows how to make its goose part
with the golden eggs. Its present meeting extends over
two Saturdays with afternoon and evening performances,
so that the enthusiast who wants to see the races through
is called upon to pay four times. This “to be continued
in our next” business disgusts the public, the programmes
being so artfully arranged that there is no satisfaction
worth mentioning in any one of the four acts.
* » * *
Clem. Fisher, ex-eycling-champion of Maori-
land, who has of late years ridden chiefly in Melbourne,
has “ given the game best.” His smash up on A.N.A.
day satisfied him that it would be wise to step out whilst
enough of him remained to swear by. As it is, he has
plugged the cracks on scores of asphalt racing-tracks with
scraps of his anatomy, and watered all the grass paths
with his gore. He claims to be the unluckiest rider on
earth, and is tired of travelling from one metropolitan
track to another merely to afford the spectators the thrill
of seeing his mangled remains hawked off in a truck.
* * * »
At the big annual amateur M.L. cycle-meeting,
held in Wellington on the new track (3 laps 201 yds. to
the mile), the five-mile championship was a gruesome
spectacle. Sixteen started, and fifteen fell, some more
than once. Four toppled at pistol-fall, ten came down
later, when there w-as gore galore and smashed noses
and arms. The accident was repeated later, when five
fell, and the race was declared void. Reynolds (Auck-
land) won eventually. The track is good, but looking
round and wobbly wheeling will yet end in a graveyard
plot.
* * * *
“ Carbine ” Hunt and amateur Reynolds are far
and away the best cyclists in their classes in M.L. In-
ducements have been vainly held out to Reynolds (a
cycle-maker) to throw over lily-white amateurism and
meet Hunt for £2OO. “Make it £5OO and I’m on,” says
Reynolds.
* * * *
Boidi returns to Italy in April. He has never
been able to go the pace in Australia, and blames the
food-supply in choice Italian. He says all the cooks sent
by the devil emigrated to Australia. Some Australians
agree with him, having married the cooks.
* * * *
All around Melbourne one hears funny little
tales of time-payment bicycles getting seized for non-
payment of “calls.” An army of confiscators must be
employed by the various firms.
• * * «
X.Z. : “On the look-out for a sound roadster
bike, I have spent a fortnight chasing round for a good
second-hander to suit. I have struck already about 5000
machines which are the original machine that Lord
Brassey rode when he first came out. With straw in my
hair, I seek the explanation. Is the Gov. a cycle-agent in
disguise who brought with him 5000 cycles? One curious
point is that the more inferior the article the more
Brassey-ness was used as an inducement.”
* * * *
In a Sydney bicycle shop cyclist Porta unwit-
tingly seized hold of an almost red-hot handle-bar. His
subsequent description of an encounter with a man whom
he mistook for a chemist is graphic : “ He say not sell
medseen ; he denteest. My hand burn like red-hot. I say
‘ Go-o to-o hell! —go-o to-o hell! I seej r ou on de ground;
I keeck you; I jomp on you ; I see you dead. Go-o to-o
hell ’! ”
* * * *
What’s the matter with a tax on cycles ?
Another cycle victim has been trying to cut his
throat at Maryborough, Vic. Long-distance racing means
insomnia and madness to most people. A competitor in
a Warrnambool-to-Melbourne race told his trainer casually
on dismounting : “ A skeleton paced me all the way from
Werribee.” Bike d.t.’s are very much more dangerous
than the old variety,
* * •
Curious that it should have been left to N.S.W.
Justice Cohen—one of the Chosen—to decide that cash-
betting on the racecourse is illegal. But there is Scrip-
tural precedent for it: Moses smashed the Books of the
Law.
» * » *
What a boom the kinetoscopic and cinemato-
graphic pictures of the Fitzsimmons-Corbett fight, which
was lightning-photographed all through, will have when
they come along ! Presumably some Australian showman
has arranged for the series.
* * * *
Corbett said : “I will take no chances with
Fitzsimmons, and I think I can whip him in 15 rounds.”
By the style in which he went for Fitz. right from the
start, he seems to have kept his word in the former
respect, and he very nearly brought off the 15th round—
for the wrong man ! After his Sullivan fight, Corbett
told an interviewer that he had all but perfected the art
of easing-away from a hard-un—a notion over which more
pugilists than himself have fallen in badly.
* * * *
During a conversation on the Fitzsimmons-Cor-
bett fight, in a Melb. bar the other night, the barman
chipped in with a reminiscence of John L. Sullivan’s visit
to Melbourne. One night Johnl was taking nobblers at
the bar “ like pills,” when Mrs. Johnl entered arid tried to
get him home. Failing, she lost her temper, and swooped
down on the slugger’s ears with sounding smacks, and
flounced out, leaving John grinning sheepishly. Which is
the most complimentary thing The Bulletin has ever
heard of John L. Sullivan.

“P. Chenery, a Goulburn Valley 12st. boy,
represents Australia in the Interhospital Boxing Compe-
titions. A pupil of Dan Creedon’s, with a heavy right, a
clever left, and an ironbark punching-piece, the medical
honor of ‘ down below ’ will be gallantly fought for by
this doctor in embryo,” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent. “ King’s College Hospital has his assist-
ance in the Rugby scrum, and relies on the straight kick-
ing of another Australian, R. H. Strong, late of the
Carlton F.C. and son of Melbourne’s Mayor, a smart,
clean-limbed athlete, when a shot for goal is secured. St.
Bartholomew’s Hospital mopped the sward with King’s in
the Rugby Cup tie, and were led to victory by an Aus-
tralian captain—Carruthers. We may be cronk at bank-
ing and financing generally, but we are sound as red-gum
blocks at W”
* * * *
“ The hope of the billiardistic world is that
Peall will beat Roberts in the present big game,” writes
our London correspondent. “ Most billiard-bailers are
aweary of the spot-barred ‘ push ’-played champion who
will have his own rules and stipulations, or no one’s. He
bars the spot-stroke—a clever one plainly visible to the
audience —and makes two or three hundred foul nursery
cannons in an evening with his perfection ‘ push ’ that
not more than two or three can see or be at all sure of
either way. The ‘ spot ’ is not half as tiresome to watch
as such nursery cannons. You might just as well bar
Lyons hitting ‘ fourers ’ at cricket and limit him to pokey
singles. The game of billiards is all that is fair, and no
player counts the ‘ push ’ fair.
“ In this match,” proceeds the writer, “Roberts
performed a record ‘ push.’ His ball was eight inches off
and facing the right-hand baulk-pocket. The red six
inches away from and covering the left centre hole.
(Measurements roughly gauged by eye). The Push Pro.
followed his ball in one sweeping push until the butt
reached his hand-rest and the ‘ white’hit the ‘red.’ It
went three-quarters through the vermilion and fell in the
left centre pocket. A phenomenal ‘ push.’ ”
* » • *
Despite inspired pars, in the dailies, the various
provincial rowing associations are not a happy family re
the amateur rowing definition. In May, ’96, a conference,
in which N.S. Wales, Vic., and Q’sland were represented,
agreed that any rower who had competed in any branch
of cash sport should be debarred from rowing in an inter-
provincial eight-oar race. Q’sland has informed N.S.VV.
that Vic. has decided that any athlete who has not com-
peted for cash since the beginning of this year shall be
eligible. An answer as to what definition the race is to be
rowed under is now urgently pressed for by N.S.W., and
unless the amateur definition as decided upon in the ’96
conference is adhered to, N.S.W. will send no crew.
* * * *
George Towns, of the Hunter River, sensibly
declines to meet Stanbury before going to try his luck in
England. Towns proposes to work his passage as fire-
man, so should be in good orderto meetsome of the second
or third-raters, just to see what he is made of. His beat-
ing of Neilson was not a performance good enough to
place him alongside first-flight men, and he shows sound
sense in proposing to tackle some of the dimmer lights, and
row himself into form for a go with Gaudaur, should he
win up to that point. Taking a line from Neilson’s show-
ing with him, Towns’ chance with Stanbury would be
simply nil. No rowing men in Australia look upon
Gaudaur’s victory over Stanbury as any criterion of the
latter’s form, for judges of the game think Stanbury as
good a man in a wager-boat as even Searle.
* * * *
Swimmer Percy Cavill has proved himself best
man in Australia from 440yds. to a mile. He was born in
England, but came to Australia when three or four years
old. His father, born and matured in England, measured in
condition 49in. round the chest, stood sft. 9in. and weighed
14st. 111 b. Ernest Cavill, the eldest son, comingto Australia
when 11, goes in condition 44 round the chest, stands 6ft.,
weighs 13st. Chas. Cavill, the second son, gives chest
measurement 41, height 6ft., weight 12st. 71b. Percy
Cavill, the fourth son, measures 38 chest, stands sft. llin.,
weighs list. 21b. As all the Cavills were taught to swim
as soon as they could walk, and have had daily prac-
tice at this best of exercises, and have lived in the
open air always, it follows that their development has
been well ensured. But why this falling away ? Clearly
the boys who were youngest when they came to Australia
are the weedyish ones. The eldest brother has 7in. chest-
expansion ; Percy has but three. The father, as a young
man, was a favorite model with sculptors and artists ; in
fact, one statue of the Prince of Wales has the Prince’s
head and Cavill’s body. No artist, however, would take
Percy Cavill for a model—he is too emphatically Corn-
stalkish. The falling away of the Australian sons troubles
their mother not a little.' She says : “ All my boys were
born little models of their father, and continued such until
they got into their teens. Then gradually they grew lanky
and their chests flattened; they are Australians, not
Englishmen.” Percy Cavill is an exhibition swimmer; he
travels in a dead straight line ; the water hisses and
bubbles between the strokes of his sinewy limber legs,
and his hands and arms cut their way as do fishes’ fins.
His grit is undeniable: all his would-be rivals concede
him that. When he gets a fighter like he got in Regan
last Saturday, he swims him into cold exhaustion, and
clips 34 3-ssec. off the world’s half-mile amateur record.
The swimming enthusiasts are talking of sending him to
England. But could the Australian ever swim the
Channel as his English father did before him ?

• Rumor hath it that Resolute’s preparation is but
patchy, and that if he comes across the border he will not

being in the best of condition.
* * * *
Last week Mr. H. C. White shipped for ’Ome
the horses Form (Cranbrook —La Mode) and Georgie
(Clan Stuart—Mirzapore). The latter should do well if
she goes on all right, for as a two-year-old she was very
“slippy,” easily beating Wallace and Hova at w.f.a. in
A. J.C. All-Aged Stakes. When her racing career is ended
she is to be mated with the famous sire St. Serf (St.
Simon Feronia by Thormanby). The Martini-Henry
mare Acmena, now at ’Ome, is to be put to the same sire.
* * * •
Unless something unlooked-for happens between
this and the end of the season, Glebe must again take first
place for the Hordern Shield, and Sydney University come
a bad last. It is a question whether University is now
“ form” enough to take a place with the first-class clubs.
They not alone keep other teams from using the first-class
grounds, but it is pretty much the same as drawing a bye
for the club having the luck to be drawn against them.
Outsiders cannot understand how the members comply
with the residential clause, and, taking one consideration
with another, University should next season be missing
from the teams battling for the pennant.
• • • •
East Sydney had a bad time on Saturday after-
noon trying to dispose of Paddington. M‘Kenzie and
Bannerman —the two Alicks, giant and midget—put up
177 for the first wicket, M’Kenzie being caught when three
short of his century. Bannerman and Noble then got
together for 50 minutes and left off for the day, the
former not out 118, the latter ditto 16—one wicket for 242.
* * * *
Giffen’s Australian team will play two matches
at Perth and probably two at Coolgardie. A ground
cannot be got at Kalgoorlie. New turf wickets have been
prepared at Perth. What sort of a time will lightning
Jones have on a “ new ” turf wicket ?
* * » *
G. G. Stead, the wealthy M.L. racing magnate,
is now on his waj' ’Ome for pleasure. He hasn’t made the
fashionable error of taking either of his fine colts with
him to throw away on a flooded market, but intends
securing a first-class stallion to mate with his grand brood
mares at Yaldhurst.
* * * *
The recent successes of Courallie and Mercia, in
India, call attention again to the English prospects of
that sterling mare, Maluma, who showed her heels to both
of them at Flemington a year ago.
• • * •
Dear Bulletin, —It was the rule with certain
of aboriginal sprinter Charley Samuels’ “ backers ” to put
a tolerable suit of clothes on his back after he had achieved
a win, and then set him adrift among jugs and bottles.
After a fortnight or so, he would be told that his money
was all vanished. By the way, his return is made just at
the time when those in the know say that it only needs
an enterprising man with a few hundreds to set the ped.
business on its feet.—T.D.
» » * *
At the recent great carnival of the N.S.W.
Wheelmen’s League all the placed men in the great paced
race used Dunlop tires. J. Fitzgerald won the big handi-
cap—the Sydney Wheel Race—on Dunlop tires, and Joe
Megson, riding Dunlops and Westwood rims, surprised
everybody by his easy defeat of Bilmartin in the 10-mile
paced race.*
* * * *
More bicycles for Australia. Joe Hill, the crack
Syracuse missionary, has come to Sydney to make con-
verts to his bi-cycle. He will establish agencies through-
out Australia, and he won’t stop there—Joe and his
bi-cycle are going around the world.*

Civil-servant “ Felix ” goes on with his cricket
in the Australasian, gratis.
Condensed horror as reported from Mel-
bourne :
A young bank-clerk named Trader cut his throat on
Friday night at Maryborough. He is in a very critical
condition. It is supposed that he became insane through
the excessive strain of bicycling in a road-race from
Warrnambool to Melbourne. He was unable to speak,
but wrote on a piece of paper that it.was sad that he was
an atheist.. He endeavored to escape from Dr. Miller,
who was stitching up his wound, and wrote that he had
already done for one doctor. He is not expected to re-
cover.
What a subject for the ordinary “ ranting ” par-
son to maunder about in his Sunday evening dis-
course—the insane bank-clerk with his throat cut
writing on a scrap of paper that he was sorry he
was an atheist.

Pearson’s Weekly, of 9th Jan., 1897,
contains the sensational announcement that
“ while everybody is talking of the gold-mines of
the Australian continent, and while company after
company is being formed for the purpose of ex-
ploiting them and digging from the bowels of the
earth the metal which dazzles the eye of man and
so frequently turns his brain, a humbler but far
more brilliant source of wealth has recently been
discovered —coal! Where coal is,” continues
Pearson, “ wealth must inevitably follow. . . .
present indications proclaim the fact that there is
a continent of fuel.” Talk about going abroad to
hear home news. Coal was discovered in Aus-
tralia in 1797 in different places north and south
of Sydney. The gross ignorance of Pearson,
which claims a huge circulation in England, is
hardly made more manifest by the fact that the
output, which was 40,732 tons for the year so long
ago as 1847, expanded in 1891 to 4,037,929 tons.

S.S. : “The committee of Melb. national
gallery is organising a loyal, royal peep-show in
honor of Her Majesty’s sexagesimal jubilee, and
requests all persons having paintings, etchings, or
prints, the subjects of which bear directly upon
Queen Victoria’s great and glorious reign and are
of special interest, to be kind enough to lend them
to the trustees for the exhibition. It seems to me
that it is the duty of The Bulletin to encourage
this worthy object by sending along half-a-dozen
or so of Phil May’s loyal cartoons, particularly
those epitomising Her Majesty’s devotion to
Thrift —the one virtue that is its own reward.”
A sidelight on the work of a more-or-less-
backblocks bishop :
Editor Bulletin, — I have had iritis, and have not been
able to read or write, but I am told you have been chaff-
inn me for driving about my work with four ponies. I
had that picture taken by a photographer whom I met by
chance in a desolate lignum swamp, near the border of
Q. and N.S.W., and hoped to evoke sympathy thereby,
but it seems I have failed. No one drives four horses
in Q. for show. The roads are too bad. It is grim
necessity. One must either drive the four or run a pair
loose, with a man to drive them/ That means six horses,
and carrying feed and at times rugs for six horses is
serious. If you have ever been blacksoiled, you will
know what the four horses are for. A gentleman kindly
came down here to drive the Bishop of Brisbane and me
from Miles to Tarwon, some 90 miles. He had, I think,
14 horses, three four-horse teams, and two men driving
the loose horses. The last three miles took four hours,
and six horses, and I have always felt distressed at the
thought of the self-restraint which the presence of two
Bishops forced upon the several kind people who came
out to assist us. No. Driving in Q. is hard graft, not
show. I managed to do 2000 miles on that trip with
eight horses, and kept my appointments right through,
with one regrettable exception, though to do it I once
had to pay L 2 ss. for a bag of maize, holding less than
four bushels. And I did not take up any collection,
though I must next time, as I find I do not get any credit
for my forbearance. That same trip, I had been driving
a good many hours along a very-little-used track, where
one had to swing off into the scrub to avoid fallen trees,
and chance getting back without tangling up one’s horses,
when I saw a man riding along the road. I drew my
horses up on an ant-hill, where the inhabitants were all
in full work, so there was much kicking till I got into a
more peaceful place. I then asked him “How far is it to
station?” He: “About five miles. Are you
going to play to-night?” I (puzzled): “No. To pray,
perhaps, if I get the chance of a service.” He : “ What ?
Aren’t you Ashton’s circus?” I: “No. I am only a
bush-bishop.” This is the result of driving cream ponies.
I regret to say he did not turn hack with me, as perhaps
he would have done had I been “ going to play.” Don’t
you think that, after this, we deserve sympathy, and that
a grateful country which sends Premiers and riflemen to
England might send a colonial product in the way of a
bush-bishop to represent it at the Diamond Jubilee ? If
the country really desired it, and would pay, I would re-
luctantly consent to go. This is, I think, the right way of
putting it.—l am, &c., John F. Stretch, Bishop-Coadjutor
of Brisbane.

A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfalme Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become s 6
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible I
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

lian Press: —

prove.”

It is alleged at Hobart that a case of at-
tempted smuggling by an officer of one of the
warships has been hushed up.
Lady Glasgow, like Governors Jersey and
Brassey, came accidentally on an old friend while
wandering about the slopes of Moss Vale (N.S. W.).
The old identity was settled on the land and doing
so well that he declined an offer made him to go
at once to England and take the position of head
gardener on the Glasgow estates.

A Lady’s Troubles.
FOUND HOUSEHOLD DUTIES
FATIGUING.
WANTED TO REMAIN IN BED ALL DAY.
HAD NO AMBITION TO DO ANYTHING
AND FELT TERRIBLY LANGUID.
From the Australian Courier.
“Up to the Spring of 1890,”says Mrs. Margaret
Layfieid, of 09 Surrey-street, Darlinghurst, Sydney
(in answer to a reporter’s inquiry concerning her-
self), “I was comparatively healthy, and could
carry out my household duties without feeling in
the slightest degree fatigued, and would enjoy a
sound and restful night’s sleep. Then I began to
suffer from weakness, and a feeling of depression.
I had a fullness and a pain at the chest and
round the sides ; I was also troubled with palpita-
tion of the heart and a sense of overbearing ex-
haustion, lost a great deal of sleep ; in fact, I kept
tossing about the bed all night. In the morning I
awoke unrefreshed, and looked at the day before
me as one of positive torture. Frequently I had
to remain in bed all day, and call in the assistance
of a neighbor, being really too weak to do any-
thing; I also had a terribly languid feeling.
Having inherited a good constitution, and being
always averse to calling in a medical man, I en-
deavored to battle through my troubles, trusting
that a certain amount of determination would
throw off this terrible nausea. Exhausted nature,
however, at last gave way, and I was on the eve
of sending for a doctor, when a friend drew my
attention to a remarkable cure of dyspepsia, and
nervous debility effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People. I got some of the Pills as
an experiment, and at once proceeded to take
them in accordance with the printed instructions.
After a few days, and before I had completed the
first box, I felt a visible improvement, my appetite
returned, and once more food agreed with me. |I
continued taking the pills, and am now happy to
relate that I seem as well and strong as ever, and
have suffered no more from that terrible indi-
gestion. I feel deeply grateful that chance threw
in my way Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, as I tell
everyone ; in fact, I should consider myself a
heartless woman if I should not make this thing
public, and endeavor to alleviate the troubles of
my suffering sisters.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are not
a patent medicine, but are a thoroughly scientific
preparation, the result of years of careful study on
the part of an eminent Edinburgh University
physician, and they were successfully used by him
in his every-day practice for years before being
offered for general sale. They positively cure
Rheumatism, Sciatica, Lumbago, and Neuralgia,
and are a specific for all diseases of women, such
as anaemia, poor and watery blood, female irregu-
larities, nervous headache and hysteria. They
are not a purgative medicine, but brace up and
permanently strengthen the whole system.
To obtain Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills apply to
your chemist, or send to the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Coy., Sydney, who will forward (post paid)
on receipt of stamps or post order, 1 box for 2s. 9d.,
or dozen for 14s 9d. #
