The emu and the grey and the red kangaroo
are in Victoria to have a close season extending
over the whole year.
Dear Bulletin, —Re Butler. In every
“Morning Liar ” and “Twilight Dreadful” I’ve
met this : “ Shortly afterwards several people
passed his (Butler’s) camp and were surprised to
notice that the tent was down and everything
gone.” This sort of thing makes me mad.
What, in the name of Bully Hayes, were these
people surprised about ? Tents are pitched nightly
and pulled down next morning by travellers as
regularly as sunrise and sunset near every Aus-
tralian bushtown. —6 x 8.
A Bulletin correspondent writes from
S.A. :
“ Politics tabooed ” was the injunction which the com*
mittee-men of Mount Barker (S.A.) Agricultural Society
issued with their invitations to luncheon on the occasion
of the recent Jubilee show of the society. And this is
how they carried out their injunction. They invited
every representative of the genus Fatman who could claim
the slightest connection with the district, and carefully
excluded Dr. Cockburn, who resides in the district,
represents the district in Parliament, is Minister of
Agriculture, and has done more for the producers than
any other S.A. Minister of the Crown. He is vice-patron
of the society, he subscribed to the show prize-fund, he
evinced his practical interest in the producers by exhibit-
ing at the show, and he was on the grounds and, what is
more, was the most popular man there. But against all
these was put in the balance the fact that he is a leader
in the Forward Democratic movement.
Writes the Rev. E. G. Moberly from
Walcha, N.S.W. :
Editor Bulletin,— ln a kindly paragraph which you
devote to me in “ Personal Items ” of March 6, I am sorry
(for my own sake) that there are two inaccuracies. In the
first place, I did not leave a Northern incumbency, to go
to Fiji. I had to resign it, more than two years ago,
partiv from being once more almost starved out, partly
from physical incapacity for the amount of travelling
necessary to do the duties efficiently. Being unable to
obtain any other clerical work, I was living as assistant
to a country photographer, when the agent for the Vicar
of Suva appointed me to leave Sydney in a week, to take
temporary charge of the chaplaincy. I gave up my billet,
came from Armidale to Sydney, to find that the agent,
having failed to inform his principal of my appointment,
the latter had already filled the vacancy. So I have not
gone to Fiji, but am out in the cold. Such are “ life’s
little ironies.”
Tasmanian Parliament opened on March 9
in the most hopeless confusion. First, there was
a lengthy deadlock over the election of a Speaker.
This trouble being surmounted, leave of absence
was granted to a member, Attorney-General
Clark, but, as a matter of fact, Clark is not yet
a member, not having been sworn in. Everything
might have passed off fairly well, but just here an
Oppositionist asked leave to put a motion without
notice. Then rose a Ministerial uproar that no
business could be done till the following day, in
accordance with the standing; orders, followed by
Opposition uproar that business had been done
re Clark. In consequence the House had to
deliberate for something like an hour as to how it
was going to put itself in order without making
Clark forfeit his seat, and whether it could be done
at all. At last it was decided that the House
should simply resolve that it was in order, and
that any rules which prevented it being in order
were hereby suspended to whatever extent might
be required. Then the Oppositionist asked, leave
to put his motion, and after much fuss a division
was taken and the permission was refused on
the ground that the House didn’t feel sure that it
might not be putting itself out of order again.
Then the brief session ended suddenly amid con-
tumely.

In Forty Seconds I
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hardman put down his “ Oleeve upon Contracts, ”
got out of his easy chair, and went lazily over to
the mantelpiece for his pipe. Next moment it
fell from his fingers, and—cherished meerschaum
though it was—lay broken and unheeded in the
fender, while Hardman, with the look of one who
has received a heavy blow on the head, stood star-
ing intently into the mantel-mirror until his little
Irish terrier, glad to see the book laid aside, came
over and reared up against his leg. Then, with a
sudden movement, the man turned his back upon
the glass and looked down with an air of relief at
the dog.
“ Why, Paddy,” he said, “ that was a curious
thing ! A very curious thing ! ”
He took out his handkerchief and wiped from
his forehead the perspiration of shock. The im-
pulse was strong upon him to look again into the
glass, but—self-dominated by nature and methodi-
cal by training—he resisted.
“ Not yet, Paddy ; at all events ” —still with his
eye on the dog—“ not till we have eliminated
some possible causes of this very extraordinary
phenomenon. First place, I don’t drink. Second
place, I am, to the best of my belief,, sound in
mind and body ; not likely, at 28, to be threatened
with insanity or apoplexy. Third place, I have
not, so far as I know, been overworking myself.
Pooh, Paddy ! it must be some freak of the optic
nerve,” and, wheeling round with a half-laugh,
Hardman looked again into the glass. The smile
died suddenly away from his mouth ; the sweat
sprang out again upon his forehead. “ Great
God ! ” he said, and took a couple of steps back-
wards. It was as before; the mirror reflected
neither himself nor the room he stood in ! A
bright little den, the latter ; with gilt-framed
pictures upon the delicately-tinted walls, and a
tall silver sculling-trophy shining to the brilliant
gas-light from above a host of glittering nick-nacks
upon the buffet. Singlesticks here ; boxing-gloves
there ; everywhere the insignia of lusty, unfan-
tastic young bachelorhood.
“This thing cannot be!” Hardman muttered,
as he gathered in all this at a glance. “ My sight
is disordered—that is all. I’ll see Couchwell to-
morrow ; he’ll soon put the matter right, I know !
I daresay such cases are common enough in his
experience.”
But not a word did he believe of his own reas-
surances—and he knew that he didn’t, as he turned
again and forced himself to gaze upon what the
glass presented.
A large, lofty room, dimly lighted by two candles
that stood in tall iron candlesticks upon a small
table rudely carved in some nearly-black wood.
Of dark wood also were the few chairs and the
ceiling, but the walls, unbroken by any windows,
were of naked stone.
This want of windows curiously exercised and
irritated Hardman’s mind. How did the room
get daylight ? he asked himself, and the matter
seized upon him—mastered him in an unaccount-
able way at which he wondered. He felt an irre-
sistible desire to put the question to an old gentle-
man seated at the little table, with his back
towards Hardman.
“ Pardon me, sir ” —the words would out —“ but
would you kindly explain to me how the daylight
is admitted to that curious apartment of yours ?
I see no windows.”
The old man deliberately got up and turned
round. The light was behind him, and all Hard-
man could make out was that the figure was tall
and straight, and had its leanness accentuated by
a long-skirted, tightly-fitting frock-coat. Keeping
% finger between the leaves of a large book, as if
to mark a place, the old gentleman made an
elaborate and antiquated bow.
“ I am sorry, sir, that I have not the pleasure
of your acquaintance ; but, if you will do me the
honor to step in, I shall be happy to explain to
you why no windows are required in any apart-
ment I may occupy.”
“ Step in ? ” echoed the astonished Hardman.
“ How can I do that ? ”
“Nothing simpler, my dear sir! Move aside
that clock ; bring a chair ; get upon the mantel-
piece, and, as I said before —just step in ! ”
Automatically the bewildered Hardman dis-
placed the mantel-clock and brought the chair.
“But how am I to get through the glass?” he
said to himself as he climbed up on the mantel,
and then, to his amazement, he found that
there was no glass ! The mirror-frame surrounded
merely an open space in the wall, through which
he had no difficulty in reaching the chair which
the old gentleman placed for him to descend upon.
“ This puts me in mind,” Hardman thought as
he climbed down, “ of something I’ve read some-
where.”
“Probably of ‘Through a Looking-Glass,’”
observed the old gentleman as categorically as if
Hardman had asked the question in so many
words. “ But that, my dear sir, is a mere child’s
story. This story will be a very grown-up one,
indeed.”
“ This story ! What story ? ”
“ Why, yours. But, pray be seated; and, to
begin with, let us settle the window-question.”
The light fell now on the old gentleman’s face,
and Hardman saw that it was dark, and lean, and
delicately-featured, with an inscrutable expression
and undecipherable eyes. The hair was grizzling
black ; the pointed beard was nearly white ; and
the man might have been any age between 55
and 70.
“ But, tell me, first,” the wondering Hardman
said, “ how you knew what I was thinking of just
now ? ”
“What you were thinking of?” The old
gentleman crossed his legs and elevated his pecu-
liarly sloped eyebrows. “Why, my dear sir, I
always know what everybody is thinking of ! It
has just now occurred to you, for instance, to
wonder if you will be able to get back to the —er
—actual world, as easily as you have just stepped
out of it.”
‘ ‘ The actual world ? ” Hardman repeated-
turned his head, and saw that there was now no
opening behind him—nothing but the roughly-
jointed stones of the prison-like wall. He got
hastily to his feet. “Why,” he said, “ you must
be the Dev ”
The old gentleman held up a deprecatory hand.
“Not that name!” he said. “Please, not that
name ! It is so offensive—and I am sure you have
no wish to be insulting.”
“ Well, Belial, then ! —or Beelzebub ! ” said
Hardman recklessly. “ Whatever you like.”
“ My dear sir ! —my dear sir ! ” —the old gentle-
man was profoundly shocked—“ I cannot tell you
how much it surprises me to find a gentleman of
your profession so preposterously ignorant of dis-
tinctions in—er—down there /”
And The Old Gentleman—he is entitled now to
the capital initials —got up and paced the room
disgustedly, while Hardman sat and swore inter-
nally that he never could have believed this thing
without actual demonstration. There was, after
all, then, a concrete, personal Devil!
“Once more, sir,” The Old Gentleman put in
severely, “let me beg of you to forego, even in
your own thoughts, the use of that objectionable
appellation.”
“Oh! —ah! —yes, certainly,” said Hardman,
taken aback. “But, now, what’s the next move ?
Y#u don’t want to make a bargain for my soul in
the good old regulation way, I suppose ? ”
“Oh, dear, no!” said The Old Gentleman,
softening a little. “ Quite unnecessary, quite —
with a member of your profession. Go on, my
dear sir ! —go on and prosper in the Law, and the
result is—er—you will pardon me, I’m sure—a
foregone conclusion. ”
“ The devil it is ! ” Hardman broke in angrily,
and in an instant the candles went out, leaving at
first a pitchy darkness, through which came The
Old Gentleman’s voice in accents of severe repre-
hension.
“You are abusing my hospitality, sir ! and, by
way of return, I will satisfy your impertinent
curiosity as to the absence of windows in this
room. The simple fact is that I can, if necessary,
provide from within me whatever light I happen
to require.”
“ From within you ? What, then, do you want
with candles ? Pouf ! —how hot it is growing ! ”
“ Because—you observe a faint glow in this
direction ; because—don’t you find it getting un-
comfortably warm ? ”
“ Warm ! it’s getting simply intolerable,” and
Hardman twisted about on his seat as he noticed
near him a faint, reddish light that grew every
instant brighter as the heat increased, till at last
was clearly visible the Old Gentleman’s figure,
glowing from head to foot like molten metal;
filling the room with an angry illumination and
with the heat almost of a smelting-furnace. The
fiery features wore a mocking smile, and Hardman
rolled upon the floor.
“ Ah ! Oh ! Ugh ! Stop it! Put yourself out!
Switch yourself off, before I’m completely
“There !” The Old Gentleman re-lighted the
candles at his nose, and his incandescence
diminished as rapidly as it had increased. “You
understand, now, what I want with candles ; or,
rather, what you want with them. If I were
obliged, you see, while I have the honor of your
company, to make continued use of my personal
effulgence, you would be —er —well—the frizzling,
so to speak, would be —er—premature. ”
“ Premature ! ” gasped Hardman, as he got up
from the floor and mopped his streaming forehead.
“ I don’t understand.”
“You soon will. How long, let me ask, have
you been in practice ? ”
“Two years or thereabouts.”
“ And your age is ? ”
“ Going on twenty-eight ? ”
“Just so. Well, we’ll subtract 28 from, let us
say, 55—for you will prosper and retire compara-
tively early. That gives you 27 solid years of
practice, yet. Why, my dear sir, it would be the
grossest mismanagement on my part to close up
suddenly such a long and promising perspective
of rascality !—especially since, as I have already
observed, the ultimate result admits of no doubt
whatever. It is, perhaps, a long time for which
to say au revoir, but ... it was as tough a
grinder to get out as ever I tackled. Only for the
gas, it would have given you pepper ! ”
» * * *
The dentist laughed as he stood over Hardman,
with his forceps grasping the molar that had for
days been excruciating that astonished person’s
jaw.
“ How long, in Heaven’s name, have I been
under the influence of the gas?” Hardman asked.
“An hour?”
The dentist smiled and looked at his watch.
“ Why do you ask ? ” he said.
“ Because I’ve been having at least an hour of
the most extraordinary experiences.”
“Yes, nitrous-oxide does affect some subjects in
that way. You’ve been insensible about forty
seconds ! ”
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
Because of the stringent Sunday-closing
provisions of last year’s S.A. Licensed Victuallers
Act, Bung joined forces with Money Bags to
knock the Labor men out at the Federation elec-
tions.
S.M. Herald makes the captain of the
Lika say in an interview that when the ship was
cannoning off one iceberg on to another, and the
thermometer said 6.7 deg. : “I kept as cool as
possible.” The same scribe learnedly discusses a
“ cross jack topsail ” and the “after quarter.*
Sailors will smile.
From Oondobolin :
As showing the state to which the present drought has
reduced local stock-owners, a selector out back, who has
cut down all the edible shrubs on his holding to keep his
sheep alive, has now started to educate his sheep up to
eating “ mallee,” of which pest he has a plentiful supply.
He cuts bunches of the latter and soaks them in a solution
of salt-and-water, and offers them in this tempting form
to his perishing stock. It may have an educative influ-
ence, but it cannot be far-reaching, as the bulk will perish
watching the few who eat.
In a Sydney shop-window there is a copy
of “ The Virginians ” containing the following
autograph letter :
Kensington, W. June 2, 1859.
Dear Ned,—l will be with you to-morrow night; you
may expect me. Here is a story I heard but yesterday.
Listen. An acquaintance of a certain Scotch bailie made
a grievous complaint to him one day of the hard times
and the impossibility of scraping together a livelihood in
that wretched country. The bailie’s own experience ran
directly counter to these croakings, for his industry had
realised a handsome competence ; but he knew too much
of the world to attempt proving to the complhiner that
his ill-success might be partly his own fault. He con-
tented himself with remarking that it was surely possible
fora tradesman to draw together a tolerable business.
“Not in this country,” his friend repeated. “ Weel,
then,” said the bailie, “ What say ye to emigra-
tion ? I have heard that some push their way
weel in Australia.” “ Yes,” replied his desponding towns-
man, “ that may be the case once in a day ; but if there is
business there, mair folk are there than can get a share
of it." “ Weel, it may be true, ye say,” replied the bailie,
“ but ye might gang farther—ye might gang up into the
interior.” “ There’s naebody there,” said the grumbler,
“ but kangaroos.” The worthy magistrate, concluding
that kangaroos were a tribe of native savages, among
whom a careful peddler might make indifferent good bar-
gains, replied, “ Weel, a-weel, and isna a kangaroo’s
siller as guid as anither man’s ?”(I!)—Ever thine, W. M.
Thackeray.

The Wool Fleet.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We have other tales for telling, we have other
songs to sing, * ,
Who have looped the planet’s waters in a plait of
tarry string;
With a tarry rope a-tether, with the sun, the
wind, and weather,
From the muddy banks o’ Yarra to the ice-bound
Arctic ring.
We have ladled up the oceans in the hollow of a
spoon;
We have hailed the iceberg sailing ’neath a grey
midwinter moon;
We have been to greet the devil when the water
lifted level
And the whirring line made answer to the whiz of
the harpoon.
You have seen the gas-lamps glisten on the water
where they lie,
With the southern stars far showing through their
rigging in the sky.
You have heard the clear bells clanging while the
rowdy winch is banging
To the squeaking screw’s caresses, to the sobbing
of the presses,
When its “Bully in Our Alley,” and “ We’ll Meet
You By-and-Bye.”
Have you heard the night wind talking to the
Wool Fleet ere the rose
From the blushing face of morning, like a dream
of lovers, goes ?
Have you fevered for the lotion of its ever-potent
potion
For the calm of open ocean and the freest breath
that blows ?
You must see them at the sun-up, with their
redd’ning sails aglow,
When the gangs begin to muster and the laden
lighters go,
When the night clouds wheel and scatter and their
crews commence to chatter
In the polygot palaver of a dozen tongues, or so.
They have rallied to the gorging. At the uproar of
the feast,
They are swooping South to swallow eighty thou-
sand bales at least,
With an appetite unsated, with a hunger unabated,
For a greedy London market and the markets
further East.
Oh, the ragged ewes are bleating on the downs
among their lambs,
Where a squatter man hath mated them with
Tassie’s choicest rams,
And our shearer men are riding, for there’s little
time for biding
When the noisy North starts knocking and the
silken East salaams.
So, its “ Haul upon the bowl’n ! ” and another
clipper in.
With a salt-dried score of & )ners who are wasting
for their sin,
While the crowd that gathers round her turn to
cheer the out ward-bounder
That with locks and fleeces freighted, with our
staple product weighted,*
Slips her slackened hawser gaily in the ramping,
rousing din.
Have you hearkened to the night-wind that hath
drifted over-sea,
Where the dead men lie a-rocking in their deep
graves restlessly?
In their weighted hammocks rotten, on the outer
ways forgotten,
On the unremembered inner ways these countless
dead men be.
And the night-wind tells his story of the ghostly
ships that sail
By their ghostly helmsmen guided till the dawn-
light cometh, pale ;
Of the sallow arms that beckon, of that drowned
Vanderdecken
Who for ancient sin unshriven, still, by storm and
thunder driven,
In the teeth of tempests horrid sets his course
against the gale!
But a sweeter tale for telling hath the night-wind
as he rides
Of the flaking foam fast-flying from a roving
trader’s sides,
Of the sunlit waters swelling where the sea-man
makes his dwelling
’Twixt the parting of the oceans and the meeting
of the tides.
There's a drowsy Dutchman’s over, who will sing
his “ Wacht am Rhein ”
When this Frenchman’s finished shouting for
“ revanche ” and further wine:
There’s a dainty English clipper, with a dainty,
dandy skipper
Who was educated early at Newcastle-on-the-
Tyne.
There’s a squat, big-bellied Belgian with a fore-
hold like a tank ;
There’s a Swedish barque ’longside him and the
other side a Yank ;
There’s an old New Bedford whaler rubbing noses
with a sailer
Of the latest modern fashion and the highest
modern rank.
There’s a jaunty White Star liner and her decks
are scrubbed and clean,
And her tall white spars are spotless, and her hull
is painted green.
Don’t you smell the smoky stingo ? Ech ! ye’ll
ken the Gaelic lingo
Of the porridge-eating person who was shipped in
Aberdeen.
There’s a whiff of foreign cooking and a stronger
stink of tar,
And the rattle of the chop-sticks and “ ’e dunno
where ’e are.”
Oh, it’s “ Crachious ! Vot’s der madder?” Oh,
it’s ankles down the ladder,
And a woman laughing softly where the cabin
door’s ajar.
There’s a pretty girl a-flirting with the second
engineer;
There’s a virgin shy declining skipper Yonson’s
pottled peer;
And you’ll find them gaily tripping in their gew-
gaws to the shipping,
“Jan, I lofe you.” “Oui, you lof me! No spik
Englees moch, my dear.”
So, you’ll grease the whole caboodle, and the pis-
ton-rods you’ll shine;
So, you’ll paint em aft and forrard, though
they’ll blister on the Line;
Oh, you’ll clean the whole caboodle to the tune of
“ Yankee Doodle,”
But you’ll sing another ditty at the Horn, oh,
skipper mine !
Ye have answered to the message that they flashed
along the ooze,
Now the ink it drieth quickly, and there’s
time to lose;
’Tis the Philistines’ to barter. Take your mani-
fest and charter,
Hence, ye trouser-hitching legion, we have busi-
ness with the Jews !
Let the Four Winds rise and whisper as ye carry
over sea—
Let them speak of drowned dead men, it hath
naught to do with ye.
Be it yours to freight our plunder o’er their grey
bones rocking under :
Be it yours to freight our plunder o’er the plunder
of the sea.
There’s another song for singing, there’s another
tale to tell,
When the rim of Heaven toucheth on the upward
rim of Hell:
When they’ve spliced the stars together with a
tarry rope a-tether—
When the dead men all foregather with the sun
and wind and weather,
Who have tied the seas together, who have tied
them very well.
B. J. BEADY.
Settler Nixon, on the Mossman (N.Q.),
had an experience the other day. After crossing
the river he found he had forgotten his pipe, and
returned per punt to secure it. On stepping out
of the punt and commencing to wade inshore a
large alligator seized him and dragged him under.
Nixon struggled furiously, and finally escaped
with two broken ribs, torn clothes, and a severe
mauling generally. A man who can wrestle
with and escape from an alligator must be pretty
tough.
S.A. Licensed Victuallers’ Association re-
cently gave a delirious whoop of joy because seven
out of the ten S.A. candidates elected to the
Federation Convention are friendly to the Drink
party. The way in which everyone has been satis-
fied is simply marvellous. The Bible-Readers
look upon the result of the election as a direct
message from God, the Register says it shows the
public abhorrence of the Kingston Ministry’s con-
duct in hospital matters (the three Ministers
headed the poll, by the way), and even the Wes-
leyans can see clearly in Premier Kingston’s elec-
tion a direct command from the Almighty in the
direction of Methodist union.
Duncan Gillies drove one more nail into his
political coffin at Ballarat the other day, when he
rose to explain how he wasn’t solely responsible
for his infamous, ludicrous, odorous, “octopus”
Railway Bill. Duncan —the One Wise Head of
the Conservative party in Victoria —“ took this
opportunity to point out that the Bill was first
introduced by Mr. Bent in the Leg. Assembly
when Sir Bryan O’Loghlen was Premier.” The
explanation set his hearers thinking of the Athan-
asian Creed and the Blessed Trinity. Therefore,
there was not one Octopus Biller, but three Octopus
Billers —Bent, the proposer; O’Loghlen, the
seconder; and Gillies, the patriot who tried to put
it through. The people of Victoria, perceiving
here a divided duty, have got their knife into the
three responsible persons for evermore. Amen.
Bill and Jim Nearly Get Taken
Down.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ You see, jt was this way,” said Bill reflectively,
as we sat on the rails of the horseyard, “me and
Jim was down at Buckatowndown show with that
jumpin’ pony Jim has, and in the high jump our
pony jumped seven foot, and they gave the prize
to Spondulix that only jumped six foot ten. You
know what these country shows are ; a man can’t
get no sort of fair play at all. We asked the
stooards why the prize was give to Spondulix, and
they said because he jumped better style than
the pony. So Jim he ups and whips the
saddle and bridle off the pony, and he says
to the cove at the jump, ‘ Put the bar
up to seven foot six,’ he says, and he
rides the pony at it without saddle or bridle,
and over he goes, never lays a toe on it, and Spon-
dulix was frighted to come at it. And we offered
to jump Spondulix for a hundred quid any time.
And I went to the stooards and I offered to back
the pony to run any horse on the ground two
miles over as many fences as they could put up in
the distance, and the bigger the better ; and Jim,
he offered to fight as many of the stooards as
could get into a room with him. And even then
they wouldn’t give us the prize—a man can’t ever
get fair play at a country show. But what I
wanted to tell you about was the way we almost
got took down afterwards. By gum, it was a near
thing !
“We went down from the show to the pub.,
and there was a lot o’ toffs at the pub. was bettin’
Jim a pound here and a pound there that he
wouldn’t ride the pony at this fence and at that
fence, and Jim picked up a few quid jumpin’ ’em
easy, for most of the fences weren’t no more than
six foot six high, and, of course, that was like
drinkin’ tea to the pony. And at last one cove he
points to a big palin’ fence, and he says : ‘ I’ll bet
you a fiver your horse won’t get over that one
safely.’ Well, of course, it was a fair-sized fence,
being seven feet solid palin’s, but we knew the
pony could do it all right, and Jim wheels round
to goat it. And just as he sails at it, I runs up to
the fence and pulls myself up with my hands and
looks over, and there was a great gully the other
side a hundred feet deep and all rocks and stones.
So I yelled out at Jim to stop, but it was too late,
for he had set the pony going, and once that pony
went at a fence you couldn’t stop him with a block
and tackle. And the pony rose over the fence,
and when Jim saw what was the other side,
what do you think he did! Why, he turned
the pony round in the air, and came baek
again to the same side he started from ! My
oath, it astonished those toffs. You see, they
thought they would take us down about getting
over safely, but they had to pay up because he
went over the fence and back again as safe as a
church. Did you say Jim must have been a good
rider —well, not too bad, but that was nothin’ to —
hello, here comes the boss; I must be off. So
long ! ”
